SOME RECENT BOOKS.
“The Jewel of Ynys Galon,” by Owen Rhos-
comyl (paper, 2s lOd; cloth, 4s), is a piratical
romance in the familiar “ Treasure Island ” vein.
It is an uncommonly good specimen of its class ;
the interest is strong, there is genuine “go” in
the fights, and the blood and brains are artistically
spattered.
“The Honour of Savclli,’ by S. Levett Yeats
(paper. 3s ; cloth, 4s), is a passable romance in the
style of Weyman, with more tedious detail and
less vigour and fascination. The author explains,
however, that when his book was written he had
not read Weyman—which shows that unconscious
imitation is less to be relied on than conscious.
“South Sea Bubbles,” that charming book of
island travel-sketches, “ by the Earl (Pembroke)
and the Doctor ” (George Kingsley), appears in a
cheap edition (cloth, 3s) while the news of the
titled co-author’s death is yet fresh. Its atmo-
sphere is anything but that of mourning. There
is no art in the descriptions—not even the art of
studied simplicity ; but the pictures of South Sea
life are fresh, vivid, and unaffected, and the book
has a breezy, open-air fascination worth any
number of charms stewed over midnight tapers.
Cole’s “ Chairman’s Guide ” (Is 3d) and “ Ball-
Room Guide” (Is) are * ell-written little manuals.
“ Euancond.it,” by Henry Goldsmith (Goo.
Robertson and Co.) is “an Australian romance”
built on lines w hich are understood to be much
favored in England, but which custom has stated
for the unromantie Australians themselves. The
story occupies itself with the typical remittance-
man who rises through hu-dcs to a fortune which
enables him to marry the typical Australian girl—
who allows the remittance-man to add<ess her by
her Christian name the second time she sees him.
Plot and treatment are essentially commonplace,
but the author manages to get interest enough to
satisfy a not too critical reader. Cloth, 4s.
To lovers of George Eliot, the new cheap
edition of her works can be recommended. Bind-
ing and type are good ; and the price (3s) is
moderate. “ Adam Bede ” is the first volume
(now ob ainable), and will be followed by others
in due course.
A selection of Heine’s “ Letters,” with some of
his best criticisms on art and music, appears in the
r>cott Library (cloth. Is 10d) uniform with the
same author’s “ Italian Travel Sketches.”
“ The Root of the Platter,” by H. H. Champion
(paper, Is 3d), is a scries of dialogues on social ques-
tions which really touch little more than the
fringe of the matter. ..The author defines nothing,
takes his premisses mostly for granted, and arrays
many interesting facts and arguments on unproven
postulates. It is true that what he - takes for
granted is generally a matter of common economic
knowledge ; and that if he had begun at the
beginning he would never have reached his end in
160 pages ; so complaint lies mainly against his
title. The pamphlet seems originally designed for
English readers, and the dialogue “business” is
irritating, but there are many true statements
and forcible phrases, and the whole is worth read-
ing. Mr. Champion’s Socialism is apparently
the variety known as State Socialism, and can
certainly not be denounced as either foolish or
impracticable.
“The Female Offender,” by Lombroso and
Ferrero (Cloth, 6s 9d), is published by Unwin
as the first of a much-needed “Criminology
Series.” The book (a translation, of course) is
companion to Lombroso’S study of “The (male)
Criminal” Englished in the Contemporary Science
series. The female offender is more monotonous
and less interesting than the male, but her peculi-
arities are none the less worth study. Conclu-
sions, in her case, however, are still uncertain;
the authors have had to make their brick with a
quite insufficient quantity of inductive straw.

Handy Language Books.
As a language is alive it is as .difficult
to bag as a cat: it slips through pen
and tongue as quicksilver slips through
your fingers. Yet, though no writer or
speaker can hope to be absolutely correct,
it is plain to everyone with eyes and ears
that some are more correct than qthers.
Within the last few years a tribe of little
handy-books has sprung up to point the
way to linguistic precision, and some of
them will repay purchase and study.
“How to Talk Correctly” (Bd.) quotes a
large number of common errors in speak-
ing and writing, and supplies corrections.
“Don’t,” a similar manual (Bd.), carries
censorship into the domain of manners,
and gives a large number of hints which it
is pleasant to approve without heeding.
“Just the Word Wanted” (Is. 2d.) is a
simple book of synonyms, easily come-at-
able.
“The Handy Guide to Correct Pronuncia-
tion ” (Is. 2d.) list ) words commonly mis-
pronounced, and supplies the proper
pronunciation.
“The Secretary’s Assistant ” (Is. 2d.) teaches
how to address letters correctly to C.M.G.’s
and others of touchy dignity and indignity.
“The Dictionary of Blunders” (Is. sets
one right upon common errors of epithet
and phrase.
“ Familiar English Quotations ” (Is 2d) is a
vest-pocket book useful as far as it goes,
but going hardly far enough. ,
“Everybody's Writing-Desk Book” (Is 3d)
is cordially recommended as the best of
the lot, covering nearly as much ground
as all the preceding together.
“Common Words Commonly Mispronouced”
(2s 4d) goes into the finer shades of pro-
nunciation, and appeals to professional
speakers rather than to the ordinary ama-
teur variety.

*** With the doubtful exception of “The
Odd Number,” the stories in this series
are rather recommended by style anc
sympathy than by plot or vivid'interest,
Davis and Harris have considerable Ameri-
can vogue, justified by their drollery and
perception ; but the average reader finds
them tending to dullness. ' Yerga, Suder-
mann, and Anatole France employ the
charm of simplicity, not the fascination of
strength and splendour. Bourget’s stories
are lilies, not roses of literature—unless it
be white moss-roses. De Maupassant’s
striking book can be recommended for
general purchase : the others require some
“ acquired taste,” some welcome of appre-
ciative culture. Paper, 3s. each.

o URSBLVES!
We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inc'.usive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask fqr everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the sani“ thing
occurs, and tlie same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big prodts they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contented ness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their service, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is w r orthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDE UN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they W'ork, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

“A GOLDEN SHANTY.”
ALL
AGENTS.
Bards of the Backblocks.
SPELL OH!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas in the Fraser country, where the coast is
wild and strange;
And “ swagging ” up the long divide that leads to
Daybreak Range
We came, and reached the saddle where the steep
rise starts to slow ;
Then dropped our loads and, resting, gazed upon
the gorge below.
And out beyond we looked towards the faintly-
gleaming sea,
Along the dim horizon-line where forests cease to
be;
And from the white-fringed coast there came the
soft, persistent sound
Of countless sighs that dying waves breathed o’er
their burial-ground.
And then, in strangest humor, borne on Fancy’s
whimsome feet,
Far, far away from Daybreak Range I paced the
crowded street;
The countless thousands passed along with loads
that bent the back—
They carried more than “eighty ” as they strug-
gled up the track !
Some were, alas ! so feeble that they fainted by
the way ;
And some rushed wildly through the throng with
gestures of dismay ;
And some there were of giant strenglh who seemed
to walk in sleep,
And slowly crept with crushing loads in trance
unconscious, deep.
And some I saw whose faces held no human
destiny—
Pale spectres of the men that once they fondly
hoped to be;
And some there were but young in years, yet from
whom Hope had fled, *
To leave them, careless of their chains, in all but
seeming dead !
Then suddenly a sound was heard that thrilled
that toiling band—
A voice that, clear as trumpet-call, went speeding
o’er the land.
And men whose ears were longtime dulhd by
Labor’s iron knell
Stopped still, with leaping hearts, to hear the cry
of “Spell oh ! Spell ! ” '’ ’ • .
Then loads were dropped, and weary forms sank
panting on the ground ;
And Care a moment ceased to brood as respite
sweet was found ;
Whilst haggard faces, in the flush of rest, shone
almost fair,
And silent tongues were loosed, and babbled music
on the air!
And then I woke, or seemed to wake, and on the
j column pressed,
Whilst at its head some unseen band played softly
hymns of rest;
And in their “ homes ” the women of that dumb
and awful crowd
With wasted fingers swiftly sewed another worker’s
shroud.
But oft at night, when heaven hangs close, there
echoes strangely clear
Some semblance of that clarion-call that speaks
the good time near
When voice of man, or voice of God, in accents all
may tell,
Shall wakgn every deadened soul with cry of
“ Spell oh ! Spell ! ”
Yalgoo, W. A.
W. E. CAREW.
I HAD NEVER STRUCK A SNAG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had pierced the “Never Never” with a “bluey”
slung behind,
And been caught in “ Darling showers ” that had
driven many blind;
I had “slung the billy” often and possessed a
“ tucker-bag,”
And the only thing that pained me was—l’d never
“struck a snag.”
I had travelled “on the wallalby ” “a paddin’ of
the hoof,”
And much preferred the “ firmament ” to any
other roof;
I had jogged along in company and chatted with
a “ lag,”
And I thought I was a bushman; but I’d never
“struck a snag.”
I had cracked the longest stockwhip that was ever
on a run—
Knew the men that strike the station at the setting
of the sun—
And had “broken” ev’ry “ brumby ” into quite
a decent nag ;
But I sighed and felt a longing—l had never
“ struck a snag.”
I had mined on Waukaringa ere the Doctor oame
to stay,
And was snug among the blankets when the
“ timbers gave away ; ”
I had “shore ” on many stations, and had eaten
of the “’slag;”
But so help me Larry Foley ! I had never
“struck a snag.”
Then one night I struck a shanty where the
crowd was on the wine,
And I sneered and told a fellow that he couldn’t
toe the line;
And it turned out he was shamming, he was what
they term a wag,
And he licked me post to finish—l had struck a
blooming snag.
’94.
R.F.D.
TO THE WEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Westward the summer whirlwinds go
Through sleepy inland towns,
Now roaring past, then turning slow
Upon the throbbing downs.
Tire camping sheep they scatter wide,
Crossing the dusty roads
The carrier’s children scare, who ride
Perched high upon the loads.
They lift the ashes of dead fires,
And take them where they go,
To watch them quicken vague desires
Dead hopes of long ago.
Of vagrant fancy they take hold,
And, like the columned smoke
That Israel followed far of old
From Egypt’s iron yoke,
They lead us over desert dry,
To where, with whirling hands,
They grasp and circle up on high
Coolgardie’sgolden sands.
R.A.F.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE' SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish th% agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THIiBBE CASTLES ” and “ C^k.^ST^lsr.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Villa’ “Three Castles’ ’ and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
k ‘ Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. ( Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney._ hi that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maoleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Slack and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, oj• any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to he re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of Tub Bulletin can he obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— li A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetoivn, South Africa.
Notice IjO Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
S. Australia, W. Australia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Governments on Interprovincial News-
papers, the annual subscription outside N.S. Wales is
now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JUNE 22, 1895.
Municipal Socialism.
For a number of years past the Democratic
party in Australia has been engaged in a
constant effort to get possession of the
legislatures, and, despite many fluctuations
of fortune, it has accomplished a good deal
in this direction. In Maoriland it has
gained entire control of the Assembly and
partial control of the Council, and has
established a Ministry which, no matter
what mistakes it may have committed, has
done more for the province than the best of
its predecessors. The Democrats of South
Australia have obtained practical command
of both Houses, and there also they have
established a Government which can claim
to have created a better record than any of
its short-lived ancestors, in that land where
Cabinets are born suddenly and die very
young. In the three Eastern provinces a
distinct change for the better is manifest.
The present Tory Ministry in Queensland
sits rather less securely than it did. The
New South Wales Labor-party has been, to
some extent, a failure, mainly by reason of
its aggressive name and its too exclusive
devotion to the interests of the horny-
handed element alone instead of those of
the nation at large ; yet, in an indirect
sense, it has been a great success. Its
own numbers have diminished instead of
increasing, but it has levered the other two
parties out of the bog of hopeless Toryism,
and has familiarised the Assembly with ideas
which were altogether strange to it only ten
years ago. And Victoria, where there is
practically no Labor-party in the Trades Hall
sense, and where The Bulletin sincerely
hopes there will be none in that sense, has
now got what is almost the most progressive
and Liberal Government in Australia. What
the Democratic party has accomplished may
seem very little, but if the Australasia of ’95
is compared with that of ’B5 then the amount
that has been done is remarkable. The only
thing that is still more remarkable is the
amount which remains to be done in the
future.
# # *
On one point, however, the Democratic
party has been strikingly remiss. It has put
forth all its efforts to capture the Legisla-
tures, and yet, in most cases, it has left the
municipal councils to be dominated as before
by the butcher-alderman, and the auctioneer,
and the other unprogressive elements of the
community. In isolated cases some good
has been done, but no combined or well-
organised effort has been made. There are
any number of visible reasons for this
neglect. As matters are at present, very
little glory attaches to the position of aider-
man. There are no rousing party fights, few
chances for flights of gaudy oratory, no
reports in Hansard, no Government to be
overturned, no portfolios to bo distributed,
no free passes, no admiring constituents —
nothing. The average alderman is a dull
and stupid person and an object of contempt.
He attends in a dreary fashion at a periodical
meeting, and muddles clumsily through cer-
tain solemn routine business concerning
drainage and paving, and his ideas of finance
generally consist in borrowing at an exor-
bitant rate of interest from somebody named
Cohen, and struggling ever afterwards to
raise enough money to pay the interest.
And when this device fails, his only remain-
ing idea is to get a helpless kind of over-
draft at 8 per cent. The typical alderman
is a person with large hands, and hair that
stands up on end, and a feebly unconvincing
aspect generally. And, as a consequence of
all this, the typical municipality is a neg-
lected, unkempt, and badly-paved place with
as much mortgage as it can stagger under —a
place which is badly lighted by contract with
a gas company, badly drained on no parti-
cular principle, scavenged by an unsavoury
cart which goes around like a thief in the
night, built up on no definite plan, and alto-
gether unprogressive.
# • «
Two weeks ago The Bulletin published,
under the heading of “A Model Munici-
pality,” an account of what has been done in
Glasgow by a vigorous and enlightened local
authority. In that city, which is now re-
garded as the best-governed in the United
Kingdom, and one of the best-governed in
the world, the city father has ceased to
be an object of scorn and a synonym for
stupidity, The Corporation of Glasgow
owns and personally manages the tramways,
water-supply, gas and electric-lighting works,
parks, recreation - grounds, public wash-
houses, gymnasia, swimming and other baths,
sewage farms, dessicating works, model-
lodging houses, model tenements for work-
men’s families, fire-brigades, and many other
works. It is constantly employed in de-
molishing unsavory slums and replacing
them with fine buildings and wide streets.
It is one of the largest, most considerate,
and least exorbitant landlords in the city.
It has enormously reduced the prices of gas
and water since it took them under its man-
agement, and now supplies them at a lower
rate than any other corporation or private
co. in Great Britain. Since it took over the
tramways it has reduced the fares, raised
the wages of the employes, and shortened
their hours. And with all this it not only
makes a steady and continuous reduction in
rates, and meets the interest on its liabilities
punctually, but is gradually paying off the
principal of its debts by means of sinking
funds. This is the sort of example which
the cities and towns of Australia should
follow, and they will begin to follow it when
an intelligent Democratic party—if, as The
Bulletin sincerely trusts, there is such a
thing—captures the municipalities. It is as
essential to do this as it is to capture the
Legislatures, and the fact that the old-
fashioned, slow-going variety of alderman
with the cart-horse soul has been generally
left in possession is the sole reason for the
general want of municipal enterprise in Aus-
tralia.
• • •
The experience of Great Britain is an evi-
dence that State Socialism cannot be brought
about by the Legislature alone. That
country was, a few years ago, pre-eminently
the homo of “private enterprise.” It is
still the home of private enterprise, but to a
greatly diminished extent. The Britons
had—and some Britons still have—a fixed
idea that the management of any business
undertaking by the people’s representatives
is an expensive calamity. In these days
any proposal that the Legislature should en-
courage the municipalisation of such exten-
sive works as those now carried out by the
corporations of Glasgow and many other
cities, would have been met by the unani-
mous reply that the scheme was visionary,
hopeless and ridiculous. If the necessary
authority had been granted it would have
led to little or nothing, for the old, stolid,
slow-going variety of municipal potentate
was as deeply convinced of the advantages
of private enterprise as the rest of the popu-
lation. Parliamentary eloquence alone
would have done little or nothing to alter
this state of things, even if there had been
any party which desired to be eloquent on
the subject, for Parliamentary eloquence,
on such a matter, and at that time, was mere
theory. But one democratic corporation
upset the old-established convictions of the
nation by simply doing that which the great
majority regarded as impossible. It proved
that municipal socialism, under intelligent
control, was cheaper and more effective than
private ownership, and that in a vast num-
ber of industries private enterprise was, by
comparison, antiquated, unsatisfactory and
unprogreasive. That one example did more
to upset Monopoly than could have been
effected by volumes of eloquent denuncia-
tion and innumerable political platforms.
Other cities, almost of necessity, adopted
the same system in steadily increasing num-
bers, and, as a consequence, it seems likely
that, in a few years, Britain will leave Aus-
tralia far behind in socialistic progress.
* * •
It is necessary that State and municipal
socialism should go hand in hand. There is
an increasing tendency, of late years, to
delegate more and more of the duties of
government to local bodies, for as popula-
tion increases, and the State machine grows
more complex, the burden of details becomes
more than the central authority can stagger
under. The central authority makes laws,
but their administration is left, to a
very large extent, to local institutions.
Therefore, when the Democratic party
exerts itself to control the authority
which makes the laws, and leaves almost
anybody who likes to run that one which
so largely administers them, it makes an
obvious mistake. A democratic majority in
the Legislature may authorise the local
authorities to supply and control water, gas
and electric-light supply, tramways, hospitals
and the like, but unless it takes hold of the
other end of the machine also it can’t make
them do it, or, at all events, it can’t make
them do it except in a way which will bring
the principle of national enterprise into
contempt. It can pass elaborate laws
whereby local bodies are authorised to raise
money for sewerage and road-making pur-
poses, to resume slum properties and replace
them with well-built and wholesome struc-
tures, to make parks and recreation-grounds,
to beautify the streets with trees, to regulate
building operations so as to obtain some
semblance of architectural seemliness, and
to do innumerable other things —in fact,
there is nothing that an intelligent Legisla-
ture cannot authorise the local rulers to do.
But unless intelligence and a progressive
spirit are instilled into the corporations thus
authorised, the money will still be raised in
a ruinous fashion, the roads and sewers will
be neglected, the attempts at sanitation,
architectural adornment and artistic comeli-
ness will be done, if they are done at all,
strictly according to the local butcher’s
ideas of art, and the result will be general
failure. Progress is made up of an in-
finity of details, and as it is necessary to
leave much of the detail of government to
local authority it is essential that the pro-
gressive party should control the local
authority, Otherwise the result will be
much the same as if the Legislature author-
ised an ox to be a racehorse, and left him at
that.
In one sense democratic control of
municipal affairs yields more immediate
returns than democratic control of the
Legislature. The progressive party desires
to promote public enterprise as distin-
guished from private monopoly. But its
efforts are woefully hampered by the system
of Government by Contradiction ; hythe cum-
bersome and elaborate forms of Parliament ;
by motions of censure and adjournment ; by
the opposition of the Upper House ; and
many other difficulties. Local bodies have
none of these obstacles to contend with.
They have already extensive powers, and
these, if intelligently used, would serve to
demonstrate the practicability of municipal
socialism, which is simply State socialism in
a subdivided form ; and by such demon-
stration they would make converts and
vastly strengthen their party. As matters
now stand, the local bodies have the power
of doing much good in their own limited
sphere, while the Legislature, reduced to
comparative impotence by its own forms and
ceremonies, has the power to talk of doing
much good to the country at large, and
seldom gets very far beyond that, except by
a superlative and unwonted effort. And the
party of progress almost utterly neglects the
smaller and more workable machine in its
wholly commendable efforts to get hold of
the one which is unwieldy, rusted, and
almost unworkable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Democratic Isaacs.
The legal mind is astonishingly narrow and
restricted—a circumstance which is probably
traceable to the law rather than to the mind,
The raw material of which lawyers are con-
structed is probably as Democratic as the
average, but the finished lawyer hardly ever
remains Democratic. Law is mainly precedent,
and precedent involves a constant struggle to
do exactly as our dull forefathers did, and this
perpetual looking backwards is fata! to
originality. Isaac A. Isaacs, the present Vic-
torian Attorney-General, bears the reputation
of being unusually liberal and progressive for a
member of his hide-bound trade, and the other
day the Vic. Parliament got the exact measure
of his progressiveness. One of the numerous
Smiths who infest the Legislature asked if the
Government would appoint a Crown Defender,
with a staff of assistants similar to that
of i the Crown Prosecutor, to look after
the interests of penniless prisoners. The
proposal looks simple and equitable enough, and
almost every intelligent layman now holds that
a Public Defender is one of the most urgent re-
quirements of the day. But Isaac A. Isaacs,
Attorney-General, was flabbergasted at the
mere mention of such a thing, and professed
himself utterly unable to take the proposal
seriously. Also, he failed to understand how
anybody else could take it seriously. It would
mean, said Isaacs, with unspeakable scorn,
that “ society, which has an interest in getting
rogues into gaol, would actually be paying to
keep them out.” All the lawyers applauded,
The House applauded. The Argus applauded.
And Isaacs evidently thought—and, perhaps,
still thinks—that he had said something pro-
found and logical. Of course, if every man
who was put in the dock was a rogue —if the
law never made a mistake, and put an honest
man behind the spikes, and left the real crimi-
nal outside —then the law would he paying to
keep rogues out of gaol. When Isaacs stated
the case that way, he distinctly implied that
the man arrested is always—invariably —with-
out any possible exception whatever, a rogue ;
consequently, according to Isaacs’ logic, the
money spent on paying a Crown Prosecutor,
Judge, jury, &c., to prove that he is a rogue, is
pure waste. He is a rogue simply because an
information was sworn against him, and that
ends the matter, and his guilt might as well he
taken for granted. The fact that the law
sometimes arrests the wrong man—that society
doesn’t want to gaol the wrong man, because
sending a man to gaol for a crime he didn’t
commit generally means leaving the ruffian who
did commit it at large—that it can’t be reason-
ably sure of getting the rogue it really
wants unless both sides of the case are fairly
presented at the trial—that both sides can’t be
fairly presented unless the defence is as well
conducted as the prosecution—and that that is
wholly impossible when a penniless prisoner is
fighting against the Treasury and the best legal
talent in the country—not one of these things
has ever yet occurred to Isaacs. To him the
law is apparently a machine for getting into
gaol, by fair means or foul, every man whom
any dunderheaded constable may happen to
arrest. And yet IsaaG A. Isaacs is reputed to
be an enlightened and Democratic member of
his profession. If he is, then what, in the
name of the great primitive Ape, can the others
be like ?
The N.S.W. “Tote” Bill.
The New South Wales Totalisator Bill of
Nicholas Willis, M.L.A., provides :
That yearly licenses shall he granted for the use of the
instrument by the Colonial Secretary to any racing club
giving in stakes at least £6OO at every day's racing held
by it, at which the totalisator shall be used. Every such
club, if its race meetings are held within 20 miles of
Sydney, must have at least 100 members. For clubs
beyond that radius they must have at least 50 members,
exclusive of those who may be members of any other
racing club, and their daily prize-list must be of the
value of not less than £3OO. Every member must be a
subscriber of a guinea or upwards a year, and the license
fee for a totalisator shall be £25, in addition to £lO per
day for et ery day on which it is used. The clubs are to
be allowed to retain only 10 per cent, of the takings as
commission, and this money must be expended in stakes
for the legitimate encouragement of racing. A fourth of
the net profits are to be paid to a fund known as the sup-
port of charities fund, which shall be disposed of annually
by the Colonial Secretary. Each club shall only be
allowed the use of the instrument on eight days in each
year, and it must not be used on any one racecourse for
more than 20 days in a year.
All these elaborate restrictions are evidently
intended as concessions to the snufflebusting
element of the community, which won’t grab
the unclean thing with both hands, but may
possibly he willing to touch it, in a shocked
sort of fashion, with the point of one finger.
But there is altogether too much restriction in
the matter. The object of the legalised machine is
to suppress the bookmaker and the surreptitious
machine with the “faked” returns; and the
more difficulties placed in the way of its
use, the more scope is left for the “ bookie” to
avoid being suppressed and for the surreptitious
implement to survive. If it is advisable that
the honest “ tote ” should take the place of the
raucous and dishonest live-meat metallician, then
it is advisable that it should do so on every day
and at every place where racing is conducted, in-
stead of merely eight times a year for each club
and 20 days in the year on each racecourse. If
this restriction is intended to imply that no
duh should race more than eight days per
annum, and that no racecourse should be used
more than 20 days in the year, then the Bill
ought to go at the matter straight instead of ap-
proaching it sideways—in other words, it should
enact that no club shall race more than eight
days a year, instead of enacting that it may
only hold eight days’ honest sport and as many
more days of the dishonest variety as it pleases.
The assumption that the tote is so much better
than the bookmaker that the Legislature should
lend it special encouragement, and yet so much
worse that it should be hampered by all manner
of vexatious restrictions from which the garish
“penciller” is free, is wholly illogical.
Therefore, The Bulletin moves that the
conditions under which the instrument is-
used should be reduced to two: (1) The
licence fee to be paid to Government
shall be £25, and £lO for each day the instru-
ment is used; (2) the racing club shall de-
duct 10 per cent, from the takings, and shall
expend three-fourths of this amount in stakes
and pay one-fourth to a charities fund. These
conditions are sufficiently stringent to shut out-
all the small, wild-cat clubs* and to prevent the
instrument being used for more days in the
year than is good for its health, or being em-
ployed by private racecourse owners for their
own benefit. The totalisator is a necessary
evil, like alcohol, and should be dealt with
accordingly. The State recognises that people
will drink, so it allows them to do so, but put*
on the brake by raising the price of liquor
through Customs and Excise duties. No one
ever struck the idea of further enacting that
drink should not be sold more than 20 days in the
year in one place, or that no club of less than
100 members should be allowed to drink at all,
and that even it should drink only eight times
a year.
Prior Vaughan’s Mission.
There is no insolence like the insolence of
ecclesiastics. A demagogue may be silenced
by a noisier demagogue ; there are joints in the
armor of the most impudent quack ; the show-
man’s brazen face is only a mask. But the
priests of all religions achieve impossibilities
denied to the gods from whom they claim to
draw their authority ; out-Herod Herod, and
out-face Omnipotence itself. Logic is ridiculed,
facts are defied, by the servants of superstition.
They believe, not because of evidence, but in
spite of evidence. Doubtless, from a certain
class of minds, Prior Vaughan’s assertions will
find credence, and his mission will receive sup-
port. It is for that other class which is for-


tunately increasing in Australia the class
which will not believe that day is night
because a black-coat says so—that this paper
briefly points out Prior Vaughan’s title to
be deemed a reed shaken by the wind of error.
Prior Vaughan is a modern begging-friar come
to empty Australian purses. He wishes to
bring ‘ ‘ religion ” to the English poor. He
implies that the Gregorian order of priests, for
which he collects, teaches unsectarian Christi-
anity, though a purely R.O. brotherhood. It
may, or it may not. The Bulletin does not
know. But consider the history of the Church
of Rome, “ compassing land and sea to make
one proselyte consider that it is this church’s
boast that its creed is unchangeable ; consider
that at the present moment the head of this
church is leading a crusade to proselytise Pro-
testant England ; and say whether it is likely
that the Gregorian mission is unsectarian. Let
Apella the Jew believe it : Australians will
not. Can Prior Vaughan specifically say
that it is possible for Catholic priests to
teach religion without teaching the Catholic
creed, and can he specifically say that the
Gregorian missionaries have not taught, do not
teach, and will not teach the Catholic creed ?
Further, supposing the Gregorian mission un-
sectarian, it is no part of Australia’s duty to
take “religion” to England at present. Let
her first set her own house in order. There is
more religious work to be done among the slums
of Australian cities and in the solitudes of the
Australian bush than this generation will ever
accomplish. Australian missions languish for
lack of funds : better sweep our own doorstep
before taking a hypocritical broom to England.
Again, England wants not more religion, but
less. Prior Vaughan comments on the
modern spread of irreligion in England,
and descants upon its awful results of
poverty and crime. Yet, partly because
the decay of religion is accompanied by the
growth of knowledge, the standard of national
life in Great Britain is steadily rising. During
the last 22 years criminal convictions in the
United Kingdom have declined 37 per cent.
(Mulhall’s Statistics, ’92). The annual death-
rate per 1000 in England and Wales (same
authority) shows a decrease from 22 - 4 in 1841-50
to 19’3 in 1881-85. As to poverty, since 1849,
“ with a constantly increasing population there
is an absolute decline in the amount of pauper-
ism ” (Gifeen’s “Progress of the Working
Classes in the Last Half-Century ”). The same
authority shows that working-class wages have
increased relatively as wellas absolutely, and that
working-class savings have largely increased.
These conclusions are solid and undeniable ;
and are accepted as final by everyone competent
to weigh the value of evidence. London
Morning Post, of April 9 last, says that during
the Commons’ debate on the Local Option Bill,
Sir Edward Clarke “showed how 23 years
ago Sir William Harcourt had himself con-
clusively demonstrated from the statistics of
the Home Office and the Local Govt. Board
that crime and disease and poverty were dimi-
nishing, and bringing those statistics up to date,
he further proved that this desirable social
progress has been continuous and permanent.”
Since, then, there has been an improvement in
the chief conditions of British national existence
almost contemporaneous with the decay of
religion lamented by Prior Vaughan, it is clear
that if Australia sends missionaries to England
at all, she should rather send them to spread
irreligion than religion, since the spread of
irreligion means the spread of knowledge, and
the spread of knowledge means the improve-
ment of morals, the lengthening of life and the
filling of pockets.
Law versus Justice.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir, re your remarks about
Vic. Full Court capsizing the verdict for £6OO
damages given by the jury in the Kilpatrick
v. Huddart, Parker & Co. case. Many
reasons were given by their Honors for grant-
ing a new trial, but one of the principal ones
was apparently based on the fact that the cer-
tificates given by Melb. Marine Board gave
carte blanche to the Alert’s owners. Now,
the fact should be mentioned that two
members of the Marine Board are also
members of the firm of Huddart, Parker &
Co. In my opinion, it would be a
wiser plan to have Marine Boards composed
of men who are not shipowners. We all know
that in this world it is not enough to do right.
We must go further and make things look right.
During the arguments in the Alert case—
which I, being a seaman of experience,
daily listened to with interest —Chief Justice
Madden not only attached great weight to the
certificates, but he also argued inferentially
that if there had been anything materially
wrong with the Alert, either in shape, con-
struction, or rig, someone in, or connected with,
the vessel would have drawn attention to the de-
fect. To quote his own words : “ I think the man
would be a wicked one who, knowing the un-
seaworthy condition of a vessel, did not report
it to the proper authorities.” A healthy senti-
ment, but much depends upon circumstances.
There are positions wherein men can report
defects in anything, and get praise—as they
should—for doing so, and there are also posi-
tions wherein men, on shore, if they drew atten-
tion to defects, would be instantly “sacked”
from their employment, and, if on board ship,
would, in all probability, be sent to gaol as
wicked, designing men ! For instance : Some
three weeks after the jury brought in a
verdict in favor of Mrs. Kilpatrick, I, at
his own request, interviewed a seaman on board
of a steamer then lying in the Yarra. He made
a statement which I took down in writing and,
after reading same over to him, he, in my
presence, declared it was true in every particu-
lar. Here it is :
My name is . lam an able seaman. I came
out to Melb. as such in the barque Alert. She was a long,
narrow and shallow vessel built for a river steamer, but
we brought her out under sail only. Her machinery was
in position but her funnels were not, they were stowed
below. In the first instance she sailed for Melb. from
Greenock, and after being a week at sea the crew refn ■ <1
to go any further in her. They went aft in a body ml
desired the captain to put the ship into the ne i -,i.
port. Accordingly, the Alert was taken into the thnl-
well Roads, Cardigan Bay, Wales, where the crew
were taken ashore, tried for refusing duty and sent
to gaol for six weeks. At the earnest request of the men
a surveyor was sent to examine the ship. He stated that
the men had a just cause of complaint, and pronounced
the Alert unseaworthy. The magistrates on learning this
at once made an order-releasing the men from confine-
ment. A new crew was shipped, of whom I was one, but,
owing to the name the vessel had got and the extra risk
to run, we demanded and got £3 15s. per month, the t hen
highest wages out of the port. During a period of three
weeks we made 15 attempts to get the Alert out of the
Bay, and 15 times she had to go back to her anchorage.
The reason we could not get outside was because the
wind was not fair and the ship would not stay. There
was not room enough to wear her round, so there was
nothing for it but go back each time. At last a slant
of wind was got and we sailed for Melbourne. Prior
to leaving Stidwell Hoads, the authorities on shore
told us that, although we had signed articles, we
need hot go to sea in the ship unless we liked, and
Capt. Webb, to encourage us, said he was going out him-
self as a passenger to Melb. At the last moment he
changed his mind and did not come with us. The Alert
carried eight hands, all told. She was very crank, or
tender, and every night at sundown, fine or foul, the top
gallant sails were taken in and stowed. At no time
during the passage out would the vessel stay. When we
wanted to put her on the other tack we had always to
wear her round. She was loaded with ballast and trimmed
on a fairly even keel. The decks were never dry except
during calms. Frequently a lot of water got into the
saloon and was got out by all hands baling with buckets.
In one breeze we had she shipped d sea over the quarter
which smashed in the skylight and carried away the
wheel and binnacle. We were about five months coming
out, and ran short of provisions on the way. Ido not
remember a pantry window in front of the poop. If there
was one it must have been covered over, for I never saw
it, or heard of it at any time. We never expected to
reach Melb. in her. She was a dangerous vessel and not
fit, in a breeze with a seaway, to either stay, wear or run.
I would not ship in her again under any circumstances.
When the vessel lam now in was in Q’sland, I read there
some account of the trial, and was sorry that, I was not in
Melb. to give evidence as to what I knew of the Alert.
Such is said to have been the ship which
held certificates that she was “ fit to carry dry
cargo to any part of the world.” It certificates
are incontestable proof of a ship’s seaworthiness,
how did Mr. Plimsoll manage to stop so many
certificated ships from going to sea ? The wreck
of such a vessel as the Alfcrt shows that
there is need enough of an Australian Plimsoll,
and the result of the Full Court’s decision shows
that Macklin was not far wrong when he said:
“The law is a sort of hocus-pocus science that
smiles in yer face while it picks yer pocket,
and the glorious uncertainty of it is mair use to
the professors than the justice of it.”— Tom
Cringle, Melbourne. June 1, 1895.
Beauty before Duty.
The Turner Government’s proposal to save
some £19,000 a year in the working of the Vic.
Education Department has been defeated by a
large majority, therefore 190 superfluous civil
servants who thought they were pensioned off
are again doing nothing in particular at the
same salaries as before. Conscientious objec-
tion to pensions is the foremost reason advanced
for annulling this particular retrenchment
scheme, although the discomfiture of Turner
& Co. was seemingly due to the influence of 28
married lady teachers, who, having been re-
tired last year from their billets without
pensions, but with compensation, are ob-
jects of sympathy to Hancock and other
soulful members of the Victorian Assembly.
Twenty-eight members only had previously
voted for granting pensions to the 28 matrons
aforesaid one member for each matron,
as was wickedly remarked and 20 of
these susceptible creatures were included in the
majority (51 to 35) which refused to permit the
Government to get rid of 190 other civil servants
by pensioning them. Premier Turner made
out a good case for the Government. He told
his turbulent party that “The great object of
the Bill was to enable the saving to take place
at once, and it would increase every year,
because the persons retiring would get incre-
ments if they remained in the service. There
would, of course, be a larger saving if all these
servants were dispensed with at once, than by
retiring them on pensions ; but the Govern-
ment was not justified in dismissing a large
number to swell the ranks of the unemployed,
though it was justified in allowing servants who
could be dispensed with to retire voluntarily,
and give them the pensions they had earned
for the time they had served. That was the dif-
ference between the policy of the present and
the late Government.” It was not proposed
to give full superannuation pensions, but
merely as much retiring allowance as each
official had earned up to date. The House
had to choose between two unavoidable evils
bred by the corrupt extravagance of the
Gillies regime : the larger evil of retain-
ing unnecessary civil servants who have a
legitimate claim to fair treatment, and the
smaller evil of getting rid of them on the
cheapest terms consistent with justice. Justice,
it is true, seldom rules in private commercial
business, where servants are “sacked” with
cold-blooded contempt for any claims they may
have established to their employers’ considera-
tion. Yet, unless the Vic. Assembly was pre-
pared (which it wasn’t) to demand the uncon-
ditional dismissal of these 190 persons, it had
to accept one or other of the expensive alterna-
tives aforesaid. A mixed majority, including
all the Opposition and many professed Radicals,
chose the alternative which costs an extra
£19,000 a year. A glance at the division-lists
reveals the true cause of this Government
defeat. The 28 married lady-teachers were
responsible for it. Hancock and Co. practically
stipulated for the pensioning of these lovely
lobbyists as the condition on which they would
support pensions in general, and, when Turner
steeled his heart against the two dozen and
four matrons, Hancock and Co. decided that
the country had got to “ jolly well suffer.”
“ Made in Victoria.”
F. : The Bulletin says that the Vic. Tariff
Board’s proposal that local manufactures should
be branded “ Made in Victoria ”is “ like poll-
taxing the white man to discourage the China-
man.” If the local manufacturers were any-
thing more than plagiarists and imitators they
might deserve generous treatment. But it is
because they have been disguising their pro-
ducts as imports that the results of Protection
are not overwhelming proof of the advantages
of the system. Hundreds of people who will-
ingly wear imported boots made in Colling-
wood, are ready to swear that “ colonial
boots ” are rubbish. Not one hat-factory
has the pluck to put its own brand
on its goods. Quite lately a woollen-mill
has taken to advertising its rugs, but the cir-
cumstance is unique in that trade. Cigars and
cigarettes are put on the market under a foreign-
sounding name, and packed in boxes that are a
colorable imitation of foreign packages. One
big firm supplies a cigar that is a, fac-simile of a
certain well-advertised foreign cigar this
“colonial” cigar is bought by publicans and
tobacconists and put in genuine boxes and sold
as an imported cigar. Further, it is within 1
per cent, of the quality it pretends to be, and
is quite good enough to sell on its merits, be-
cause it is 10s. per 100 cheaper. Vic. twine,
Vic. hats, Vic. boots, the best Vic. tweed are
good enough for anywhere, but the pestilent
underhand methods of the protected manufac-
turer go a long way to impede success. It is
asserted that the recent European treaty re-
garding the branding of goods with the country
of their origin “made in Germany,” &c., has
served as an advt. and impetus to trade for the
countries concerned. Here is a slab from an
English Parliamentary report concerning India
since this treaty :
An examination of the returns tends to support the con-
clusion that in respect to British manufactures, Con-
tinental competition is gradually making its way, and in
respect to Continental goods, of which the United King-
dom was the dep6t for shipment, the trade with India is
being conducted direct in an increasing degree.
Some of this British unsupremacy is un-
doubtedly due to the eternal verities of Free
Raid, but it is also maintained that the selling
of a good article on its own merits has ad-
vanced the success of Belgium and Germany in
India, and elsewhere. Similarly in Vic. the
local manufacturers would be advantaged by a
little compulsory honesty.
A Hazy Divine.
Rev. Dr. Torrance, discoursing on “Political
corruption ” from his Melb. pulpit, shows how
dangerous it is for a parson to stray from the
beaten track in response to the alleged yearning
of the people for enlightenment on domestic
and political affairs from the “ cludgy.” “ Poli-
tical corruption ” is a comprehensive theme in
the City of Dreadful Knights; but the reverend
handled it with pitiful weakness and a palpable
anxiety to commend his spineless discourse to a
bedizened audience, the main portion of which
was red-jowled and paunchy. Dr. Torrance
is evidently desirous of being considered
a devil of a fellow, and, in his fiercer moments,
is suggestive of the Rev. Mr. Spalding when
fully roused, and threatening to give somebody
“ a real hard knock.” Talking big-and-large,
he hurled at Parliament most of the defamatory
adjectives that have been thrown in the teeth
of the Church for ages, but his only attempt to
particularise the corruptionists, log-rollers,
place-hunters, and so on, was when he re-
marked, grandly : “ We want men of character
in the House, and men of character to return
them. We do not want men who are mere
talkers or blatant democrats, but men of true
religious instincts.” Then he instanced Higin-
botham as the kind of politician he did want,
which was somewhat paradoxical on the part of
the wandering pulpiteer, as Higinbotham per-
sonified most of the principles Dr. Torrance
most vigorously abused—he was an “irreligious”
person, a manhood-suffragist, favored payment
of members, and backed-up the labor move-
ment, all of which things are anathema to the
right-thinking person who goes to church morn-
ing and evening, and believes in the Divine
right of the upper-classes. But The Bulletin
admires the stupendous presumption of the
clerical Tory who—standing amidst the ruins of
scores of pious reputations, in the thick of a
tremendous social smash brought about by the
chicanery and chartered brigandage of a set of
men who were elevated to power because of
their duly advertised “religious instincts,” and
who hated manhood suffrage and all democracy—
implicates only the Labor-party, the one set of
men who have come through the days of
abomination with clean hands and unsmirched
character, in his denunciation of the corrup-
tionist, the sycophant, and the self-seeker. It is
best, after all, for the average parson to “ lay
to his Book ” —the masses don’t pay him to
teach them, and the classes won’t. Even the
latter appreciate him better as a soporific than
an eye-opener.
Thing's as They Are.
A slab from the Rev. 0. W. Stubbs’ “ Christ
and Economics” —a contrast of teaching—is
going round the newspapers :
CHRIST AND ECONOMICS.
From “ Christ and Economics,” by the Very Rev. C.
W. Stubbs, Dean of Ely.
Christ Says:— Conventional Political
Economy Says
Love your enemies. Undersell your friends.
Resist not evil. Strike, lest ye be struck or
locked out.
Thou shalt love thy neigh- Every man for himself, and
hour as thyself. the devil take the hind-
most.
Blessed are the peacemakers. Laissez-faire, Laissbz-
Passbr.
Blessed are the meek, for Property in land is for the
they shall inherit the most part the result of
earth. State grants for mili-
tary service.
Give to him that asketh Indiscriminate charity is a
thee. frightful evil.
Lend, hoping for nothing Interest is the legitimate re-
again. muneration of capital,
its rate varies with the
value of the security.
Forgive men their debts. Put the bailiffs in the house.
Lay not up treasure on earth. Capital is the result of sav-
ing, and is a prime re-
quisite of production.
Be merciful and give good It is impossible to interfere
measure, pressed down with the law of supply
and running over. and demand.
I will give unto this last, I will give unto this last the
even as unto thee. very least wage that
the haggle of themarket
makes possible.
Ye cannot serve God and Buy in the cheapest market
Mammon. and sell in the dearest,
and then you may drive
to church with an easy
conscience and a car-
riage and pair.
This paper, of course, endorses as a whole
neither one set of maxims nor the other, and
merely comments on the inutility of the contrast.
The inference obviously meant to be drawn is
that economic science is all wrong, since it
disagrees with Christ. The inference that
obviously should be drawn is that Christ is
some 1865 years behind the age —or in front of
it, as you please. Economics are based on
experience—things as they are. Christ’s teach-
ing is based on hopes and longings—things as
we might like them to be. Plainly, Christ is
an unsafe guide. As Bishop Magee said, “ a
State which transacted its business on the prin-
ciples of the Sermon in the Mount would not
exist for a week.” “Resist not evil” is a
pusillanimous precept, an encouragement and
defence to brutality and tyranny. “Give to
him that asketh thee” deprives industry of its
legitimate reward, and is coolly disregarded by
the holiest elder who sands sugar. So with
others. We live in earth, not Heaven ; and
Christ’s most vital maxims are more applicable
to Heaven than to earth. It is no use kicking
against the pricks. Human nature sticks out
all round us, and economics are the essence of
human nature. You can’t bridge here and
hereafter in a day or a generation. Begin
building, by all means ; but you must use the
materials to hand. The conditions neces-
sary for the success of Christ’s principles
aren’t to hand —are not within centuries of us.
So we must just use principles applicable to the
conditions which are to hand —drive our nail as
far as it will go, and not an inch farther.
And, as the Rev. C. W. Stubbs knows quite
well that this is so, and gets .£I4OO a year as
Dean of Ely because it is so, and doesn’t in the
least propose to carry the teaching of Christ
in his own person further than employing it to
make a neat literary antithesis, one wonders
why so many people rave and imagine a vain
thing at his bidding or suggestion.

An Alleged “ Sacrifice.”
So much has been said about the alleged
£550,000 which the Reid Cabinet is sacrificing
by abolishing the duties levied under the recent
Dibbs Government that very few people seem
to have noticed up to date that the “ sacrifice ”
is altogether bogus. It is bogus, none the less,
and, as a consequence, the promise that the
taxpayer will be relieved of £550,000 of Customs
taxation is equally bogus. The relief, to those
who look merely on the surface of things, ap-
pears sufficiently real, inasmuch as certain
duties, which now yield £550,000 per annum,
are to be abolished ; but the matter won’t bear
intelligent inspection. Reid prox>oses, for the
time being, to revert to the old Parkes tariff,
which yielded about £1,900,000 per annum from
Customs alone. When the Dibbs tariff came into
force the Customs revenue boomed for the first
year to £2,677,000. But all Protectionist tariffs
tend to reduce Customs revenue by discourag-
ing imports and encouraging local production,
and, as usual, imports diminished in New South
Wales, and local supplies increased. The
Customs revenue for ’93 dropped to £2,127,000.
In ’94 it further dropped to £2,072,000 ; and
the returns for ’95, so far as they are available,
suggest that it has now got down toashadeunder
£2,ooo,oooperannum. Premier Reid proposes to
replace this with that reliable old tariff which
grinds out £1,900,000 or so per annum with
such beautiful regularity, and calls the dif-
ference a remission of £550,000, whereas it is
really less than one-fifth of that amount. The
increase in the quantity of imports under the
low tariff almost makes up for the difference in
duties, according to all past experience, and
the alteration in the “ burden” is hardly worth
mentioning. The Bulletin is getting tired of
that bogus “sacrifice.” It is also tired of the
companion statement that the Government
can’t carry out its policy unless it gets the land
and income tax to fill the gap in the revenue,
for by establishing a revenue tariff instead of
its much-promised Freetrade policy, it has care-
fully avoided creating any gap.
Much of the trafficking in forbidden articles,
insuppressible now in Australian prisons, would
be discontinued if the French system of “ can-
teen ” were introduced. Under this a prisoner
can spend daily at the gaol-store a certain por-
tion of his earnings. The privilege, which is
eminently equitable, produces quite a crop of
beneficial results. In the first place, it mini-
mising trafficking, it being recognised that the
prisoner is entitled to purchase, with his own
money, harmless luxuries such as tobacco and
cheese, which otherwise he would, often success-
fully, seek to obtain illicitly. Then it permits
the well-behaved man to immediately enjoy the
fruits of his good conduct. To be debarred
from “ canteen ” for a week, is, to the French
detenu, a serious punishment. Not so is the
deprivation of a dozen marks or so under the
artificial English system, which makes both its
penalties and awards too remote. A man
naturally feels the immediate lopping-off of
luxuries more than he would an addition of a
week to his sentence of years. The very fact
that one penalty is more human than the other
makes it more impressive. Finally, when
prisoners daily handle their earnings, and when
those earnings fluctuate according to industry,
the stimulus to work is far greater and healthier
than that of gaining the three extra marks which
represent a quarter of a day’s remission of sen-
tence. Criminals, above all other men, live for
the day. The Bulletin hopes that some en-
lightened prison administrator in Australia, if
such a being exists, will urge his Government
to give “ canteen ” a trial. The cat and gag
have had a fair innings ; and a change to some-
thing sensible is about due.

N.S.W. Justice Minister Slattery got his
prison-gag regulations almost word for word
from the Queensland gaol code, apropos of
which the following extract from Kinnaird
Rose and Parry Okeden’s sensational report,
in ’B9, on Q. gaols is curious reading : 4
The evidence before us does not convince us that the
lash or the trax improves discipline, but the contrary.
The gag has been abolished in New South Wales, and, so
far as we can ascertain, in England. We recommend that
the use of the gag should be abolished.
That recommendation was ignored, and now, as
a consequence, New South Wales quotes
Queensland in justification of the barbarous
instrument! In Victoria the gag is not
authorised, but, all the same, it is sometimes
used. Only very rarely, however, because the
dark and “dumb” cell, in small doses, is
found equally effective and much more humane.
Flogging for offences committed in prison is
legalised in Queensland, New South Wales, and
South Australia. Victoria is following suit, in
deference to the persistent appeal of Inspector-
General Evans, whose naval experience has
naturally afflicted him with an insatiable crav-
ing for “ cat.” Last session, the Bill providing
for the infliction of the lash was slaughtered,
but that is to be avoided this Parliament by
the earlier introduction of the measure. On the
whole, therefore, Australia, in the matter of
criminology, is admirably maintaining its pres-
tige for crablike progress.
The Economist, commenting on Judge Moles-
worth’s recent outbreak in Melb. Insolvency
Court, says : “ Until the Judges are absolutely
independent, it is scarcely to be wondered at
if British investors fear lest political exigency
should defeat justice in Victoria.” Until
Judges hold aloof from Fat Man gatherings
and refrain from hobnobbing with “ representa-
tive ” persons, at home or abroad ; until they
cut all connection with Guvment House, finan-
cial speculation, freemasonry, and parlia-
mentary theatre-parties, they will never be
independent. Molesworth, notwithstanding
that his salary remains subject to political in-
fluence, is probably the most independent Judge
of the whole crowd. Anyhow, he is less
friendly with Fat Man than are some of his col-
leagues. How can Chief Justice Madden —no
matter how honest his intention, he is still but
human ! —be regarded as independent ? He
plays, with infinite relish, the part of Acting-
Governor ; the Speaker and Premier are his
two nearest official relatives, “ everybody who
is anybody ” becomes more or less friendly with
his wife and family. He is socially “ nobbled ”
every day of his life, though he receives a
very large salary (to say nothing of his huge
prospective pension), which was in part surely
intended to be the price of his social isolation.
Had he been Acting-Governor five years ago
the officials who were the chief apostles of the
Boom would have figured as his chief disciples
at levees and solemn functions in general. He
does all that he ought not to do if he
wishes to be, and to be regarded as being a fit
and proper person to preside over the adminis-
tration of justice when the next land-boom
bursts, and the next lot of mushroom bankers
fall due for punishment, and the subtlest and
most adroit of “influences” come forward to
protect them. Experience has proved that the
occupants of high places in Victoria are the
most likely to put themselves within reach of
the criminal law—the criminal law’s indisposi-
tion to grab hold of them doesn’t affect the
argument—and yet the chuckle-headed public
fail to observe the sin of an arrangement which
places the Chief Justice in close official and
social intercourse with a possible den of thieves.
This is wild talk, maybe. But there’s a lot of
truth sandwiched between the shrieks.
