The friendly understanding between China
and Russia (evidenced by Russia’s undertaking to
guarantee the imminent Chinese £17,000,000 loan)
must strengthen still further the bond between
Britain and Japan lately cemented by a commer-
cial treaty —even though Japan offers to extend
the same commercial privileges to Russia. No
Greek bearing gifts was ever more to be
feared than Russia. She probably looks for
China’s failure to meet her obligations, when,
either by agreement or by force, Russia will
acquire new territory at Chinese expense.
George Higinbotham: Democrat.
Professor Morris, of Melbourne University,
somewhat ambiguously confesses that it had
long been his ambition to write a life of George
Higinbotham. He has achieved his ambition ;
and the life of George Higinbotham will be
composed of all that Prof. Morris has omitted.
For things left undone the Professor will
be entitled to the coining biographer’s thanks.
Apart from ipsa dicta, there is not one bright
spot, not a single quotable passage, not a soli-
tary snappy sentence throughout the book.
Its utmost merit is to contain a certain amount
of journalistic jackal work—prepared food for
the literary lion who will breathe soul into the
story of the Aristides of Australia. It forms
an admirable record of dates, election-polls,
&c., and a handy, though by no means com-
plete, compendium of speeches and documents.
Where and when Higinbotham was born ; who
were his parents, his ancestors and his early
friends; where and with whom he went to
school; how he passed through Dublin Univer-
sity ; how he started life on London Morning
Chronicle ; why, and in what vessel, he went to
Australia, and what he did when he got there ;
when and whom he married, and how he became
a barrister, an editor, a M.P., a responsible
Minister, a plain member again, a simple citizen
lawyer, a Judge, Chief Justice, lecturer, Exhi-
bition President, and strike-sympathiser—such
items are all set down in proper chronological
order, with due and discreet detail. Every-
thing has been said with the greatest propriety
in the meekest and mildest of Morrisese ; and
The Bulletin is glad that none of it is ever
likely to be said again.
* # *
When a biographer, who is the son-in-law of
his subject, announces that he has no special
material to work upon, all diaries, letters,
MSS., &c., having been destroyed in accordance
with a testamentary direction, there at least
remains the hope that ana will have been col-
lected and presented in more or less readable
Boswellian style. This hope is gloomily dis-
appointed. There is hardly an item in the
book that has not been published before. Cold
slabs of Hansard and ragouts of newspaper are
mechanically arrayed. Sometimes associates of
the dead Chief are given a show, but generally
they forget to talk about Higinbotham, and
abuse the misguided Prof. Morris’s biblospi-
tality by wandering into egotistical excursions.
Several alleged friends trespass with pointless
anecdotes and irrelevant remarks, and one
throws off a deliberately concocted eulogy.
With the exception of the interesting Higin-
botham letters sent in by his forlorn friend
Richardson these diversions of the main bio-
graphical current are very tiresome ; and several
more or less brief biographies of various unim-
portant persons, and histories of uninteresting
institutions, localities and newspapers, would
have been better left out.
* * *
Among the very few valuable specimens
buried in Prof. Morris’s smoky amber are Higin-
botham’s noble and statesmanlike oration on
his resolutions referring to Victoria’s imperial
relations ; and the simple, yet dynamitic, letter
with which he forwarded the first instalment of
his strike subscriptions to the Trades Hall
Council. These two deliverances are the lofty
measure of Higinbotham’s proportions as a
statesman and a Democrat. It is good that
they should be revived, for it is essential that
they be never forgotten. One is almost startled
to find them in this book of big omissions, and
their presence can only be accounted for by the
circumstance that they could not, with decency,
have been left out. No qualms, however,
preserved the magnificent Brunswick oration of
1874 from suppression. In it were advocated
One Man One Vote, the taxation of Wealth,
and the abolition of the Legislative Council.
Then the steady growth of labor organisations
was traced, and combination was justified
against “that wealth which, when not regulated
by law—when not reduced to terms of equality
with Labor—is, in spite of everything political
economists may say, the most deadly enemy of
Labor.” Finally, the orator prophesied an im-
pending upheaval consequent upon “ the rapid
growth of poverty at one end and of wealth at
the other end of the social scale. ” The ‘ ‘ Science
and Religion ” lecture is noticed, but is slurred
over. No mention is made of the fact that it
condemned “ the representation of God in the
likeness of man,” as “no longer possible in any
shape.” Or, that the lecturer declared “ arbi-
trary dealing, capricious favor, vengeful punish-
ment, sudden passionate change ” to be “ attri-
butes wholly unthinkable in regard to the
Creator.” Or, that he “ finally condemned all
Christian churches alike.” Not a word is hinted
of the “ Strong persecution ” which was the
direct outcome of the deliverance. No comment
is made on the fact that the great thinker
had, in 1883, only brought up to date the
opinions held by him in 1868-9, when he
said, among many other things, that “he
did not believe the religious denominations
in this country had anything to do with
religion;” and that “the Church of England
in all the Australian colonies was unquestion-
ably the most weak, undecided, and incon-
sistent with itself of all of the religious
denominations.” These are a few among many
things which Prof. Morris might have recalled
without in any respect breaking his apparent
rule of only utilising published material.
* * *
Prof. Morris sat down to the manufacture of
his memoir a pedagogue, a fanatic Freetrader,
an Imperial Federationist, an ultra-loyalist, an
inflexible churchman, a bigoted Conservative,
and an Argus contributor. He brought with
him all the prejudice and narrowness of a re-
stricted training, disposition, and environment:
his cherished ideas were Higinbotham’s abom-
ination ; his friends and connections were, or
had been, Higinbotham’s bitterest enemies ;
his sympathies, sentiments, and tastes were
alien to those of his subject; and his offence is
that he has attempted to tincture that giant
with the taints of his own pigmy school, to
belittle his true nobleness, and to dilute his
uncompromising fervor for Democracy. This
Prof. Morris has done to soothe his own vanity,
which every honest thought of Higinbotham’s
must have ruffled ; to avoid offence to his own
friends and patrons ; and, lastly, to spite the
people who have set up Higinbotham as an
idol in their hearts. Such an offence is unfor-
giveable.
• • *
By the exercise of all the insidious arts he
possesses, which are not many, Prof. Morris at-
tempts to tone down Higinbotham’s democratic
character by limning the man as a Freetrader,
an anti-Home Ruler, a loyalist, an anti-imperial
Separatist, and a conservative. As for his
abstract adherence to Freetrade, that is out-
balanced by the concrete fact that but for him
Protection would not have been established in
Victoria so soon as it was. The remarkable
assertion that he was an anti-Home Ruler im-
plies an anomaly in a logical mind which is as
utterly incomprehensible as it is incredible.
But his expressions of loyalty were strictly
official, an empty courtesy paid to an equally
empty form ; and they are outweighed by the
circumstance that he always referred to Eng-
land as a “foreign power,” and to the monarch
as a “foreign potentate.” His position on
Separation was practically, “ Let us retain all
the advantages of formal connection, but recog-
nise no vital obligations on either side. Make
the connection weaker, but vigorously oppose
every movement that would make it stronger.”
On these terms the Jingoes and Freetraders
can have Higinbotham, and get what joy they
can out of the possession.
# # »
Coming to Mr. Morris’s statement that “ his
mind was essentially Conservative,” it is neces-
sary to exhibit Higinbotham with every em-
phasis as he was, the embodiment and exemplar
of every democratic principle. In every rela-
tion between himself and his fellow men, except
in the formalities of ceremonial functions when
he merely represented his office, Higinbotham
embodied the ideal of the republican trilogy :
Libert}', Equality, Fraternity. Moreover, The
Bulletin can produce a hundred extracts from his
utterances to conclusively prove that, through-
out the 40 years which they cover, he was a
consistent Radical. Indeed, item for item, it
can be shown that he, at some time or other,
advocated almost every plank that constitutes
the platform of this paper. Under his editor-
ship the Argus advocated the abolition of
oaths, the abolition of capital punishment,
women’s suffrage, one-man-one-vote, and a
land-tax. His acts were as his principles. He
steadfastly adhered to the principle of payment
of members, although he left his salary in the
Treasury because he did not need it. He was
the declared enemy of Government by Contra-
diction, and a sworn foe to compromise. He
relinquished private legal practice so long as he
drew a salary as Attorney-General. He would
not act as a Minister so long as illegal imperial
instructions to the Governor were tolerated.
He would not accept a Judgeship except on the
understanding that he would not be subject to
any such instructions referring to the Judges.
He refused knighthood. He abhorred the pre-
tensions, the mummeries, and the aristocratic
airs of the vulgar coterie that dubs itself
“Society.” To his judgment in the Ah Toy
case Australia owes the recognition of its right
to exclude aliens. He looked forward to the
time when “ the world would come to having a
class of advocates paid like Judges, by the
State, and not by clients, and that to them
causes would be assigned to conduct fairly.”
His masterly treatment of the Shenandoah case,
in the face of traitorous Tory obstruction, is a
standing lesson to Australian statesmen in
international law. He performed (gratuitously)
the herculean task of consolidating the statutes
that the law might become cheaper and less
confusing to the people. To his guiding
influence, almost directly, Victoria owes the
principles of free, secular and compulsory
education. The second of his famous resolu-
tion on the imperial relations made the Legis-
lative Assembly acknowledge “ the obligation
to provide for the defence of the shores of
Victoria against foreign invasion, by means
furnished at the sole cost, and retained within
the exclusive control of the people of Victoria,”
and this Declaration of Australian Independ-
ence, graven on the records of the House, the
Assembly, in an act of base self-stultification,
under the baleful influence of the Gillies-
Dbakin combination, literally spat upon when
it carried the iniquitous naval subsidy to the
accompaniment of “ God save the Queen.” A
column to the same effect could be written,
exposing in every line the utter fatuousness of
the Tory claim to the great Democrat. But on
the terms here laid down the Conservatives can
again have him, and get what joy they can out
of the possession.
* # #
Higinbotham’s most heroic effort was his
attack on the principle of imperial interference
with Australian affairs ; and the precise effect
that the attack would have had if successful
never seems to have been calmly contemplated.
His aim, still unachieved, was to give Victoria,
and practically Australia, complete independ-
ence in internal Government. The Crown might
appoint a Governor, but there its authority
would end. That Governor should have no dele-
gated duties apart from those indicated by the
Constitution and expounded by his responsible
advisers. If it were necessary—absolutely
essential—to correspond with England, the
correspondence should be conducted directly
between the Premier and the Secretary of State,
just as the Premier of one free foreign country
communicates with the Premier of another.
Had this end been gained, an Australian pro-
vince would have become a peculiarly free and
happy country, combining, as it would, all the
advantages of every enlightened form of govern-
ment, and minimising, or totally discarding,
their defects. It would have been more repub-
lican than a republic, while ensconced in the
mask of a mock monarchy—easily worn, and as
easily doffed. A formal, altogether forceless
figurehead, appointed from afar without popular
friction, maintained without appanage or any
parasitical expense, and freed from the tempta-
tion, because without the power, to favor or to
patronise, would have presided over a Govern-
ment constitutionally embodying the declared
dominant will of the people. Such was his ideal
Democracy. It was never properly understood,
although the Tory gang and the imperial
authorities instinctively and accurately gauged
it as the Prologue to a Republic. The Democrat
used no adventitious arts to realise his design;
his opponents used every means, scrupulous
and unscrupulous, to thwart it. They suc-
ceeded, and having failed in his sublime object,
the sphere of. peddling politics became the
statesman’s only prospect. So he withdrew,
and his ideal Democracy remained a dream.
• # *
Prof. Morris’s memoir is silent upon the
subject of the mean and cruel opposition
that did Governor Darling to death, and
would have rejoiced to have ruined and humili-
ated Higinbotham. The Argus accounts for
this reticence. “ Professor Morris,” it purrs,
“ has too much good taste to revive, more than
is necessary, forgotten feuds.” But the needs
of Democracy demand that they shall never be
forgotten. The Legislative Council, the 22
petitioning Executive Councillors, the Conser-
vative press, the squattocracy, the mercantile
classes, the professions, the banks, and each
traitorous toady whose patriotism had a price,
acted in the battles of the Darling grant just
as they would under similar circumstances act
again. All of these influences had their mouth-
pieces in England. The press commanded un-
limited space in leading English reviews ; the
others had their plaints phonographed by tuft-
hunting absentees at chance interviews with
Secretaries of State in the privity of the Home
office. By these agents the Secretary of State
was treacherously soaked with truthless versions
of the fight, while the local principals did their
share by bullying the Governor, traducing the
Ministry, and sending threats of assassination
to its most violently detested members. The
Argus played its part with a ferocious bril-
liance that it has never since commanded. It
referred to the Cabinet of which Higinbotham
was declared to be the “ dominating influence,”
the “ animating spirit,” as one “ marked with
little variation by deceit, chicane and lawless-
ness ” : it was said to have “ lowered the tone
of political morality,” and its acts were des-
cribed as “illegal, dishonorable and unworthy.”
Higinbotham was a man “ possessed by self-
esteem and pertinacity ” and a “variable and
distempered mind.” His principles were
declared “ revolutionary and impracticable.”
In the English journals its scribes implied that
he was the chief of a crew of thieving cut-throats.
He was accused of “extraordinary arrogance
and violence,” of “despotism,” and “blind
rancour,” while the steps that were taken on
his advice were stigmatised as “a series of
revolutionary crimes.” Opposition members
called him “traitor,” and spoke of his “treason.”
So much was said in the heat of the fray. Only
the vitriolic historian Rusden could have had
the temerity to describe him in cooler after
years as a man “ who would have striven to
carry a point at the cost of disorder and blood-
shed. . . . It seemed that life and property
might be unsafe if Mr. Higinbotham could have
his way.” Such were the estimates formed of
him when he was alive and in the forefront of
the fight. Those who agreed with these things
are naturally grateful because Morris has not
marshalled them forth to stand side by side
with the now reiterated hypocrisies mouthed
two years ago across his open grave.
# # #
This chapter is missing from Prof. Morris’s
memoir. As suggested in the beginning, the Book—
which will be the Bible of Australian Democrats,
just as Higinbotham was the Jesus of Australian
Democracy—will be made up of Prof. Morris’s
omissions. Still, this life has not been written
in vain. The Professor confesses that he dis-
couraged several biographers, among whom The
Bulletin surmises the coming biographer was
probably one, and he has now shot his own
bolt. His work represents exactly how much
the dead Democrat’s family will contribute
towards the telling of the complete story.
It has made the necessity for the whole truth
imperative, for the memory of a great man
awaits a noble vindication. Apparently, Prof.
Morris’s sole qualification for writing this
biography is the fact that he was Higin-
botham’s son-in-law. If the example is to be
generally followed, The Bulletin devoutly
trusts that succeeding sons-in-law will have
worse fathers-in-law, or that succeeding
fathers-in-law will have better biographers.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivery may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Tobv’s Rolled Oats.

Sydney Lyceum was crammed on the first
night of the “ Gaiety Girl,” and the audience took
to the new form of entertainment very kindly. It
is not distinctly a burlesque, nor an opera, nor a
comedy, nor anything else that is calculated to
supply the police with a clue. There is a faint
shadow of a plot in it, or at least various things
are done, or left undone, which lead to an indis-
tinct impression that the author once intended to
begin to think about putting something of the sort
in it, but never actually got there. To have a
plot it is necessary that people should do some-
thing definite, and when the wicked girl has to
sing various songs and do eight or more dances
while she is on her way to do it she hasn’t time to
arrive. The “ Gaiety Girl ”is rather a spiecies of
indiscriminate dazzle in two beautiful fits. The
performers assume an aspect of seriousness by
wearing their .everyday clothes, which im-
proves things considerably, for a thing that
wouldn’t look funny if done by a girl in
tights seems at least remarkable when per-
petrated by a sedate-looking lady in a long
skirt and a bonnet. Almost every girl in the
show is a separate and distinct dream of love-
liness, and each one is lovelier than all the rest
put together. Miss Madge Rossell’s solo-dance is
about the wildest, prettiest, cleverest thing of its
kind ever seen in Sydney. Miss Grace Palotta is
another vision, who sings a song in which the
public is invited to “take a ’bus,” and gets a tre-
mendous encore. She has also most to do with
constructing the shadowy plot aforementioned,
and here the lamentable fact obtrudes itself like a
bowsprit that the vision can’t act worth a cent.
But, regarded as a vision, Miss Grace is irre-
proachable. She was the first principal to appear
on Saturday night, and when she smiled the house
was undone.
Dainty Decima Moore acts her
little part much better, and sings and
dances admirably. As for the heroine,
Miss Blanche Massey has little to do
save look icily beautiful and languish
under a baseless accusation, and she
languishes in a poetic frock and a hat
which must commend itself to all.
Misses Orossland, Murray and May
Lucas do a high-kick dance which en-
trances every bald beholder ; and the
massive Miss Maud Hobson, repre-
senting a lady with a wicked past
who rashly left her comb on the pre-
mises of the co-respondent, fills her
comedy part well and intelligently. It
is a lamentable fact, however, that
Charles Ryley looks rather heavy and
can’t sing the rousing strain of “Tommy
Atkins ” anything like Arnold used to
sing it. Harry Monk house, the very
large, pale, fat comedian, is probably
the funniest person in the show, and
the mere fact that he represents the
bright Decima’s parent gives him a
claim upon this country’s respectful
consideration. Also, the perky Leed-
ham Bantock, as the judge, comes in a
moderate second.
** * *
, The Dacres began their Sydney
season at Her Majesty’s with “Esther
Sandraz,” an eminently artistic play,
with a moral less apparent than it
might be. Its hero, played by Mr.
Dacre, is a nondescript, hard-up man
of fashion, who is intensely in love with
a Portuguese semi-adventuress. Miss
Amy Roselle, in the latter character,
is also hard-up, therefore he throws
her over, and marries a girl with
money. The girl was deeply in love
with another man, who was likewise hard-up,
and he is also thrown over. The husband and
wife lead a respectably miserable life together,
and he spends his spare time writing passionate
love-letters to his lost Portuguese, and asking
her to come. Finally she comes, and won’t go
away again. She is a blight upon him. She
tells him perpetually what a miserable object
he was, and is, and will be as long as he
lives. She reads his love-letters to him, and ex-
plains about his wife’s former engagement, and
breathes horribly into his ear, and whispers
cankering suspicions into his back-hair. She
brings the wife's former lover into the house, and
shows him to the anguished husband from behind
the pot-plant in the doorway, and the husband
rushes out to get his gun. While he is looking for the
gun Scot Inglis, as the lover, grasps the situation
with both hands, and brings the lady to a state of
repentance. He fixes her with his large, grave
eye, and talks to her in a low, monotonous,
musical voice, and apparently she goes off
into a sort of benevolent trance. Just here
the husband comes in and shoots her by
mistake, and later on he blows out his
unsatisfactory brains in the back premises, while
the dying woman is babbling about “ mother ”
the said mother having been a wild creature who
habitually carried firearms.
The husband is a weak, morally-unfinished
person all through. He has no visible character,
and Mr. Dacre probably does all that can be done
with him by endowing him with a lovely mous-
tache and a pair of most poetic legs, and letting
him drift. Miss Amy Roselle hardly looks her
part of the brilliant adventuress in the first act,
but after that, as the black-robed curse which
comes home to roost on the soul of the leggy
betrayer, she is profoundly impressive. Her cold
sneer is a work of genius, and her laugh suggests
an echo from the bottomless pit, and she is
a perfectly lady-like fury dancing on the
stomach of her lost admirer according to
the strictest rules of etiquette. Miss Agnes
Knights is a pleasant nonentity in a white frock,
and Miss Kate Bishop’s fine acting is obscured by
a glaring red costume, like the sun setting behind
meat in a butcher’s shop. Stirling Whyte, as a
funny, bad old man, lends the best support to the
star. The presumable moral of the drama is that
man should marry for love instead of money, and
the said moral is considerably obscured by the
fact that the woman Vandelle married for money
is much nicer than the one he didn’t marry for
love. Moreover, Scot Inglis, the time he has her
fixed with his eye, convinces the latter that she
never loved at all, so there is no moral left.
** * *
Rev. Haskett Smith lectures to-night
(Thursday) at Balmain, and on Friday at North
Sydney, and gives a farewell lecture in the city on
Saturday. Whoever wants reliable information
about Palestine, and about the dead hog that
swells to an unnatural size in the stagnant pool of
Siloam, and the thick, green scum which seems to
be everywhere on top of the local water-supply,
and the flies, and the ruins, and other things,
should avail himself of this final opportunity.
** * *
The Irish drama at Sydney Royal, which
once looked as if it would out-last time itself, and
be still going on when the earth is hung out like a
fish to dry in the blackened heavens, and the sun
has faded to a pale-green corpse-light, and Parkes’s
last poem is blowing around a frozen universe, is
now actually doing its last nights. It and Maggie
Moore will probably depart together at the end
of next week, or in a month or two, or some years
hence.
** * *
The minstrel shows at the Tivoli and the
Empire have done nothing sufficiently unusual to
call for extended notice. Both continue reason-
ably healthy, and either can be recommended as
a very cheap refuge from the wet.
** * *
About years ago the death of Fred.
Leslie left a great gap in the burlesque show then
running at the London Gaiety, and Louis Brad-
field was the young actor promoted to the vacant
place. The fact that he was subsequently engaged
to undertake Leslie parts on tour speaks for itself.
Manager Edwardes couldn’t find another actor so
well-qualified for the work, and, again, when “The
Gaiety Girl” travelling co. was organised, the
honor of taking over an Arthur Roberts part in
one of their musical comedies fell to the versatile
new man. Mr. Bradfield, critically considered,
lacks distinction of style and stage presence.
Leslie had a valuable figure, fine eyes, and a
flexible, incisive voice. Arthur Roberts, also not-
Photo. by Falk.
LOUIS BRADFIELD, Gaiety Co. Comedian.
able for his eyes and voice, is a more strikingly
original “ droll ” than even Leslie. Louis Bradfield
has an ordinary sorb of voice, and an everyday
type of face, and his acting is more or less strongly
suggestive of Leslie when it isn’t very like Roberts.
All the same, Mr. Bradfield is a first-rate enter-
tainer —bright, brisk, amusing and clever at every
point. His “In Town” performance has been
overpraised by the Melb. press, bub that is nob a
surprising circumstance. A sense of proportion
in theatrical matters is not expected of news-
papers, or from the public, who estimate the
excellence of an actor’s ability—always provided
thab he has ability—by the length and breadth of
the character he happens to be filling ab the
moment.
* * *
On Saturday last Bland Holt had the
“ pleasing gratification” [vide advt.) of introduc-
ing “The Fatal Card” to Melbourne. He also
had the pleasing gratification of knowing that the
capacious Theatre Royal was not large enough for
the occasion, and of hearing the distant moan of
the populace whose money was refused at the
door. Evidently the populace knew something
about this successful play from Sydney. The
strength of “The Fatal Card” lies in its con-
tempt for melodramatic precedent. To begin with,
the hero has quarrelled with his inflexible parent
and gone abroad ere the curtain ascends, and as
early as the second act he and the villains are
back in London, although nobody expected them
back before 10 o’clock. The murdered corpse lies
weltering in its gore for a longer time than
usual, and the oration delivered in its honor
by the remorseful accessory is quite a novelty
in after-murder speaking. Also, the final
arrest of the chief wrong-doer by two seedy
policemen is dispensed with. An infernal
machine brings “The Fatal Card ” to a sudden
end. The box of ruin explodes with a loud
report, destroying a freehold cottage, together
with the benevolent burglar of the piece, but the
hero’s innocence remains unproven. Indeed, the
good young man seems tolerably certain to get
hanged (for no Judge Windeyer would swallow
his statement of all that has occurred) whilst the
real murderer waxes fat on stolen bonds. There
are many original, or semi-original, situations in
the play, and this last situation is the best of all.
One likes to think the hero dies upon the gallows
for the sake of variety. Walter Baker is, ad-
mittedly, a general favorite. His escapes from un-
just suspicion, through aseries of melodramas, have
been well received, yet it is time for his luck to
take a bad turn. The laws of chance must be
respected, and virtue can’t go on turning up
triumphant for ever. Why shouldn’t Baker hang
occasionally, so that Cosgrove may lurk around
the gaol and fire off his fiendish laugh when the
black flag is hoisted ? Why shouldn’t Mr. O. live
to buy a copy of the evening journal containing a
full account of Baker’s sickening thud, and pre-
sent it to Miss Hilda Spong, saying, “ I’m glad to
see his legs twitched convulsively, ha-ha ! ” The
reward of industry ought to come to a man who
has scored three murders and “several behinds”
—as footballers would say—in the last 10
weeks. Cosgrove works hard in this drama,
whereas Baker is a hero without a purpose.
You find him drinking bad whisky in Colorado,
until one of the barmaids sings “ Be it never so
hura6e(f, there’s no place like Home.” That sends
him off to Hingland. A braver man might have
stopped to face a repetition of the ballad, but
Baker flies immediately. In Hingland he becomes
a mere dude, with strong tendencies to loaf on his
father, a most respectable and mercenary old gen-
tleman. He carries a flash walking-stick, and
with this emblem of indolence the aged relative is
casually slain ; therefore, the blood of his father is
more or less upon Walter Baker’s head. All
things considered, he deserves to be hanged for a
change.
• * *
The mounting of the “ Fatal Card ” is
exceptionally handsome and solid, even for a
Bland Holt show. The dialogue has a literary
backbone, and some of the characters are better
drawn than quartered. Albert Norman, as a
burglar with mixed emotions, would be more satis-
factory if he spoke his long speeches instead of
declaiming them. Miss Yirgie Vivienne needs to
borrow a bigger voice for the evening, and Charles
Brown could do with the loan of a cold, commer-
cial demeanor. Walter Baker, Cosgrove and Miss
Spong are effective in their usual places, but Mr,
and Mrs. Bland Holt have to palm off absurdity
as humor. Nobody, thus far, has been very funny
in a Haddon Chambers play. Haddon’s notions
of comedy are crude, and his collaborateur,
Stephenson, seems to have written a comic scene
for the gallery only.
** * *
“An Ideal Husband,” at Melb. Princess’,
has not filled the treasury in accordance with the
promise of its first night. Oscar Wilde’s play
“knocked them” on the Saturday, after which
the supply of enthusiasts began to run short, and
the acting lost a little of its early vigor in conse-
quence. Seeing that a brilliant play by a notorious
author has excited so little interest, there is
reason to fear that B. and B. came along at the
wrong moment. High-class comedy wasn’t loudly
called for, judging from the general appearance
of the dress-circle. Perhaps the last perform-
ance of “An Ideal Husband” will be rushed.
On Saturday (22nd) “The Masqueraders” goes
up for the first time in Melbourne, and Mr.
Arthur Elwood introduces himself to notice.
** * *
Miss Ribbentrop’s first appearance at
Melb. Town Hall gave some color to the report
that she possesses a splendid soprano voice, but
the tall and stately damsel of 22 didn’t sing up
to what was expected of Clifford Halle’s re-
presentative pupil. She was very nervous,
and if there is much natural sweetness in her
tones, stage fright certainly spoilt their quality
the other evening. Furthermore, her lower notes
showed the very faults which her tutor holds up
to scorn. Clifford Halle made the errors of his
star soprano the more apparent by singing admir-
ably for his own part, and the concert was a suc-
cess in that it attracted a good crowd of people
who will be disposed to give Miss Ribbentrop
another hearing.

Nellie Stewart points out to an interviewer that her
salary in London as Blue-eyed Susan was twice as much
as that she received on returning to Australia. Outside
the perfesh a short salary for a long time is considered
better than a long salary for a short time.

An incident of the Melb. theatrical carnival. A pretty
damsel dressed in a short, showy frock, who was selling
photos, of star artistes taken in tights, accosted a simple-
looking young curate and asked him to buy a photo, of
Miss Marie Luella dressed in black tights, or “ would he
kindly take one of Miss Palotta in a bathing costume?”
But the poor shepherd was obdurate and yielded not,
though she “ threw her whole soul ” at him.
The impudent and irrepressible Mr. J. R. Rogers,
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, appeared
to such great disadvantage in the divorce-court, a few
days ago, that no one would accuse him of being “ Yours
Merrily.” Mr. Justice Jeune seemed determined to sift
to the bottom the whole of the suspicious circumstances
connected with his former proceedings against Minnie
Palmer. Sir William Rose, who was the co-respondent
on that occasion, has given evidence and strongly indi-
cated that cash, not freedom from marital chains, was
what Rogers was seeking. George Lewis likewise gave
evidence to the same effect. The giddy old Bart, said that
£5OOO was demanded from him, but eventually some £6OO
was accepted, coupled with the promise (so Rogers says)
that “ a dozen co-respondents ” would be found for him.
Ada Crossley, Marie Elster, Lalla Miranda, Florence
Saville all gorfe to London ; Flo Graupner about to go ;
Florence Young thinking about it; Violet Varley dead.
What it has come to in Melbourne. Argus advt.:— A
Lady will give good lessons, singing and piano, in return
for dresses made. Music, Argus.
The musical critic of a Melbourne morning paper is a
prominent figure at Marshall Hall’s orchestral concerts,
sitting in the front row of the gallery with a tremendous
score of Wagner’s “Tristram ’’ spread out before him on
the rail, following the performance note by note. A rapt
look of critical sagacitv is fixed upon his face, and he
turns over the leaves with much rustling. People who
pose thus and can’t form an impression of the music
without carrying a library about ought to be suppressed.
Hilda Spong leaves Bland Holt and melodrama shortly,
and joins B. & B. at Melb. Princess’s.
Geraldine Olliffe’s Bernhardtesque trick of dropping
her voice from a high, sweet soprano to deep bass when
she wishes to express rage or passion, makes a profound
impression on the Melb. audience. In other respects,
too, she recalls the great Sarah —notably in her gliding,
panther-like stride, and her manner of throwing herself
down in a heap in the corner of a sofa.
The Gaiety people do not find the Australian tour all
joy. On Wednesday, their last day in Adelaide, there
was a “Shop Girl” matinee at 2 p.m., performance at
7.30, and at 11.30 a tedious train journey right through
to Sydney had to be commenced. Altogether, there were
13 performances in Adelaide in 10 days, not counting
rehearsals.
The Gaiety takings for 10 nights in Adelaide are
estimated at £2600, including three matinee perform-
ances. The profit to the “ firm ” is estimated, by one
behind the scenes, at about £BOO.
Decima Moore’s singing voice is liable to go at any
time, as she is a victim to asthma, and cannot sing in
England in winter.
Apparently it pays better now to tame the raging lion
than to draw the raging tooth. At any rate, Arthur
Russell, a Bourke-street (Melb.) dentist, thinks so,
for he is preparing to tour Australia with a cage of
trick lions. The part of Daniel in the lion’s den will bo
undertaken by Miss Florence, a near relative of Arthur
Russell, he himself contributing conjuring and spook
items. Two of Russell’s lions, by the way, are Australian
natives, as also are both Mr. Russell and Miss Florence.
Louis Bradfield, of the Gaiety Co., was formerly a
lieutenant in the navy.
Melb. Royal will be to let from 14th Sept, next for a
term, including Cup-week and the pantomime season.
What odds against Maggie Moore doing another burlesque
and panto, season there?
HYoung “Sammy Hayes,” with a dramaticco., including
old-stagers like Jack Hesford, Will South, and Teddy
Haygarth, fills Collingwood (Melb.) Town Hall once a
week to overflowing. Admission, one silver coin ; average
takings, £3O per night. Dan Barry’s Co. and the Taylor-
Carrington’s have both tried to oust “Sammy,” and
failed utterly.
B. and B. in Melb. billed Oscar Wilde’s play, "An
Ideal Husband,” merely as “ by the author of ‘ Lady Win-
dermere’s Fan.’ ” “He who taketh from me my good
name robs me of that which not enricheth him, and
makes me poor indeed.” Shakespeare knew the world.
Young Dorrington took to the stage without having
obtained pa and ma’s consent.
A London paper on a new show : “ Mr. Lonnen, as
usual, was far too exuberant in his restless desire ‘to
keep things moving.’ One wishes that in his fooling,
often excellent as it is, he could add the restraint that is
art to the activity which is nature, or natural.” A
restrained Lonnen, The Bulletin fancies, would be an
unspeakably awful thing. The man has no humor ;he is
merely an indefatigable mummer, who, after working
himself into a profuse perspiration at the outset, must
either keep things moving or catoh cold. By the way,
Hinglish critics had always overlooked the ghastly
exuberance and “ painful activity ” of Lonnen until he
was duly reckoned up in these columns. The same man
was praised sky-high in his own country before he went
to Australia.
At the sale of Oscar Wilde’s furniture and effects his
children’s cast-off clothes, toys, and picture-books were
knocked down at extravagant prices to an old lady who
said she “wasn’t going to let the dealers have the chil-
dren’s things if she could help it.” The dealers ulti-
mately gave her three cheers for being “a good old
sort.”
The play-bill of the future will tell us, besides the name
of the prima donna’s dressmaker, who made the grease-
paint for her lovely complexion, who was the johnny that
gave her the diamonds, and what dentist claims credit
for her fascinating smile.
The nice, short title of Henry Arthur Jones’ new
“ original comedy in three acts ” is “ The Triumph of the
Philistines : And how Mr. Jorgon Preserved the Morals of
Market Pewbury under Trying Circumstances.’'
The first circus in Fiji (Cooper and Bailey’s) took away
such a lot of native money that the Government, it is
averred, won’t let another land there. After O. &B. had
gone away the plantation overseers could not get their
laborers to work properly—they were always attempting
trapeze acts on the trees, &c. Once a Tokelau boy
smashed his leg in attempting one of the circus tricks.
The limb was amputated, and the poor fellow had to
“ stump ” about as a sorb of timekeeper. After his first
appearance with the stump on his plantation, all the
laborers rammed their hoes down one leg of their pants
and stumped about after him. Polynesians take a dif-
ferent view of hard luck.
The United Costermongers’ Society is a live institution
in London. There are Coster Nights at the ’alls, and we
hear of a ’all giving a benefit performance in aid of a new
banner for the bloomin’ serciety’s Drury Lane branch.
A slap-up banner, too, make no error about that. A
banner as cost eighty quid, and presents four pictures of
coster life.
< ‘lThe study of music does not conduce to personal
beauty. . • . The skin of the face becomes full and
baggy, especially about the eyes and the throat, filled
out with local accumulations of fat. . . . The nose
becomes characterless and differs essentially from the
clear-cut nose of a man of intellect. The eyes become
prominent and dull, and the mouth loose and undeter-
mined. . . . The hair is unduly stimulated.”—Dr.
Louis Robinson in “ Blackwood.”

A lady recently complimented a country
P.M. on his rumored promotion to a Sydney
Stipendiary Magistracy. “Oh, no!’’ said he,

Lord Rosebery was 48 on the 7th of last
month. It is time for him to marry again, if he
doesn’t want to get into trouble with the Non-
conformist conscience.
All the abominable witnesses at the Oscar
Wilde trial were jingo Tories. The police-court
case opened on the anniversary of Lord Beacons-
field’s death, and the loathsome young men came
along with primroses in their button-holes.
The abomination of long hours still con-
tinues at Darlinghurst, Last week, on the trial
of Kothe, for wounding with intent, the Court sat
till 11 p.m., and Judge, counsel, and jury were all
about as tired and crabby as men could be. It is
really time that this sort of thing was done away
with. The life of a human being is surely as im-
portant an issue as any which can arise in a
“ goods-sold-and-delivered ” case. The reason
assigned for the long hours is consideration for
the jurors, and desire to avoid the necessity of
locking them up. But why lock juries up at all ?
In all cases of misdemeanor, they are allowed to
go home; and some misdemeanors—conspiracy,
for instance—are quite as important as most
felonies.

Society
Vic. Acting-Gov. Madden paid £l2O for
his full major-general’s uniform worn at the
Frankston review, on Queen’s Birthday. A smart
price to pay for an hour’s mock soldiering—
especially as he may never have a chance of wear-
ing it in his present capacity again. After which
grotesque item of bogus glory it is positively
refreshing to read of a dozen or two plain police-
men having replaced the spangled and feathered
military “guard of honor ” at the opening of Ade-
laide Parliament. The democracy’s pattern in
such matters should be President Andrew Jack-
son, who, when he opened Congress, rode up on
his old white horse and hitched it to a post; and,
when he had done his work, unhitched it and rode
away again.

An amalgamated par. showing the working
of Divine Providence, and containing an item of
shipping news which won’t appear in The Bul-
letin’s daily contemptuaries. The captain of a
“foreign” Australo-European liner angrily for-
bade the band to play “God save the Queen”
after a ball on board. The very next day he lost
two blades of his propeller in the Suez Canal.

The New Woman. In Melb. University
there are at the present time 14 women-students
of medicine. Of these, four are in the first year,
one in the second, two in the third, five tn the
fifth, and two are doing “singles.” Also, there
are nine women doctors, graduated from Melb.
University, now in practice, to -wit: Doctors
Lilian Alexander, Marie Castilla, Annie O’Hara,
Elizabeth O’Hara, Helen Sexton, Emily Stone,
Grace Stone, Grace Yale, and Margaret Whyte.
In North America there are over 2000 women
practising medicine, of whom 130 are homeopaths.
The majority are in general practice, but among
the remainder are 40 oculists and aurists, 30
electrotherapeutists, 65 orthopedists, 70 alienists,
70 hospital physicians or surgeons, 95 professors
in medical schools, and 610 specialists in diseases
of women.

* * *
Lady Hopetoun very soon set herself
going after her arrival in London, writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent; and she is
to be s' en almost every day at Egham driving a
team of Shetland ponies. I really believe that
there was no humbug about the “ very young
man’s ” regret at his departure from Victoria, but
I am not sufficiently intimate with the family to
be able to say the same of his wife, who was pro-
bably very glad indeed to get “ home ” again.
“ Montmorganaire Tom Hall and his wife,”
writes a London correspondent to The Bulletin,
“are on a visit to the D’Arcys at Stanmore
before settling down to housekeeping ‘on their
own.’ People are wondering whether Tom will
go in for ‘ sassiety ’ and its consequent expenses,
or simply keep a modest establishment. Rich
men are so scarce now that he would soon become
a society boom if he were to launch out. But
perhaps he may take warning by the fate of
another Melbourne Mount Morgan man, who
spent his cash for a very poor return.”
• # *
A very plump and prominent Adelaide
society dame is just now wishing she had never
been born. She went to “In Town,” and her
dress, which was of bottle-green with
cream guipure and puffed sleeves, or something
like that, was the envy of the circle. All went
well until someone made an extra funny gag.
The lady leaned forward and laughed consumedly,
and just there the bottle-green dress split down
the front and—exit.

• # *
A Spencer-st. station (Melb.) railway
official got into trouble about the departure of
the “Gaiety Girl”co. The amorous man took
off his uniform cap, put on a silk hat, and hurried
to the place where all the farewell kissing was
going on to get his share. He got it—but there
was nothing about kissing passengers in his list of
duties, and he was called upon to explain. A
departmental reprimand was considered sufficient
to meet the case, under the exceptionally tempting
circumstances.

Melb. Herald publishes a cable to the
effect that the Yorks are really coming to Aus-
tralia before the year is out. Ex-Premier Patter-
son, who instigated the visitation, leaves the
responsibility to Turner, who feels like intimating
to their Highnesses that 1895 is his washing-day.
* * *
Varasemai, the Kanaka murderer, lately
executed in Brisbane Gaol, was a “ walk-about. ”
The “walk-about” Polynesian is becoming a
serious danger. He is the terror of women and
shop-girls who meet him of an evening; he
organises intertribal fights and often makes the
streets impassable. His morals are unspeakable,
and he often pays the police-court fines of women
of ill-fame. It is proposed in Bananaland to
limit by law the time he may remain unemployed;
also, to stop Kanakas coming into town on Satur-
day evening and making Sunday a heathen orgie.
By the way, how far is an islander, whose liberty
is thus curtailed, removed from a slave?
• * •
According to Bundaberg Star, when
kanakas sight a bandicoot they all immediately
give chase. When the unlucky marsupial has
been killed by one of their number, the others
each walk up and (in accordance with some island
superstion) gives the defunct animal a blow. The
Star suggests that this circumstance may throw
some light on the fact that when the unfortunate
Macartney was struck down and killed by the
foremost Polynesian assailant the others each
advanced and then gave the body a blow.
* « *
Queensland Education Department now
finds it necessary to allay the fears of scholars
and parents by sending a circular to the schools
testifying, on medical authority, that leprosy is
not contagious. If this be so, why should the
Department take the trouble to seclude a leprous
pupil as they did the other day in Brisbane? By
the way, a new leper, an aboriginal, has just been
discovered on Frazer Island.

Since he has been knighted a certain Mel-
bourneite has taken to going about with enormous
silver buckles on his shoes. Knights are by
“etiquette” permitted to wear buckles. So are
chimney sweeps and scavengers—at least, there is
nothing to prevent them —but they are not pre-
tentious enough to do it. No other Australian
knight, so far as The Bulletin knows, wears
buckles, but then there are not many, even among
“ colonial ” knights, who have the characteristi o
vanity of the lately-ennobbled Victorian.

# * *
A Sydney cancer - patient was being
operated on, the other day, by a surgeon who
saws his bones on the lower flat of a boarding-
house, and one of the boarders played “The Dead
March ” while the man with the cancer took off
his clothes. The surgeon asked the landlady to
lock the piano or kill the player, but she_refused,
saying she didn’t want to lose the only boarder
who paid regularly. The boarder who pays
regularly is generally a ghoul or something unfit
for publication.

One of the results of hard times is the
marriage of old men and young women. Of some
of these maidens we might say what the Athenian
wits said of Arohippa, the courtesan, in her love
for old Sophocles—that their habits are those of
the owls, which love to perch on tombs !

The number of piccaninnies born to
octogenarian “pa’s ” recalls the old Norse law by
which all children horn to fathers over 80 years of
age were declared illegitimate, and could neither
inherit the name nor property of the “old man !”
And yet some foolish writer has concluded that
the Norsemen were not a humorous race !
• # *
A pictorially-scriptural handkerchief is no
longer the correct costume for converted cannibals,
and Melb. “ladies’ missionary societies ” are kept
hard at work in consequence. Nowadays, the
average “ mission bundle ” is made up largely of
alleged pyjamas, embellished with flights of
feminine sartorial fancy denied to her who manu-
factures raiment for hypercritical white kinsmen.
The writer lately inspected a number of pink-print
garments, destined for N.Q. blackfellows, though
in girth of waist and leg suitable rather to a race
of giants. From motives of misplaced economy,
the legs terminated far above the knee, and, no
belts having been provided, the garb was to be
anchored to the wearer by a buttonhole and big
brass button above the hip on either side.

Dear Bulletin, — Melb. waxworks-man
Kreitmayer has an affecting story of wifely devo-
tion. A gentleman had been hanged, and Kreity
took a cast of him, and duly placed the figure,
dressed in his actual cldthes, among the horrors.
One day a little woman visited the show, and
identified the figure. “ It’s ’im ! ” she said, sob-
bing semi-colons; “it’s ’im ! ” The attendant
asked her if she had known the original, and the
little woman answered that the original had been
her husband until his little trouble with the
police. “ However he could a-come to do it, an’
me keepin’ ’im so neat and clean, I don’t know,”
the widow continued. “ An' that’s a fearful dirty
shirt Vs got on now—would you let me put a
clean shirt on ’im once or twice ter week ?” She
was referred to Kreitmayer, secured his consent,
and for months after that the widow visited the
show twice a week and changed the corpse’s shirt.
It was her way of putting flowers on his grave.
(There is some pathos concealed hereabout.) But
after a year the visits stopped. She was “ doing
up’’the shirts of “another bloke.” —The Reck-
less.
* * «
The St. Vincent Hospital ball, at Melb.
Town Hall last week, was just another feather in
the cap of R.C. organisation. Nick Fitzgerald,
M.L.C., Miss Madden, and Miss Beckx, pushed
the sale of tickets with so much fervor that a
matter of 800 citizens faced the strains of Block’s
b uid. It was a gorgeously successful ball, also
t ie comparative scarcity of Scotchmen lent an
appearance of novelty to the surroundings. A
Town Hall hop is too often a function whereat
ordinary beings suffer from a suspicion that they
owe somebody an apology for not having arrived
in a kilt.

According to all accounts, there is in the
chief Australian cities no lack of the vice which
was the downfall of Oscar Wilde. Quite a number
of the votaries of disgusting practice are known
to the police and to the hospital authorities of
Sydney and of Mell ourne.
In a Sydney bigamy case, last week, the
woman whom the accused was alleged to have
bigamously deceived, having to appear as a
witness, described herself as “a married woman
living with her husband.” She had lost no time
in once more chancing the market.
• » •
Sign of the times. One of Australia’s
wealthiest widows now has her three sons-in-law
and all their children living with her.
* * *
During the decade 1884-93, N.S. Wales
scored among its births 3429 cases of twins, 22 of
triplets, and two of quartettes.*
Miss Marguerite Ribbentrop, a di-
vinely tall and imposing soprano now in her
22nd year, has come to Australia, accom-
panied by her father, to sing at the Halle
concerts. Her voice is said to be of beauti-
ful quality, and as a pupil of Mr. Clifford
the young lady understands “sing-
ing as an art.” It is claimed for Miss
Ribbentrop that her rifle-shooting is almost
as fine as her high notes. Mr. Ribbentrop
rules the great Forest Department of India,
2 95 000 men (“ostly dusky) call him
boss.” The daughter of this jungle poten-
tate being an amazonian huntress with
special opportunities for tracking big game,
The Bulletin is prepared to swallow a
statement that the fair soprano has shot one
tiger, five panthers, one wild boar, and three
stags, within the space of three weeks. It
is also suggested that Miss Marguerite has
strangled lots of boa-constrictors unobserved.

The large brass-plate which decorated
the high altar at St. Mary’s (R.C.) Cathedral,
Sydney, has been removed. The legend on
it explained that the altar was presented by
the Riley family.” It was a big advt. in
its time. Its removal may be accounted for
in two ways, the departure of the glory of
the Rileys, or the presentation of the money-
value of the altar to the family.

These things are certain ; (1) That the
public was utterly dissatisfied not only with the
conduct of the Dean trial, but with the evidence on
which the man was found guilty. (2) That public
dissatisfaction at the verdict has been largely in-
creased instead of diminished by the further evi-
dence taken before the Commission. (3) That if
George Reid does not let George Dean out of gaol
George Dean will be the means of helping George
Reid out of office.
When a publican is suspected of keeping adul-
terated grog for sale the licensing-inspector goes
to his hotel, buys a sample of liquor, and divides
same into two portions. These are sealed up in
two bottles, one of which is retained by the publi-
can while the other is kept for official analysis.
Thus is secured the genuineness of the sample
submitted for examination. Now, in the Dean
case, the fluids handed to the analyst could ad-
mittedly have been tampered with by certainly
six or seven, and, possibly, by sixty or seventy,
people. Thus, evidence which would by law have
been utterly valueless as against a publican liable
to a mere <£2o fine, is considered good enough to
condemn and hang a man of good character.
“But,” it will be argued by those who believe
in upholding the Supreme Court, even should a
lunacy- warrant be out for it, “ look at all the
other circumstances.” Circumstantial evidence is
worth nothing unless every link be perfect. In
the Dean case there are twenty rotten links.

Salomons, Q.C., attributed Mrs. Dean's

* * #
The extreme of burlesque was reached by
Salomons, Q.C., in bringing witness after witness
before the Dean Commission to “ prove ” that
“ they never knew ” anything shady about Mrs.
Seymour. The late lamented Frederick Bailey
Deeming could have furnished sheaves of testi-
monials to a similar purport. The testimony of
one reliable citizen who does know something,
&c., &0., outweighs the evidence of a million who
don’t. The affirmative is at least a unit; the
negative is always nought.

Pegg.” To “Pegg,” “Rita” confided her love
for Frank, and owned that she had also written a
“ heart-rendering ” letter to another of her “old
boys.” But the cream of “Rita’s” resurrected
manuscript is in its advice, and in the home-
photograph it gives :
Oh l George, never fall in love, it is very bad for the
constitution. . . . Please bring some kiss-papers, and
give Frank a kiss for me, as I have not had the pleasure
of giving him one for myself.
Had his guardian angel given another George
prophetic warning, it might have whispered—
“Oh! George, never fall into matrimony; it’s
bad for the neck.”
However, there’s the idyllic picture. Thus and
thus did the infantile Mary Seymour make love.
Thus and thus did she prattle to her “ old boys,”
and Cupid shot darts and bore roses with im-
partial willingness. Imagination now cranes her
neck and calls for another photograph or two from
the misty past. Say, Fancy! how did Caroline
Seymour make love in the bad old days of yore ?
How did she ensnare the heart of John Asbury ?
With what lime did Carrie Asbury smear the
twigs that captured Denis Galvin and Tommy
Jones? Give us a page or two of Sycoracian
wooing.

One medical question arising out of the
Dean alleged-poisoning case is : Does skin-pig-
mentation follow on acute arsenical poisoning,
or can it only arise from chronic poisoning?
That is bo say, is it reasonable to anticipate skin-
pigmentation from one or two big isolated doses,
or can you only look for it after “slow” absorption
of many small doses? On an authoritative answer
to this much would depend, as the Crown theory,
generally believed to be wrong, is that pigmenta-
tion followed on one or two almost-fatal doses.

case is the close friendship of one of the women
concerned with a lady nob unconnected with the

* * *
One of the most humorous circumstances
of the Dean Commission was the testimonial given
to Mrs. Seymour’s honesty by her landlord, Mr.
George Withers, the first item in whose catalogue
of his tenant’s virtues was that the charming old
lady “always paid her rent regularly.” Why on
earth didn’t he add, “and never asked for
repairs.”
• • •
Dr. Newmarch, of North Sydney, martyred
himself for science’ sake by taking arsenic
in order that Govt, chemist Hamlet might experi-
ment as to how long the poison was in being
eliminated. But —on no two persons will any
one drug act exactly alike; comparing some pairs,
the differences in effect are wide, even startling.
* * *
A story the Countess Wachtmeister,
now lecturing in Melbourne, tells about H. P,
Blavatsky, illustrates the blind credulity with
which that old lady’s alleged miracles and
“ psychometrisings” have been accepted. Once,
when staying with Blavatsky, she (the Coun-
tess) was asked by her to hand her a book she
had in her trunk. “I have no books with
me,” said the Countess. “ Think again,” said
Blavatsky, “Master says he told you to bring
me a book on the Tarot and the Kabbalah.”
She looked in her trunk and found she had
the book at the bottom of it. When she was
about to hand it over, Blavatsky said, “Turn
bo page 10, and on the sixth line you will
read. . .” and she quoted a passage. The
Countess gives this story as a proof of
“H.P.B.’s” miraculous powers! An ordi-
nary sane person would have at once said,
“Why on earth have you been fumbling in
my trunk?” But Blavatsky told her “Master”
gave the information, and she believed it!

One big Australian contractor imports
nis men direct from “the ould dart,” the
passage-money being stopped out of their
starvation wages. When their time is up,
such of them as can manage it draft off into
the police-force. And yet, would you believe
it, remarks a Bulletin correspondent, prayers
are actually offered up in Ireland for that con-
tractor.

Dear Bulletin, —Parsoning isn’t all rob-
bing Barrow and Tillotson and giving pious old
ladies holy mush in exchange for afternoon tea.
The other day, on the W. Coast of Tasmania,
where one lives in a very atmosphere of rain, hail
and mud, and the towns are “ holes cub out of the
forests,” I met one (a Wesleyan) ending a walk of
30 miles from Lyell in a deluge of rain. He was
covered with mud like the brush of a road-sweeper,
and the letters he had undertaken to deliver,
although in his “inside” shirt, seemed to have
been resting on a lee-shore for a few days—so
sodden with water were they. And he wasn’t
allowed to swear ! — Willow Tea.

Dear Bulletin, —Back from Broad
Arrow the other evening, I struck a
camp, and got a game of 3d. nap. My
partner was a baronet; my opponents, a
German count and a retired English
colonel —all stone-broke, and all delving
for gold in an adjacent claim.— Plutus.

“ Managing ” mothers lose faith in
ball-rooms and tennis-courts, manned |by
arrogant youths, light of pocket as of heel.
In consequence, a feminine rush has set in
towards the Melb. golf-rinks, hitherto the
unmolested haunts of those wary birds,
moneyed widowers and seasoned bache-
lors.

If an “ unqualified person ” set up
an office on the Vic. side of the Murray
and transacted legal business, drew deeds,
&c., for N.S.W. clients only, or vice-versa,
what could either the N.S.W. or Vic.
courts do in the matter? In the one case
the “ person ” would not be within the
territorial jurisdiction of the N.S.W.
court, and could hardly be said to be
doing anything that could be construed
as affecting the Victorian court, and in
the other case, v.v. Has it been tried ?
• * m
The house-surgeon of the Women’s
Hospital (Melb.) is a young lady M.D.
who, in addition to other duties, superin-
tends the medical students, who sit at
her feet and walk her wards to gain
experience in the diseases of women and
children. One item of the prescribed
course is the washing and dressing by
each neophyte of a specified number of
infants, though, here as elsewhere, to
break the record is the ambition of all
soaring spirits.
An English view. On informing his
school-fellows that his father was going to Aus-
tralia, the son of a recently-appointed Vic. Govt,
official was immediately asked by them “ what
the old man had done?” We are still chiefly
associated in the mind of J. Bull with hemp,
chains, cats, and keys.
More true love ! Abrahams, a “ married
Turkish Jew,” of Sydney, fell in love with a single
girl, and when she smiled upon another, promptly
loaded her up with bullets. Then, as a protest
against the monogamic laws of his country and
the unromantic monotony of its statutes, he fatally
shot himself. Father Abraham, of the capacious
bosom, was really in love, and real love always
has blood and bullets in the end of it. The sorb
of love that hangs round weddings isn’t love at all.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The German Club, in Phillip-street(by the w y,
Germans must make either very sober or very
fatalistic clubmen, to have their front steps lead-
ing on to a tram-line ! —English clubmen don’t
take such risks, but choose sequestered urban
spots, with a cab-rank in the middle distance) was
all agog the other evening when Frau Buhrow,
sometimes assisted and sometimes hindered by an
amateur co., gave “The World of Boredom ” for
the benefit of something or somebody with an
unpronounceable Deutscher name, which could only
be rightly rendered by a Nitouche sneeze. “ The
World,” &c., is no fledgling in amateur dramatic
circles, for it has been played several times
already, but still it went like a shot the other
evening before a Continental sorb of audience —
women with flowers hanging in their love-looks at
an utterly impossible (English) angle, and German
naval officers, all gold buttons and spectacles. It
was mostly a full-dress gathering. Indeed, I
wondered at the display of shoulder and skirt,
until somebody arose near the curtain and re-
marked that the evening would end in a dance.
Mine. Buhrow (an actress of many laurels in the
old world) played Suzanne in a most natural way.
In a couple of virginal white frocks and her hair
down, the gifted Vrow rang the changes on every
conceivable human emotion in pretty broken-
English, which manifestly helped the plot. Even
a tiny stage—about the right size for a self-
respecting Punch-and-Judy show—by no means
handsomely arranged (fancy a countess having the
walls of her morning room trimmed with jamias,
like a butcher’s shop at Xmas !) couldn’t spoil
the artistic effect of Frau Buhrow’s finished his-
trionics. She even minimised the fatal effects of
some of her supporters, one of whom (the Coun-
tess) struck angular attitudes with a fan, suggestive
of Japanese stained glass. Another (the Duchess)
acted easily—quite as easily as she forgot her
part; and a pink-robed maiden with her bodice
off one shoulder only (is this a new dodge, I
wonder ?) went through all the wriggling motions
you see in a hooked fish on its way from the deep
to the dingey, every time she had anything to do.
People who really assisted the one-part perform-
ance were Mr. A. J. Kelynack, who looked like
Arthur Lawrence at any rate, bub only had to
stand about intelligently and let the star hug
him ; Mr. G. M'Mahon, as the Professor, got up
like Lardelli ; and Miss Duleie Wilton, as the
frozen English miss-only she shouldn’t have
worn a white gown. Two white frocks on a stage
the size of a pocket-handkerchief made it look like
a confirmation-service. By the way, a new and
neat way of inviting your adored to a tete-k-tete
is introduced into the World of Boredom. “Let
us isolate ourselves to exchange thought 1” said
the Professor. One could imagine oneself break-
ing every one of the Ten Commandments if the
stimulus were always so discreetly given.
Another amusing little nocturnal outbreak last
week was the Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society’s
musical welcome to Signor Hazon, who pays Aus-
tralia the compliment of returning much earlier
than people expected. The Signor has come back
in splendid form, looking fib to wave two batons if
necessary, and expresses himself delighted with
the progress of the S.A.O.S. under Rivers All-
press. Signora Hazon was there, too, in a very
fluffy blue blouse of tulle and satin and a black
silk skirt, and Master Mario Hazon was also a
guest of the evening, which was given over to
music tempered with light refreshments.
The Hazon reunion was really a concert in curl-
papers. It opened with a dress-rehearsal (not a
performance) of a Beethoven bit by the clever
amateur society headed by Mr, Allpress. A new
tenor, Mr. Weir (just now from Maoriland) sang
“ Stars ” very tenderly with delicate pianissimo
most unusual in a man’s voice ; bub the treat of
the evening was a Grieg concerto (Rivers Allpress
and Miss Hyam) which was really high art, and
made itself felt despite the general excellence of the
programme. Said programme was assisted by Miss
Nellie Young, Miss Colborne-Baber, Miss Evelyn,
Miss Sheridan Reed, Dr. Laure and other warb-
lers of note.
Nellie Stewart’s Pickwickian ben. (she gave all
the money away in charity), at Sydney Lyceum
last Wednesday, drew a full house, bub owing to
its being mostly packed by the matinee-
woman, in her big sprouting hat, the applause
was very cool. I think the reason why women
lon’t clap (apart from glove-considerations) is
because they don’t want to applaud the women,
and they aren’t honest enough to barrack for the
men. “Ma Mie Rosette ” was the matinee card,
with Nellie in the white night-dress of perfect
stage innocence, and Mr. Brownlow as the un-
speakable Henri, in the exquisitely-fitting black
tights of rest of the cast being the mix-
ture as before. The opera went splendidly, but
owing, I suppose, to the hen-house state of things
ln -i ii° *^ le golden-haired beneficiary was very
mildly bouqueted—flowers have been very shy all
through the comic-opera season. At the call after
the last curtain Nellie came forward, in the pink
chemise of the last scene, and said, in a small,
fluttering voice that the children of Sydney would
benefit to the extent of ,£2OO, thank you, and
then the fleeting show was o’er.
Looking at the radiant first appearance of the
Gaiety girls (their own abbreviation, the “ Gee-
Gees, ’ is likely to find favor in Australia) at
Sydney Lyceum, even from the bilious point of
view of the morning afterwards, there still appear
j? e ver y f ew flies in the amber. There was lots
of fiitor o in front before the curtain rose, though
I don’t count the big crowd outside the doors as
any great test of public enthusiasm. Inside the
theatre, things were very topsy-turvy, the regular
first-nighters (with the exception of one or two)
being scattered to the four winds out of their
accustomed niches. Nellie Stewart, in a black
silk dress, with the sleeves off the shoulder, and
■ a -v!r onc D here, there, and everywhere, scintillated
in Mr. Musgrove’s stage-box, but it was not what
the society-reporters would call a “ brilliant
assemblage ’’ —the percentage of sadly-plumaged
male birds out for to see “on their very own”
was too great. Black coats sopped up all the
brilliancy, though, as most of the youths were
elaborately button-holed, you must confess they
did their best to look festive, poor things. It was
a very noisy premiere. Before the curtain rose,
the how-de-do among the gods left the Zoo at
a bad second.
The primest portions of the Gaiety wardrobe
are emptied all oyer that majestic, luscious, if
somewhat over-painted edition de luxe of a
woman, Maud Hobson, whose one weakness seems
to be always having something to carry—parasol,
fan, stick, bouquet—she never walks on without
some weapon to cling to. Maud (who is more
spectacular than histrionic, with a singing voice
absurdly top small for her bulk and style) sports
three clinking sets of harness and a tiger, so to
speak, in the shape of a be-ruffled cream-muslin
bathing-gown cut with a great snip up above bub
more generously proportioned below (like the
lady’s calves), but her best “pinney”—the one
she’s had her picture taken in—was worn in the
last act. A white-satin Direotoire gown with
immense sleeves over a petticoat of gold em-
broidery with a foot-flounce of silver-spangled-
white chiffon. Brown fur occurs in the recipe, too
and roses; likewise, a big white hat drowned in
white feathers and modulated with dark-velvet
bows, roses and paste. A posy of yellow flowers
tied with yellow ribbons completed the picture.
Maud certainly is the clothes-horse of the “ Gee
Gees,” and evidently scouts the beauty-unadorned
idea. Her first dress of pale-blue and strawberry-
ribbon brocade with a blue-chiffon bodice of
golden tissue, finished off by a startled-looking-
toque in pink and blue and a pinky parasol, is
almost sure to be duplicated at next Melb. Cup,
and her Riviera toilet (which she dons merely to
take one turn round the stage in) delicately-tinted
brocade once more, with a sort of king’s crown in
stage diamonds in her hat, elicited gasps of horror
from married men in front. Miss Hobson cer-
tainly does open up the sartorial country before
the average Australienne, whose greatest extrava-
gance up to now has been to be “ really tailor-
made ” in a very expensive frock, but one which
she makes last her practically foy ever. Maud’s
dressing is of the butterfly breed—here to-day and
gone to-morrow, though the milliner’s bill re-
maineth.
Blanche Massey dressed to suit her water-name
—all in white both acts. Perhaps the white satin
tea-gown with cascades of old-looking lace was
Blanche’s supreme effort, though such an un-
earthly garment hardly comes within the range of
criticism, it seems to me. Miss Massey in busi-
ness hours has an angelic expression that seems to
demand an apotheosis with wadding seraphs in
the last act, and a voice like Bessie Rignold’s, but
I do wish she wouldn’t hang her head after the
fashion of a dislocated snowdrop. Tiny Decima
Moore (the prima-donna of the co.) contented
herself with a pinkish frock and floral hat (a neat,
but rather suburban-looking rig), a bewitching
blue bathing-suit (the bathing in a “ Gaiety Girl ”
reminds me of the person who hung his clothes on
a gooseberry-bush but never went near the water),
and a pink-and-green carnival frock which was not
meant to be taken seriously. Miss Decima Moore
doesn’t depend on furbelows, for she has a chirrupy
little voice of her own, and her dancing is a treat.
Not like the dreamy twistings of Madge Rossell
in a grasshopper-green gown trimmed with little
bunches of violets—but the livelier sort of heel-
and-toeing which relies for effect on a foam of lace
petticoats and tiny bronze shoes. The Gaiety
dancing is more than lively—it’s furious. The
wild carnival dance, for example, which ends with
Louis Bradfield (he is good-looking, with a crisp
singing voice, and means to become a favorite, I
can see) carrying Decima Moore off the stage in
his arms, is more like a Walpurgis episode than a
modern show.
Pretty, piquant Grace Palotta’s frocks, as the
wicked French maid, wouldn’t amount to much in
an inventory, but their effect was charming, like
her magnetic personality and her fresh, youthful
voice. By the way, Charles Ryley, the stage
masher par excellence of yore (sixmonths is “yore”
on the stage, you know), turned up again in the
“ Gaiety Girl,” and, though the house received
him with warm approval, Miss Palotta’s
“ Sharley ” had not the chance of the Charley of
the past. His voice keeps a good edge on it, but
he sang the trivial, rattling “Tommy Atkins”
song with as much slow, romantic fervor as
though that pipeclay hero was “Queen of My
Heart,” and the complete success of his re-appear-
ance was sadly handicapped by his costume. The
marvellously shapely Ryley, in the ordinary
trousers of every-day life, looks a much more com-
monplace person than the Ryley who flung his
unveiled, classic shanks at the dress circle, as
Guiseppe, in the merry gondoliers. I regret to
hear that “ Sharley ” is struggling against a severe
illness.
I think the mystery of the performance (a verit-
able dramatic souffle which you enjoy best with-
out brain-sauce) is Monkhouse’s silk hat. That
supernaturally shiny erection is made a football
0 f and subjected to most inconsiderate treatment,
and yet it comes out of the ordeal respectable
enough to go to church in. There was a good
show of vegetables on the first night, and one of
the principals (I don’t say which) received a lovely
posy of flowers, as usual, from some faithful
adorer, who ;has regularly be-flowered the
beauteous being in every ’Stralian city. People
who know all about it say it’s quite a romance,
I cannot say whether the dance at Nithsdale
last Friday evening in aid of the piano-fund of the
Children’s Hospital was a financial success or not,
for nobody seemed to have any idea how the
figures would go, but it went off very merrily
socially. Mrs. Lamb, prime mover in the affair,
wore cream satin and lace with good effect, but I
am sorry to say her pretty gown was not one of
many. The idea seemed to be chemist-shop colors,
and if you were pink you were pink to your finger-
nails, and if you were blue you were blue down to
the heels, and no mistake about it. A stout lady
wisely wore black, though perhaps she was over-
garlanded with violets, and another welter-weight
had foolishly stuffed herself into white satin, in
which she looked like an under-study to a feather
bolster. The supper-table was prettily set.
Talking of dances reminds me that the steam-
ship-companies’ ball, with Mr. T, R. Allt on deck, is
to happen at Paddington Town Hall on the 28th
inst., and promises to improve on its former, suc-
cess ; that the Tempe ball committee people are
working like ants in the good cause, and that the
Factory Club ball, under the aegis of Mrs.
Douglas Mackenzie, is also “bucking up,” as
they say in the “ Gaiety Girl.”
Miss Amy Roselle (Mrs. Arthur Dacre) opened
at Sydney Her Majesty’s last Friday evening in
the tragedy “ Esther Sandraz ” to a dress-circle
peppered with society faces. Miss Roselle is not
an actress who relies on the contents of her Sara-
toga to pull her through. In “ Esther Sandraz ”
the dressing is as simple as possible. Barring
the first-act white satin, softened with lace and
gleaming with jewels, a black silk frock,
with a little jet, is all the dressmaker has to do
with it. Miss Roselle is a fine flesh-and-blood
actress, who carries her head well up, like a game
steeplechaser, and is always mistress of the situa-
tion ; judging by the bouquets she received on her
Sydney debut, a good many people approve of her,
too. Hubby Arthur Dacre looks like the Family
Herald idea of a duke. I have never seen a
nearer approach to the long nether supports of
that marvellous hero of fiction than Mr. D.’s legs,
which look as if they would give a good account
of themselves in tights; and his moustache is
certainly the most carefully-trained and well-
behaved rnustivvy at present appearing on the
’Stralian stage. Both Mr. and Mrs. Dacre were
called after each curtain, and it was charming
to see Mr. D. patting Mrs. D.’s hand, and letting
her absorb all the praise, as a model histrionic
husband should.
