Her Majesty’s dramatic co. supports Miss
Roselle; and Miss Agnes Knights, as the wife,
wore a very stylish primrose tea-gown, trimmed
with brown fur, but I do object to the way she
arranges her hair. In Sydney, the other day, a
bookmaker strove to get out of paying a hair-
dressing account of his wife’s, as he did not con-
sider a careful coiffure “one of the necessaries of
life.” Miss Knights evidently shares the bookie’s
opinion, for her wild touzle d la tea-pot might
have been arranged with a stable-fork. The only
fault I found with Miss Kate Bishop’s Mephisto-
phelian walking-dress was that it was worn in the
wrong act, when the surrounding colors were
antagonistic.
Private letters received from Miss “ Heather
Waltz ” Fitz-Stubbs say that the trip ’Ome in the
Ophir was as pretty as a painted voyage. Miss
Fitz-Stubbs was, of course, elected final authority
of the musical programme for the voyage, and
arranged several tip-top concerts, also minstrel
shows, with victorious-cricketer Stoddart as bones
and Brockwell as tambo. On arrival at Tilbury
Docks a purse of sovereigns was presented to Miss
Fitz-Stubbs in token of the gratitude of the pas-
sengers for being so entertained en route, and the
voyage, I am glad to say, generally wound up
with three cheers for the clever Australian com-
poser.
A feature of Sydney Lyceum d.c. is the
chameleonic propensities of some of the d.c.
boutonnieres (masculine). I was sitting near a
youth and maiden the other evening and particu-
larly noticed the little bunch of red berries and
moss which adorned his coat-collar. So when he
returned, after an interval absence, proudly wear-
ing a broken-necked rosebud, which looked as if
somebody had poked his pipe with it, backed up
by a debilitated leaf or so (self-proclaimed spoils
from a barmaid’s peroxidised back hair), I couldn’t
help glancing at his lady companion out of the
tail of my eye to see how she took it. She didn’t
seem to notice —but if I went to the play with my
pet Johnnie and he didn’t cling to his sunflower,
or whatever it was, throughout the evening I
think I would feel distinctly de trap.
While on the subject of coat-flowers, can any-
body tell me if it’s one of the unwritten laws for
a woman to admire a man’s boutonniere, unless
she means she wants it ? Etiquette is such a will-
o’-the-wisp that really one never knows. I was
told, for example, the other day, that the
style of both men uncovering when only
one knows the passing lady is not by any
means universal in Hingland, whence we get
all our most amusing manners, including
turned-up trousers in a drought and the wallaby-
track walk. The up-to-date youth doesn’t bare
his bald —no, I_ mean bold—brow unless he also,
knows the “gairl,” and, I rnusc say, I like the
new arrangement. I have sometimes nodded to
one youth in a company of five strange men, and
the old-fashioned flourish of toppings-off looked as
if I was the daughter of the regiment. By the
way, some men who don’t know their friend’s fair
friend have a shockingly bad trick of turning
round and scrutinising her, as if she were—well,
somebody she shouldn’t be, and they were sizing
her up on her points. And only because the poor
woman has smiled and bowed to their chum, and
they don’t happen to know her! Why, they
couldn’t behave more badly if she had winked at
them.
But this doesn’t answer my question about
admiring a man’s flowers. Every man one does it
to suddenly and mechanically hands ’em over to
one so regularly that it’s almost as paying as say-
ing “ philopoena ’’ first and things like that. So,
until I know, I am nob going to admire any more
sixpenny floral effects. It is evidently a most
misunderstood art.
. I heard the other day a little story concern*
mg a doubtless forgotten episode in the life of
a politician named George. He was at a small
party one evening, and in the palpitating ob-
scurity of a dark verandah-corner he nestled closer
to the elbow of his last waltz-partner, and asked
her, in tender tones, not to “ Mister ” him, but to
call him George. So she did, but spoilt the true
inwardness of the situation by pronouncing it
briskly, “Gorge.” “Oh, don’t say Gorge, so
sharply,” said the politician-to-be—“say it with
sentiment, G-E-O-R-G-B !” ending with a gram-
pus-hke groan. To this day that girl and her
friends always call him GEORGE with a senti-
mental insucking of the breath.
Now that the whole of play-going Australia
seems inclined to wildly and blindly worship a
bevy of beauteous Thespiennes who have come
amongst us_ like falling stars, I want to rise and
remind the johnnies (who in the new frenzy may
be inclined to think every woman in tights an
angel unfledged) that three lovely damsels whom
all Australia worshipped not seven years ago,
were turned out of their hotel in one Australian
city for creating a disturbance. It wasn’t a
champagne-and-oyster disturbance, either, but a
vulgar, unpoetic orgy based on sordid victuals—
including pickles. The actress is almost always
disenchanting off the stage, and a cure for the
love-stricken Johnnie is for him to steadfastly
remember that the adorable vision is very fond of
onions and drinks quarts of beer at supper.
From W. H. Paling and Co. I have received a
copy of the “Katherine” waltz, based on three
airs from Signor Lardelli’s opera of that name. It
strikes me that the “ Katherine ” (which is pretty
and danceable) is just a little too previous to be a
great success yet awhile. There is no true in-
wardness in a waltz on an opera nobody as yet
whistles ; but, doubtless, when the opera is known
the waltz will have its little innings.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
Melb. Bankers’ Magazine is now chiefly
scissors and paste. The June number contains
three Bulletin cartoons mendaciously (for the
plea of ignorance cannot possibly hold) attributed
to the Review of Reviews.
Romano’s restaurant in the Strand,
London, lately destroyed by fire, has been rebuilt,
and re-opened with a superb inaugural dinner.
Australians visiting London should take note of
this as being one of the few places in London
where something like good cookery can be had.
That veteran host, Tom Asche, late of the
old Sydney Royal, and formerly of the Union
Club, Melbourne, has now taken the Imperial
Hotel, Wynyard Square, Sydney, which has been
renovated and re-furnished throughout, and now
takes family rank as one of the healthiest, home-
liest, handsomest hostelries in Sydney.
Half-a-dozen poor aborigines at Fern-
mount (N.S.W.) were fatally poisoned through
drinking a presentation bottle of what was thought
to be “overproof rum.” The presenter, of the
potheenic name of Kelly, declared that he believed
it to be that beverage; “ Indade, Oi tuk a shwig
raesilf, an’ Oi shpose that’s what Oi’m sufferin’
from now.” Faith, no. The Kelly is suffering
from an “ Irish constitution ” :
Countess Wachtmeister, widow of a
Swedish ambassador, who is just now rounding
up the meek and trusty Blavatskyites of Melb.,
and of whom an excellent photo, by Talma, is
here printed, is an intensely serious person, with
a hard and smileless visage, and hair brushed
vigorously back from a wide expanse of brow.
She is reputed to have been one of the gayest of
persons at European courts, in her halcyon days.
Now she appears to have spent her life in
prayer and fasting. She makes up as well as
Annie Besant, though plumper of figure than
that lady. She dons Annie’s grey tea-gown
garb, clasps her hands midships, Annie-wise,
and follows the Annie oratorical plan of enun-
ciating nearly every syllable with a snap. The
similarity ends there. Annie Besant is an
easy person to imitate—if one has her wonder-
ful mind, her personal magnetism, and her
matchless gift of the gab, which Countess Wacht-
meister hasn’t, and never will have with any
amount of trying.
Six begging niggers hither come,
And rum is heaven, and heaven is rum,
And virtue is a vice.
And, lo 1 they get from generous hand
A bottle of mysterious brand,
And drink it in a trice.
The drug was heaven, though ’twasn’t rum,
It sent ’em all to Kingdom Come—
Perpetual Paradise !

Higinbotham’s youngest daughter has just
been married in London.
Murderer Bertrand is now residing in
Paris under an assumed name.
Melb. Age describes Freetrader Murray
Smith as the “ grizzled hero of a lost cause.”
W. M. Clark, ex-M.L.A. for Footscray
(Vic.), is now a reporter on Coolgardie Miner.
Melba’s eldest son is now 11 years old.
She was married at 17, and has for six years been
singing professionally.
David Syme employs over 40 men on his
dairy farm at Woori Yallock(Vio.). He turns all
the milk produced into cheese.
Adelaide Hunt Club report : “We missed
Mr. Glynn and Modesty.” Humph ! Glynn had
that same* day been elected to Parliament.
Chief Secretary Gordon (S.A.) will be 45
on Wednesday, June 26, and Baronet Bryan
O’Loghlen will be 67 on Thursday, June 27.
Veteran George Coppin made his first
appearance in Melbourne at the old Queen’s
Theatre just 50 years ago on Friday, June 21.
Cock, M.P. (S.A.), arrived with the first
Governor in H.M.S. Buffalo on Dec. 28, 1836,
and was present at the proclamation of the pro-
vince.
One of the recent released N.S.W. ex-
banker-prisoners was “doing solitary ” for a breach
of prison discipline when the “open sesame”
arrived.
Dr. Campbell, of Yass, the oldest N.S.W.
medical practitioner outside Sydney, has been
killed by a fall from his buggy, while journeying
to visit a patient. Aged 80 years.
Percy Spence painted several pictures on
his way to England, and sold them at excellent
prices. He also received two good commissions
for paintings, and is full of hope for the future.
The congregation of barrister Field, the
enthusiastic Sydney amateur evangelist, are
allowed to smoke. When other preachers grant
the same privilege they will draw larger audiences.
Canon Potter (High Church), of Melb.,
evidently anticipates the union of the English and
R.C. churches. Already he has ordered a supply
of Church of England prayer-books to be printed
in Latin.
The Revd. Wheeler, who is locum tenens
for Parson Haweis at his church during the ad-
vertising pastor’s absence from London, is married
(fourth time for the lady) to a sister of Lord
Augustus Loftus.

Some years ago
Maloney, M.L.A., the
little Radical Vic.
medico, met Davitt in
Michael’s native Ros-
common. There was a
row on, and Maloney
was in the thick thereof.
Davitt’s bhoys were
smaller in numbers than
the others, and made a
masterly retreat under
a shower of stones.
Little Maloney told
Miohael he hadn’t come
all the way from Mel-
bourne to be stoned by
a mob of bailiffs and
bog-trotters. “Here’s
ammunition,” sez the
little man, pointing to
21b. granite boulders
that smiled on the road-
side. But Davitt knew
better. He had got 15
years p.s. as a Fenian
arms-agent, and wasn’t
going to play the school-
boy and be run in for a
mere breach of the
peace, so he got hold of
Maloney from Mel-
bourne under his only
arm and took him to
the nearest priest’s
house, where he was
safely chained to a de-
canter while the storm
blew over.

Carington will be presented “on the birth of an
heir,” and altogether the arrival of young Mr.
Smith has assumed the dimensions of a State
Affair of Importance.
David Syme’s youngest son is now on the
reporting staff of the Age, and has just begun to
try his hand in the Parliamentary gallery. Like
the monarchy, the post of boss-bully of Victorian
Liberalism is to be hereditary.
An Australian Judge addicted to spiritual-
ism was, in his barristerial days, no less imbued
with the craze for the quasi-marvellous. He has
never forgiven the learned counsel who so success-
fully conspired to hoax him with the usual grue-
some figure constructed per aid of sheets, flour
and phosphorus.
J. F. Hogan is alleged by Melb. Herald
(in a leading article) to have said that it, the
Herald, is “ the smartest paper in the world.”
It ought to be the smartest paper in the world,
considering that Hogan is a paid London con-
tributor to its columns.
A nun in the convent at Abbotsford
(Melb.), which is within 50 yards of the tramline,
last week saw a tramcar for the first time in her
life, though she has been in the convent for nearly
20 years. During all that time she had never
been outside the building.
The famous Parkes £lO,OOO testimonial
of a few years ago is commonly believed to have
been invested in bank shares at boom prices; it
now yields about 2 per cent, interest and would
realise about £4OOO if sold out. Parkes’s financial
ill-luck is something awful.
It was solicitor “ Jim ” Moloney, of Mel-
bourne, who killed Rufus Dawes, in Marcus
Clarke’s novel. The author originally made Dawes
return to England, and “live happily” in the
good, old orthodox way ; but Moloney fortunately
persuaded Clarke to alter this.
Gov. Brassey, an insatiable reader, marks
in the margin of every volume the most striking
passages. Then he has the marked portions type-
written on uniform-sized paper, which extracts
are classified and pigeon-holed for ready reference.
He has pursued this plan all his life.
The retirement of “Nosey Bob” having
been rumored lately, over 100 candidates made
application for the post of N.S.W. executioner.
Among them was a Chinaman, who thoughtfully
forwarded his naturalisation-paper, to show that
he was allee same as other Blitish gentlemen.
Henry Irving, Walter Besant, and that
crawlsome Lewis Morris, are reckoned by Queen
Victoria worthy only of the K. 8., the lowest
order of knighthood extant. For some years
prior to the Vic. land-boom era the K.B. had lain
torpid, and it was apparently resurrected by Gov.
Loch as a means of ennobbling Benjamin Ben-
jamin without quite placing that obese potentate
on a par with the K.’sO.M.G. of the province.
What a satire on the whole system of titles:
Henry Irving, Walter Besant, and Bingy Bingy !
It was an open secret amongst Gov.
William Robinson’s Melb. friends that he would
not return to Westralia, if he could help it. He
had received a hint from the local Mrs. Grundy
that he was not tq return without bis wife, and he
himself knew that this was easier promised than
performed. Then he went to London, hungering
to get his opera-play produced. It turned out
that “Predatoros” bore a striking likeness to
“The Chieftain,” running at the Savoy, and so
he has to wait. Better be an Agent-General
within reach of theatre managers than a Governor
without.

IT MUST HAVE BEEN THE LOBSTER.
“ A lone Victorian lady, who informed the police that a gentlemanly
young man, supposed to be a lunatic, broke into her house at midnight and
threatened to take her life unless she married him , has now admitted that
the alleged incident never happened and was merely a nightmare. A craw-
fish-supper, indulged in on the previous evening, is supposed to be the cause
of all the trouble.” — NEWS ITEM.
Miss ONTHESHELF (of St. Kilda): “ Kindly supply me with a crawfish,
and I think you may give me the largest you've got."
Vic. Chief Justice Madden’s love of horse-
flesh is no new trait in his character. When a
boy of 10 he resided in Moonee Ponds (Vic.), and,
according to a compact made between him and
one Hatton, a local batcher, young Madden used
to turn up at the latter’s stable every night and
put in an hour or so grooming the meat-man’s
mokes, receiving as compensation the loan of the
best nag in the stalls each Saturday and Sunday
afternoon. Hatton is living yet; and the weather-
board shop, at the rear of which the C.J.
“ whooshed ” his smoking prads, still stands in the
football township.
Lady Carington had been 17 years married
before she added a son and heir to her long family
of girls. Queen Victoria is said to have dropped a
silent tear upon Oarington’s gold lace when she
congratulated him on the arrival of the baby boy.
Such little matters as a direct descendant in the
malelineof an ennobbled Smith affect HerGraoious
deeply.

Apropos Salmon Wilson, just deceased. An
anecdote may be revived of him and M'Ooll, the
luckless projector of theGoulburn irrigation scheme,
the prophet of all the Ghaffeys and Milduras. At
Melb. White Hart Hotel, Mac. tried to persuade
Salmon. “Where will the people come from?”
asked Salmon. “ Where do the flies come from? ”
asked M‘Coll, dropping a pinch of sugar on the
table.
The brief of Salomons, Q.C., before the
Dean Commission was marked at 50 guineas a
day. Pilcher, Q.C., was guaranteed 15 guineas a
day—it is possible he may have received more—
although he ordinarily receives fees as high as
those of his perky opponent. Mr. Pilcher in-
timated to the Dean defence committee that he
accepted the brief more because of personal sym-
pathy than anything else.
Salmon Wilson’s death in England sur-
prised many people, who thought he was in Aus-
tralia. That monstrous white elephant, Wilson
Hall, a monument of pride, vanity, and folly, at
Melb. University, remains as his memorial of the
.£30,000 he paid for his title. The ridiculous fea-
ture of the place is that it is a useless appendage
to the tumble-down buildings, where the money
would have been really useful.
Dear Bulletin, —I am tired of hearing
people and seeing papers expatiate upon Parkes’
wonderful staying powers, and pointing him out
at the adult prodigy of our time. I am acquainted
with a man of 76 who worked amongst the dyna-
mite fumes in deep mines at Clunes for half a life-
time, and is now toiling in a wet shaft in Ballarat
East for 7s. fid. a shift, and he is not, so far as I
can gather, generally looked upon as a pheno-
menon or a fit subject for a State pension.—S.S.
The late Prof. Hearn (Melb.) had a unique
way of emphasising the salient points in his
lecture on law. His method was to make his class
illustrate the law in their own persons. For
example, to exemplify the law of maintenance, he
would pick out the youngest and most blushful
male before him—a theological student, if possible
—and in the calmest manner describe him as a
deep-dyed Lothario with interesting batches of
twins and triplets scattered over the face of a
smiling country, and ask him what he intended to
do when the cases came on. Again, turning to the
rowdiest undergrad. in the class, he would re-
mark : “Now, Robinson, suppose you were chap-
lain to a building society and Wilkinson (pro-
bably a member of the Christian Alliance) was a
professional burglar, and he stole your watch
from your pocket while you were having a quiet
five rounds with Joe Goddard, what remedy
would you have ? ” It was all done very good-
naturedly ; but the Prof, once made an ugly slip
—in supposing something about Miss Jones’s new
set of misfit teeth and a litigious dentist. The
lady, whose teeth happened to be bad, walked out
in high dudgeon.
Prior Vaughan’s determination to forego
the use of the plate in his dealings with the flock
he proposes to gather under the designation of the
“ Heathen at Home,” marks the strongest feature
of difference between his scheme and that of
General Booth. Last year Booth raised £153,000,
£139,000 of which he himself estimates as the
voluntary subscriptions of the parties to whom he
preached.
The oldest slab in the first Melb. cemetery
is inscribed “In memory of Frederick William,
son of Skene Craig, merchant, Melbourne, died
Marfch, 1837, aged 18 months.” The most expen-
sive tomb thus seeks, to perpetuate the fame apd
the virtues of a long-forgotten magnate:— In
memory of the Hon. James Horatio Nelson Cassell,
Collector of Customs, member of the Executive
and Legislative Councils of Victoria, this monu-
ment has been erected by his fellow-colonists in
acknowledgment of eminent public servicearen-

Of the men who made fortunes out of
Broken Hill, J. S. Reid became connected with
the field through establishing a little paper at
Silverton ; Walter Sully kept a small store at the
same place; the Horns were taken there Ffy a
bank-managership; the Wilsons, formerly con-
nected with S.A. mines, went first to Day Dream;
George M'Culloch was manager of Mt. Gr|ms
station ; Rasp and Charley were workmen on the
station ; the Brodribbs owned Toolamacca statfbn,
close handy ; Harvey Patterson had Burta sta-
tion ; and several other big men got in early
through their connection with far-western sqUat-
tages. Those who didn’t, are now mostly “ broke.”
Baron von Mueller, Australia’s greatest
and least pretentious scientist, and one whbse
fame reaches to the four corners of the world,
will celebrate his 70th anniversary on Sun-
day, June 30th, and the occasion will surely prove
that the good old man has “honor, kfve,
obedience, troops of friends.” In fact, the Baron
has simply honors heaped upon him. He is »
doctor of medicine and philosophy, and for his
botanical work has received three of the highest
scientific honors in the world—the Fellowship'of
the Royal Society of London and the memberships
of the Academy of Science of Berlin and of the
Institute of France. He was also created an
hereditary baron by the King of Wiirternburg, is
a chevalier of the French Legion of Honor, atfd
has been dabbed knight by the raonarohs of
Austria, Baden, Bavaria, Denmark, Hawaii,
Holland, Italy, Portugal, Prussia, Saxe-Coburg-
Gotha, Spain, and Weimar. He has been Vic.
Government Botanist and Director of the Botani-
cal Museum since ’52, and held in addition from
’57 to ’73 the post of Director of Melb. BotaniCSQ
Gardens. Likewise, he can write after his naff.e
F.L.S., F.G.S., F.G.S., F.R.G.S., and can Use
up, without difficulty, a whole fount of capitals
after that in the effort to indicate that he is also a
member —either honorary, corresponding, or ordi-
nary—of nearly 100 other societies scattered over
all six continents. Summarised, Ferdinand von
Mueller is about equal to one Baron, one Knigfit
Commander, one Chevalier, twelve Knights, tivo
Doctors, seven Fellows, and about 100 members—
i.e., he is equal to 124 other more or less distin-
guished people. His published-works and essays
are every bit as numerous as the initials he can
tack on to his name.

(< Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “ a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland, where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe ” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing tor an aspir-
ing comique, pro. or amateur.’*

amendment would be negatived/
While Mr. Hancock was considering the
misfortunes of the lady teachers, he (Mr..
Trenwith) was considering the misfortunes
of unity.
Mr. WINTER.-Hear, hear. That is the,
labour standpoint.
Mr. G. TURNER said he had explained
when bringing forward the bill that it meant
the saving of about £20,000 a year. Last
session the Government was blamed for pot
ting taxation on the peopie instead of re-
trenching, and now that it was bringing
forward retrenchment proposals it was being
condemned. (Criesof “No.”) The Govern-
ment had been challenged with having de-
stroyed the defence force because it had re-
duced its strength so as to economise. It
had been accused of dispensing with the
brains of the pnblic service and keeping the
bone and muscle ; of having trampled upon
the small daisies and alldwed the tall poppies
to remain uncut; and of having starved tbd
►neglected children, and of having starved
the public servants by dispensing with fires*.
Its proposition to reduce the fees to jurors
had been opposed, and now because it wished
to* save money by allowing office re to retire on
pensions from the overmanned public se^viee,
’ hon. members who had clamoured last
session for jeprepcbmept were complaining.

WOLFE’S
SCHNAPPS.
POLITICAL
POINTS
The Argus : “The Premier’s Premier—
Trenwith.”
The S.A. Civil Service is to be further
“out.” Even salaries up to £l5O will, it is said
be attacked.
Chinamen are now refused relief work at
the Centennial Park, Sydney, on account of the
objections of the negro and white workmen. The
negroes claim to be “ Englishmen.”
“The misfortune of Parkes’ platforms on
all occasions has been that there has never been
any room on any of them but for one man—and
that was Parkes.” — Premier Reid.
Vie. Labor-party is divided into the goats
who follow Prendergast, and the sheep whose
shepherd is Trenwith. This gives the division-lists
a queer aspect from the democratic standpoint.
Vic. Postmaster-General has decided to
close all post-offices where the average business is
not more than three letters a day. Forty offices,
costing ,£lOOO per annum, will be thus abolished.
Up to date, £150,000 has been received
on account of the Vie. Income-tax—about seven-
eighths of the year’s total. Evidently there is still
money in Victoria somewhere —buried in the back-
yard, probably.
N.S. Wales is governed by an illicitly-
distilled blend of Government by Contradiction,
Government by Coalition, Government by Com-
mission, Government by Caucus, and Govern-
ment by Inspectors.
There are now 62 members of N.S.W.
Legislative Council, almost all nominees of H.
Parkes and G. R. Dibbs. Premier Reid gets
credit for two, the juniors of the House, Dr.
Garran and J. H. Want.
The S.A. National Ass is trying to estab-
lish locally women’s political societies on the lines
of the English Primrose League. Before adult
suffrage became law, Tory politicians said only
the “lower ordahs ” would avail themselves of it.
On the Lower Burdekin plantations colored
labor is rampant —on one plantation in particular.
Here the Jap. can be seen in all his glory. A
railway is being built —by Japs. ; sleepers are
being out —by Japs. ; plates are being laid—by
Japs. ; even in the fitting-shop operations are
being carried out —by Japs. White men often
sleep out in the cow-yard and adjourn when it
rains to the stables. The Jap. is intelligent,
suave and far more adaptable and docile than the
Chinese. He looks upon Queensland as his happy
hunting-ground, and is quietly training himself to
enter any and every sphere at present occupied by
European labor.
A boot controversy is raging in N.S.W.
Under Protection the imports of boots decreased
by nearly two-thirds, but the quantity of boots
locally manufactured remained practically sta-
tionary. Therefore, it seems that during the
Protective period there was such an improvement
in the quality of the locally-made boot that the
nation got through with a smaller supply ; or else
it got so poor that it had to do with less boots ;
or there was an alarming increase in the number
of one-legged men ; or people are giving up boots;
or the statistics are wrong—or something. The
Bulletin, after much hard thinking, believes it is
something.
Premier Reid believes in compensating the
publican, if his trade is destroyed by Act of Par-
liament, because said trade has grown up with
the permission and assistance of the State, and
for the State to wipe it out now would be confisca-
tion. But he doesn’t believe in compensating the
sugar-growers, although their industry was also
created by the State, whioh industry he now pro-
poses to destroy, together with the capital invested
in it. And the Dibbs-Lyne crowd, when it gets in
again, proposes to badly damage the importing
business, also without compensation. Why should
Bung be compensated if these other people are
only to be confiscated ?
Dear Bulletin, —Re Darley at £230 a
week. Appointed C.-J. under provisions of N.S.W.
Charter of Justine. Under Sec. 7 thereof no C.-J.
is capable of taking any other office or place of
profit or emolument on pain of immediate de facto
voidance of C.-J.-ship and forfeiture of salary.
Appointed Lieut.-Gov. by Royal mandate (only).
Statutes only repealed by statutes. Query ; Did
not Darley on his first aeceptanoe of pay forfeit
his prior office ? Further : What about all subse-
quent legal proceedings before him? Further:
Would the Supreme Court hold a Governorship at
£7OOO per an. not an office or place of profit or
emolument? I want to know.— Tom Smith.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir, —The note of
triumphal jubilation by the Sydney morning
papers, on what they are pleased to term the Free-
trade victory, is net justified by the facts. The
victory is to Land-Nationalisation, not Freetrade.
Take away the plank of unimproved-land-value
tax from the Govt, platform and Freetrade would
be nowhere. The other side had just the same
chance and threw it overboard to propitiate vested
interests. This is Reid’s chance, but let him hesi-
tate to push the reform right through to the bitter
end and be is dead as Julius Cajsar. Even now
in the Freetrade ranks doubting conspirators are
watching and the distrustful Labor guerillas are
ready to strike.— Brutus.
The Parkes-Dibbs friendship is understood
to be at an end, and the N.S.W. two-headed
infant has resolved into his original component
parts, which parts regard each other with their
old hostile expression. Two-headed infants seldom
Tivs long.

July 1 is “Dominion Day” in Canada,
and “Separation Day” in Victoria. Thus an
Australian province, which professes to be the
home of Federal sentiment, still rejoices on the
anniversary of the day on which it won its'lnde-
pendence from one of the provinces with which it
now seeks alliance!
“If Australian Federation is to be judged
by the public actions and utterances of its pro-
fessed advocates, there is a danger that men will
come to regard the movement as a deliberately-
planned conspiracy for defeating the honest desires
of the people of Australia for industrial and social
reforms.”— Frank Cotton.
A very loyal Melb. paper admits that
knighthoods have never advanced Australasian
politicians. To be sure :
The politician smug who chases
The smiles of people in high places,
Is aptly made a knight at last
To indicate his day is past.
There are in N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
about 14 men who, so far as the world knows,
could afford to do without their official .£3OO a
year. There are at least 40 to whom the with-
drawal of pay would mean something like starva-
tion. Free and unfettered choice is impossible to
the electors without Payment of Members.
This is a Parkesian prophecy fulfilled.

Many Lives Lost.
The Bulletin does not believe that Aus-
tralia will be fool enough to send Judges to repre-
sent her on the British Privy Council. But if
she does it will surely only be on the ground that
England originally dumped into our country the
people of whom it wished to get rid, and that
the time has now arrived for Australia to return
the compliment.
The least death-rate in Australasia for the
past decade is debited to Maoriland. Then, in
ascending order, come South Australia, New
South Wales, Tasmania, Victoria, Queensland,
Western Australia. The highest birth-rate is
credited to Q’sland. Then, in descending order,
come N.S. Wales, Westralia, South Australia,
Tasmania, Victoria, Maoriland.

elected unopposed to the N.S.W. Assembly.
Meanwhile Daddy Parkes is roaring that the
country owes him boundless gratitude.—
The family arms have got a slew,
The family mottoes read awry,
For Varney’s now is “ I O U,”
And Henry’s sign is “ U O I.”

One of the benefits accruing from the
establishment »f the Maoriland State Loan Depart-
ment, apart from the lowering of interest, is the
great reduction in law-costs. The ordinary legal
expenses attached to a private mortgage of £2500,
if the land is under the old Act, are £45 11s.,
whereas the State does the business for £7 12s.
If the property mortgaged is under the Land
Transfer Act, the private charges are £39185., but
the Loan Department only exacts £4 7s. 6d.
State competition has already brought down
interest and it will also necessarily bring down
law charges, therefore the legal brigade gnashes
its best Sunday teeth at the department, and
would gladly consign it to the Worm that is not
quenched, or thereabouts.
The Assembly election for N. Adelaide
(S.A.) was the biggest political jumble locally
known. Paddy Glynn (elected) was the nominee
of the Ass, but was also supported by the majority
of the Labor-party, whilst Wadey, although an
Ass man, was ignored by that body. Had the
workers nominated a candidate they could have
easily carried the seat, but S.A. Labor-party is
sadly disunited just now.

When Balak sent for Balaam to curse the
Jews, Balaam found it difficult, not being ac-
quainted with the nation. Balak burnt 21 oxen,
and did his best to advertise Balaam, but Balaam
wouldn’t curse. This is the parable of Michael
Davitt and the Vic. Free-raiders. Max Hirsch
suit for Davitt to curse Protection, and the Single-
tax League made oxen of themselves, and other
Free-raiders forged an alleged saying of Davitt’s
and advertised it on the railway-stations—so
Davitt cursed them and not Protection. And
Balak’s anger was kindled against Balaam.
The expenditure on strong urink in Maori -
land for ’94 was £2 17s. 2d. per inhabitant. Ten
years previously it was«.£4 ss. per inhabitant, and
15 years back (in ’79) it is alleged to have been as
high as £6 10s. Considering this excellent result,
the Prohibitionists should leave well alone. The
nation is evidently leaving off drink of its own
accord, and where things are improving much
faster than anyone has reason to expect, it is mere
foolishness to begin shoving behind to make them
go faster still.
One consideration which doubtless hurried
the Seddon Government into its seizure of the un-
finished Midland Railway of Maoriland was the
necessity of finding land for settlement. Under
the fatuous contract which Stout and Vogel made
with the devastating English capitalist, all the
State lands remaining in Canterbury, Westland,
and Nelson provinces (6,000,000 acres or more)
were locked up against settlement until the line
was finished, and the railway co. had selected its
land-grant out of them. This lock-up has lasted
for seven years, and seemed likely to last for
another twenty, till at last the agriculturist who
wanted soil grew tired of waiting, and urged
>,<ldon into activity. Even now nothing can be
done with the land until at least 15 months’ notice
has bpim given to the defaulting co.
Melb. Single-taxers are covering the local
hoardings with huge "garish posters, inscribed :
“Michael Davitt says, ‘No intelligent working
man can be a Protectionist.’ ” If it comes to
quotes and advts., why don’t the Protectionists
garishly advertise Carlyle’s remark, “A Free-
trader is another way of saying a darned fool ”
or words to that effect.
Higgins, M.F. (one of the leaders of the
Equity Bar), takes a firm stand in defence of the
Vic. tariff, which is gall and wormwood to the
local Free-raiders. The A RGCS gets into one more
unholy dilemma by declaring in general terms
that Victoria cried aloud at last election for tariff
reform, and in particular terms that Higgins took
his stand against the alleged tariff reform so as to
secure a seat.
Victoria is at present in
possession of a tariff which has
been added to by Freetraders,
Fat men, and Conservatives, as
well as by democratic and un-
democratic Protectionists —and
the earth is asked to wonder
because it is not the most per-
fect tariff possible. The Argus
has invented a rail for politi-
cians to sit on —25 per cent,
duties—and sundry very feeble
bores take up a silly position
on this fence, without troubling
themselves as to its security.
There can be no compromise
between Protection and Free-
trade. There is a large gulf
fixed between, yet some men try
to sit on that gulf. If Foreign-
trade is desirable let it be free—
if it is desirable for certain
articles, let it be free for those
articles. If, on the other hand,
the country wants to “ protect ”
any industry—let the protection
be whole-hearted and effective.
It may extend the horizon
of some N.S.W, income-tax
orator, at the present juncture,
to be informed that, throughout
the whole history of her Re-
publics, France has rejected
income-tax proposals. The last
proposal—about a year ago—
was thrown out by a majority
of 257 to 236. in the Chamber
of Deputies. The Swiss Republic
condemns the taXj and has re-
jected it whenever proposed.
Britain, Prussia, Sweden and
Norway approve of the tax, and
collect it annually, some of them
changing the amount per £ every
year, while others retain the
same figure for decades. Aus-
tria and Denmark impose the
tax some years, and other years
reject it, the inference from all
facts being that monarchical
countries are less opposed to the
tax than republican ones.
One-adult-one-vote was first adopted in
the Southern Hemisphere 40 years ago when Guu
Denison, who served both in Tasmania and N.S.
Wales, applied the theory to the little Settlement-,
of Norfolk Island. That Gov. believed the theory
the soundest for young countries and prophesied
that it would yet prevail in every section of the
Antipodes ; but, notwithstanding the uniform
success of the Norfolk Island experiment over so
many years, half of Australia still trembles, or
pretends to tremble, when the innovation is men-
tioned.
A tax of a penny in the shilling upon
tickets of admission to “places of public amuse-
ment ” is seriously suggested by Melb. journals.
If they include theatres among taxable places of
amusement, the writers are evidently scribbling in
the dark. Williamson and Musgrove, The Bul-
letin fancies, would prefer to take a halfpenny in
the shilling on gross receipts as their share of the
profits these times, and let the Government have
the balance of profit or loss. Outdoor sport-
shows—football and cricket matches, &c. —are fair
marks for taxation, inasmuch as the promoters are
not playing up a lot of capital in a very risky busi-
ness. Racing, too, might be easily taxed through
the totalisator, if not at the gates. But a levy
upon theatre takings would never do, and suppos-
ing that bookers of seats in advance were charged
per cent, extra, the gain to the public exchequer
wouldn’t be worth talking about.
Dear Bulletin, —Nothing so illustrates
the inanity of the Freetrader as the proposed State
project of finding a market in England for N.S.W.
barley. It iss worse than sending coal to New-
castle, A cable last week intimated that Chaplin
was endeavoring to impose a tariff in the interests
of the ruined British farmer. Minister Sydney
Smith talks of the superior N.S. W. grain. Bosh !
The repeal of the malt-duty in 1880, like the
abolition of the N.S.W. tea-duty, only stimu-
lated the importation of inferior rubbish. Then
while N.S.W. is vainly seeking a market for
malting barley, all her brewers are using imported
malt, principally Victorian. Let the N.S.W.
excise on beer be reduced and a corresponding
impost placed on imported barley, and the home
brewers will immediately consume as much barley
as the farmers can grow. But while they can im-
port they will not go to the expense of erecting
kilns, cisterns, floors, &c. Shifting the tax from
the finished beer to the raw material cannot affect
the poor consumer, and thus obviates one Free-
raid “lament.” —V.


MINES REPORTED ON OR SUPERVISED
—BY—
WILLIAM NICHOLAS, PM., Lon.,
Consulting Mining Engineer,
Late Lecturer on Mining in the University of Melbourne •
Lecturer on Geology as applied to Mining, Bendigo School
of Mines; of the Mining and Geological Department of
Victoria, etc. Author of “The Golden Quartz Reefs of
Australia,” “ Coolgardie Goldfields,” etc. Address—
Coolgardie, Wbstbrn Australia.
The Wild-Cat Column.
New Plymouth (M.L.) Harbor Board, in a time
of temporary aberration, borrowed a large sum in London
at 6 per cent. After paying up honorably for a number
of years it fell into difficulties, and, as the bondholders
wouldn’t reduce their atrocious rate of interest, New
Plymouth defaulted. It now pays about 4 per cent, a
remarkably good return, and owes the rest. The bond-
holders, therefore, stuck up Treas. Ward on the sub-
ject, and demanded that the State should guarantee their
6 per cent, and pay up all arrears, failing which they
would do their best to shut out M.L. Govt, stocks from
the London market, and would wreck the new loan, if pos-
sible. Ward's reply was, practically, that the M.L.
Treasury was in no way responsible for New Plymouth’s
purely local borrowings ; that the bondholders knew that
when they lent the money, for no State-guaranteed loan
would carry such a rate as 6 per cent.; that their demand
for a State guarantee and 6 per cent, was highway rob-
bery ; that their threat to wreck the M.L. loan unless the
State bought them off by paying a liability in which it
wasn’t concerned was blackmail, pure and simple; and
that he would see the New Plymouth bondholders dead
first. Which solid, but unpleasant, truths broke up the
negotiation.
* * * *
In 1891— the last year for which complete statis-
tics are available—foreign fire and marine insurance com-*
panics, mostly British, took £70,000 out of N.S. Wales in
net profits, and sent it “Home” to pay divs. to share-
holder Bull. Assuming that the other Australian pro-
vinces are exploited at the same rate, this makes £280,000
a year sent out of the country under this heading alone,
and it is only one among dozens of small, unnoticed leak-
ages through which Australia’s coin goes to dear old
Hingland. And this country gets nothing in return for
the loss. It could get its insurance quite as easily and
safely from the local companies and keep the £280,000
per an. in the country. Out of 36 foreign co’s. doing busi-
ness in N.S.W. during ’9l, no less than 18 are returned as
having no assets whatever in the province for the security
of their policy-holders, and four more had less than £2OOO
worth of assets among them. Wanted, a massive tax on
foreign fire and marine insurance companies.
• * • «
One solid reason why the Australian Govts,
should issue their inscribed loans locally is the charge of
12s. 6d. per £lOO made by the British Govt, for stamp-
duty on all issues made in the U.K. For every million
pounds Australia thus pays £6250 for stamps alone 1
Bank and brokers’ charges run up the expense to an
average of £1 6s. per £lOO ; and as these can be dispensed
with in Australia, a loan floated at £98145. here is as good
as one that realises £lOO at the other end.
♦ * ♦ *
In England the banks hold about 10 per cent, of
their liabilities in cash ; in the U.S. about 7 per cent. ; in
Canada about 5 per cent. In Australasia they keep 25
per cent, of their deposits in cash, and with all this
money (about £26,000,000) lying idle they are no safer—
perhaps hardly so safe—as the others. The.explanation
is that in Canada, the U.S., and England the banks hold
large reserves in Govt, securities, which are as good as
cash and produce interest, while cash reserves produce
nothing. And the reason why the Australasian banks
can’t do likewise is because seven idiotic Governments
insist on selling almost all their securities in London, and
Govt, stocks are hardly obtainable at this end. The
exorbitant rate charged for advances is one of the natural
consequences. The bank that has to keep one-fourth of
its deposits—on which it pays interest—lying idle as a
reserve must lend out the other three-fourths at high
rates in order to live. And, in order to get the high
interest, it has often to take very low security—and
bursts. Even the cormorant bank has some excuse for
its rapacity.
If the Colonial Bank gets a reduction of interest
on reconstructed deposits to 24 per cent., how long will it
be before other banks have to follow suit? How can the
institution which pays 4J per cent for its money compete
with the one which pays only 24?
* * * *
South Australia proposes to “convert ” some of
her existing debt into new stock at reduced interest.
The process, unfortunately, doesn’t look promising, for
most of the loans don't mature until far on in next cen-
tury. Over £1,000,000 of the S. A. debt bears interest at
6 per cent., and the last instalment of this gruesome
liability can’t be got rid of until 1918. N.S.W., Queens-
land, W.A., Tasmania, and Maoriland also pay 6 per cent,
on a portion of their debt, and the latter province has the
fragments of some 6J and 7 per cent, loans still standing.
Also, all the provinces except Queensland are paying 5
per cent, on a large portion of their debt (N.S.W. owes
£4,000,000 at this exceedingly steep rate, and the last
instalment doesn’t fall due until 1902). The old financial
fathers of the land who borrowed money at exorbitant
interest, and fixed the liability for 30 years, left a weighty
burden behind them.

London Economist cries aloud on behalf of
that soft-headed and hard-hearted person, the British
Infestor. Vic. County Court Judges, it seems, must be
made independent to look after the 8.1.’s interests.
Which suggests a delusive hope that the British Infestor
might be eternally frightened away by a judicial white-
washing of all the boomsters and land bankrupts that
have not “ gone through.” As only the small fry are
left it would not be a big sacrifice to pass them through
the mill. But the commercial immorality of Melbourne
will not succeed where the Socialism of Maoriland has
failed. The British Infestor has lent money to Socialists,
and that proves that debentures offered by Old Nick on
the security of his sulphur matches wmuld find buyers.

N.S. Wales, at Ist January, 1895, owed
£52,052,690 in London and £6,151,563 in Australia. Cal-
culated on the amount actually received by the province
when it sold these securities, the local debt costs £3 18s.
percent, in interest, and the British debt costs a trifle
over £4 per cent. The British money-market is an ex-
pensive place.
* * * *
The Mount Lyell (Tas.) Mining 00. recently
attempted to sell £150,000 worth of 6 per cent, debentures
in London. The brokers, however, proceeded to demand
a number of further concessions, in addition to the very
liberal 6 per cent., and, after much expensive cabling, the
offer was withdrawn. The debentures were then offered
in Melbourne, and subscribed in a single day, at a much
higher price than J. Bull had offered. The only really
surprising feature about the business is the foolishness of
the Mount Lyell people in resorting to London at all.
• ♦ * •
A new swindle. Someone in Hamburg stole or
obtained some invoice-forms from a big house. With
these filled up to represent a £440 shipment of merchan-
dise, they got from the representatives of an Antipodean
bank a large portion of this amount, the bank security, of
course, being the goods, which were shipped to an Aus-
tralasian port. Delivery of documents could not be given
until the bank was paid. Now' it appears that the mer-
chandise was sent a very round-about way, and addressed
to a bogus house, so the rascals had time to vanish. The
bank eventually decided to examine its security. On the
Customs opening up the cases they found each one to
contain two other cases, one inside the other, and made
of a heavier wood than the outside package. The firm in
Hamburg, of course, knows nothing about it, and the
bank’s representatives say that this firm employs so many
clerks that they could not remember what kind of gentle-
man opened the business. The shipping-agents, of
course, willingly gave freight and B/L forms when they
saw the packages properly marked and addressed, so the
bank has to bear the loss.

N.S. Wales this year has 1,323,814 acres under
crop, being 116,822 acres more than in the previous year
and nearly 500,000 acres more than in the last year of the
Freetrade policy. That is to say, agriculture has pro-
gressed five times as fast under the present regime as
under the one which Eye-glass Reid proposes to again in-
flict on his suffering country.

A large number of counterfeit shillings—dates
1872 and 1856—are in circulation in Sydney. They are
splendid imitations, and, off-hand, would deceive any-
body. They are distinguishable only by their compara-
tive lightness and by a faint irregularity in the lettering.

The “ meejuin of exchange ” disease has invaded
the pulpit. The Rev. Tuck, of South Broken Hill
(Baptist)preached on a recent Sunday on “Bimetallism:
What say the Scriptures?” Text: “Why silver has
become dross.” Mr. Tuck floored the heretical mono-

• • • •
The London Bank of Australia’s balance-sheet
for the half-year ended 31st December, ’95, appears in
another column. Compared with the return for the same
half-year of ’93, the reconstructed deposits have been
reduced by £500,000, while the current accounts and new
deposits have increased by £160,000. The note circula-
tion has dropped by £20,000. The general business of
the bank is apparently being well sustained, and the
cash reserve is £250,000 larger than it was. Also, it would
seem that so far there have been no losses worth speak-
ing of in realising the assets of the old London Chartered
Bank, for the large sum set apart to cover any possible
liability in this direction remains intact, and is accumu-
lating interest. Also, the net profits in the present
balance-sheet are £8216, against £7026 for last half-year,
and £5916 for the one preceding. The position is ap-
parently as sound as could be desired for the bank's
creditors, and it seems reasonably good for the 54 per
cent, preference shareholders, who, so far, have received
their dividend regularly. For the ordinary shareholders
there is no position worth mentioning.
• * * •
The Bank of Victoria started a practical
“ restoration of confidence ” by paying the first instal-
ment of its old deposit liability last week. A large pro-
portion of the unloaded gold was transferred to current
accounts in the reconstructed bank.
* * • *
The Royal Bank of Queensland has been en-
deavouring to raise money in London, by the sale of 4
per cent, “deposit stock,” wherewith to pay off the re-
mainder of its 4J per cent, reconstructed deposits. Ap-
parently, however, like the Mount Lyell mining 00., it
finds the ways of the London market very hard, and, at
latest advices, proposes to give 4J per cent, for two years,
and 4 per cent, thereafter. So far as can be judged by
the scanty cable information, the Royal is doing some-
thing particularly foolish. It could almost to a certainty
get the required amount in Australia at 4 per cent., and
thus save a considerable amount of interest, and by pay-
ing off its British creditors, get rid of a remote and awk-
ward liability. And, amid the general uncertainty of
things, 4 per cent, is a sufficiently heavy liability, con-
sidering that it is a fixed burden, and not one which can
be paid off at the bank’s option, like the existing recon-
structed deposits.
* * * •
One of the Southern reconstructed banks is
notoriously pursuing the old boom policy on narrower
lines, granting small overdrafts to shaky suburban trades-
men on weak security, or no security at all.

Hillgrove and Armidale Water-power Electric
00. is to be wound up voluntarily. A meeting for this
purpose will be held on 20th inst., when the share-
holders will doubtless accept the directors’ advice.
The co. started with too little actual cash capital,
and was financed largely on paper. After working hard,
the pioneers are not to have a leg-in, but whoever goes
into possession of the works—now erected and only
awaiting a downpour of rain—will make a fortune.

At the meeting of the British depositors in the
Standard Bank (Melb.), when they agreed to reduce to 2J
per cent., it was announced that the bank had never
earned more than that rate of interest since its recon-
struction, and the remainder of the 44 per cent, had been
paid out of the assets. The same lugubrious director
stated that when the bank’s estates were realised it would
probably be found that the capital was all lost, and
meanwhile the shareholders must go on paying calls with
the best grace they could. As for amalgamation, that
was impossible. Nobody that the Standard wants to
amalgamate with would amalgamate with the Standard.

Dear Bulletin, —Apropos yours re bi-metallism,
tri-metallism, &c. Up here we are a tin district, likewise
a silver country, also a goldfield. So that we are all there
on tljf metal question. For years we have been pro-
ducing silver that doesn’t pay decent wages. At Muldiva
they got about £2 a week at the finish; at Montalbion
they got£3, but paid this in the end at the rale of 12s. 6d.
in the £. Bi-metally, we are in a bad way. The price of
tin is regulated by the Straits output. The Straits is a
silver coinage country, and, meanwhile, we are doing our
blankest to supply the employer of Coolie and Chinkie
labor in that benighted country with cheap silver so that
he can undersell us in the tin market. We may be the
salt of the earth—to put it mildly, I suppose we are—but
the Chinkie and the Coolie and the Hindoo and the
Hottentot are the mustard and pepper and roast-beef, and
the whole bill of fare and the cook. For silver has gone
bung, and tin is suffering from locomotor-ataxy and
shuffles along anyhow, and naught but the voice of the
preserved-meatfman is heard in the land. Up to date we
have supplied the Chinkie and the Malay and the Coolie
and everything having the mark of the beast, with
ofteap silver to keep their tin-mines going, and now we
are going to lay ourselves out to supply him with cheap
meat and extract and offal and hair and bones and every-
thing that pertaineth to a meat-works. And all we
require to do it decently is another million’s worth of
railway, a deep-sinking bonus, a beef bonus, and a few
Divisional Board grants chucked in. The real evil of
these days with us up here is wanto-metallism. —Yours,
Pumpkin head, Mareeba, 23rd May, 1895, N. Queensland.
♦ » * ♦
The general opinion seems to be, writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, that the Melb. universal providers
kill the small shop-keeper’s business in the neighbor-
hood. But the contrary is the fact. The big premises
are surrounded by dozens of small business jackals,
drapers, boot-shops, &c., all doing well. The business is,
in fact, attracted by the big firm, and the whole street is
well-advertised. It is the shop-keeper in other suburbs
who suffers. His suburb has no famous shop, so people
don’t trouble to come, and don’t even see his goods.
» * * *
The big Melb. universal providing firms don’t
harry their employes by means of numerous shop-walkers.
They give a bonus of 2d. in the £ on all sales, and so en-
courage them to sell as much as possible. Besides this,
a half-guinea prize is given to the selling most
for the week. A counter-jumper in the millinery dept,
was, on a recent Saturday morning, bewailing his poor
chance for the prize when a carriage drove up with a
customer for a 60-guinea mantle. This, besides giving
him 10s. 6d. commission, brought him to the top of the
list and entitled him to the extra half-guinea.
* ♦ ♦ •
Hobart Gas 00. is applying to Parliament for
permission to electrically light the city and suburbs. The
Act, if passed, should fix a definite time for the accom-
plishment of the work. Otherwise it may be used only to
nourish a monopoly by taking the wind out of the sails
of a possible opposition.

diately raised the price of flour £1 per ton, the bakers
following suit by increasing the charge for reputed 21b.
loaf by Jd.— equivalent to a rise of £3 a ton. The capi-
talist always has the big end of the stick.

The South Australian Co. (London) realised
£21,000 profit last year, and the shareholders, who com-
plain bitterly thereat, are of opinion that “ recent taxa-
tion in S.A. was intended to drive away British capital.”
Which it probably was. Only it can’t drive away the
British capital of the S.A. Co., for that grinding corpora-
tion never brought a single coin into the country, though
it has taken many hundreds of thousands out of it.

Chaffey’s Renmark (S.A.) irrigation settlement
is being floated in London as a limited liability 00.,
capital £500,000. Thus the well-meant concession mads
in a weak moment to the enterprising Chafley developes,
as all such concessions do, into another absentee syndi-
cate, which will be a permanent burden on the com-
munity. And as settlement increases in the surrounding
districts, the rent burden will grow larger and larger, as
such burdens always do. The object of the Renmark
concession was to “ open up the country” according to
the good old wheeze; but as the people who opened up
the country keep it after it is opened, S.A. profits nothing
by the opening.

Dear Bulletin, —Some remarks apropos to your
recent leader on the N.S.W. sugar industry. The s.s.
Maoleay lately landed 40 Hindoos here (Wyrallah), and I
hear that fully 200 more are on the way. That will
make a total of about 500 in this district alone. The
sugar industry is more than “piebald” here—it is
black, with white points. Rifled hen-roosts, pillaged
vegetable-gardens, exposure of person to little girls,
gross assaults on little boys, the waylaying of children
on the way to school, and what looks like leprosy, but
may be some other vile disease, are among the bless-
ings conferred on us by these black devils. Disease is
rampant in the cane, scarcely a field being free. In my
opinion, it doesn’t matter a “Hindoo” what becomes of
the sugar industry in N.S.W.—Cornstalk. Wyrallah,
Richmond River, May 25, '95.
* • • •
Editor Bulletin, —Re the sugar-industry. You
say that the low-lying lands of the Clarence and Rich-
mond are played out for cane-growing, and from this you
draw the inference (that the industry is not worth pro-
tecting. It may interest you to know that, so far from
this being the fact, there are thousands of acres of high
land not yet cultivated that can be brought under sugar-
cane which would be of high density and would yield
heavy crops. In fact, there is enough such land available
on the Richmond and Tweed to grow all the sugar re-
quired in N.S. Wales, but with the uncertainty of the
tariff no new mills are likely to be erected. The industry
at present employs (at the best wages in N.S.W., bar
shearing) 6000 white men, and I would ask Freetraders,
especially Frank Cotton, to tell me how these
men are to be profitably employed if their pre-
sent occupation is taken away. There is no other
line in agriculture that can absorb them, and where are
the factories or trades that require them ? Another point
worth considering is that £1,000,000 has been invested in
the business, and, if the duty is taken off now, would be
mostly lost. About two years ago I rented 60 acres of
land and spent £6OO on it in planting and cultivating
cane. I will only get one cutting off it before the duty
comes off, and this will not repay me the money I am out
of pocket, much less pay me for my work. My case is
simply one of hundreds. Now, coming to black labor, I
admit at once that there are a number of Kanakas on the
Tweed, but there are not 20 on the Richmond, and I don’t
think that many on the Clarence. A number of Hindoos
are growing cane on this river, but compete very little
with the white workers, and I am sure 99 per cent,
of the farmers would be glad to see them go. I,
therefore, claim that the duty is money well spent
when it is considered that it is supporting not less than
20,000 persons in a comfortable manner. I have very
little to say about the C.S.R. Co., but I would draw the
Labor-party’s attention to the fact that this co.’s relations
with its employes have always been of a most liberal
and satisfactory nature —I don’t think there has ever
been a strike among its men, and wages have always been
above the average, canecutters earning from £4O to £45
per season of 20 weeks, with tucker added. I don’t see
that the N.S.W. canegrowers will have anything to fear
from Federation, for it is not likely that Queensland
would be allowed to continue the black-labor traffic, and
we can stand on our own bottom and compete with them
if they employ white men.—l am, &c., Chbribon.

Setting-machines don’t appear to be an imme-
diate possibility on Melb. Aob. David Syme recently put
in a type-foundry, and a new dress is rapidly being oast
for the big paper.
Manager Wynne, of Sydney D.T., has accom-
plished the amalgamation of the rival press cable-syndi-
cates, and in the near future the Australian dailies will
deal out their breakfast budget of foreign fairy-tales on
purely monopolistic lines. This combine was mooted
some years ago, but the then stately Argus held aloof.
The union will effect a colossal saving to the proprietaries
concerned, and the new association will prove “a corker”
to any new opposition to the existing papers in any of the
big cities.

Dear Bulletin, —No wonder rents are low in
Melb. and landlords are “ loud in their wail,” but there
are greater sufferers than the property-owners. In boom
time thousands of workers were persuaded to secure
homes of their own through the various mushroom build-
ing-societies, &c. Crippled by the depression, at least 70
per cent, of these families fell into arrears, and, after pay-
ing off a considerable portion of the purchase-money, have
had their houses seized by societies, the original object of
which— vide prospectuses—was to provide our working
classes with healthy homes of their own in the only
manner possible to the humble wage-earner. In hundreds
of cases the houses were almost paid for when seized, and,
as a matter of course, the liquidators can afford to let
these places at absurdly low figures. The workman pays
dearly for everything that’s cheap. - Snell.
* * * •
The latest. The patent laws are practically
inoperative in Japan. Hence the Japs, find a munificent
harvest in the manufacture of patent medicines which
they sell at half the European’s selling-price, and yet
make a profit of 200 per cent.

The power of ready cash. The other day the
representative of a Parisian perfumery house waited on
an Australian mammoth provider and submitted his list-
prices. “They are of no use to me,” said the big man,
“ but if you can quote me 20 per cent, discount I’ll order
£lO,OOO worth.” The traveller cabled to Paris and his
firm took the order.

Owing to the N.S.W. duty of 2d. per lb. on
butter, cream is now taken into Broken Hill from S.A.
and churned there. The factory, though only just started,
already deals with 100 gallons of cream daily. Hitherto
the duty has led to the importation of most of the butter
from the Sydney side. The wine-duty led long ago to the
importation of grapes, and the establishment of a press at
the Hill.

have been acting as correspondents for the “leading”
Sydney dailies. Journalists know what that means.—
SOCKKR.

♦ » • ♦
While exploring the new kaolin body at the
Central (8.H.) some old uncharted workings were cut
into. The south drive at 80ft. met a mullocked-up set,
the continuation of workings from the east. The set,
evidently six or seven years old, had good kaolin ore on
two sides, and why it was mullocked-up and exploration
discontinued is a mystery. The discovery recalls one
made at the Junction some time since, where it was found
that a rich ore-shoot had been tested by bore-holes from
an adjacent drive, the holes having been then plugged up.
♦ • « »
The sale of B.H. Central for .£175,000 may be
considered. •*off.” The recent development of kaolin
where none was expected has wonderfully helped the
malcontents. The possibility of the whole business being
little else than a market move never seemed to strike
anybody. Rioh as the kaolin development may be, it has
little effect on the market, which went up on the offer
alone. If the offer to buy a mine for £175,000 subject to
a deposit of a few thousands is sufficient to send shares
to par, then the making of offers can be made good
business. Without the solution of the sulphide problem,
there is, so far, nothing in the Central mine to make it
worth £175,000.

After many years fruitless prospecting the Car-
lisle, Bendigo, is on the div. list. It was a big mine 20
years ago. After J. B. Watson’s death it acquired the
Kentish claim, out of which J. B. made hundreds of
thousands, and it is in this ground the strike has been
made.

Things are looking much better for G. Lansell

though not fully determined, is good enough as far as
opened. He is also working the Comet at a good profit,
although it is only running 6dwts., and in several of the
recent developments in other mines he is largely in-
terested. Under these circumstances, George has pro-
bably abandoned his threat to clear out and live in dear
old England.
* » • *
Roberts and Sons, of Bendigo, are turning out a
30-head battery for the Golconda mines, Murchison. It
is geared with ropes ; the practice of driving direct from
cog-wheels is rapidly going out of date.
♦ » * •
The most permanent-looking div. mines in
Bendigo to-day are the New Prince of Wales and New
Moon. The former was abandoned before the deep-sink-
ing age, and is now opening up a big body of stone at
300 ft. It. has, so far, averaged an oz. Unlike most of
the Bendigo mines, it has no deep levels, and has, there-
fore, all virgin country below 300 ft. The New Moon, too,
is not a deep mine. Both occupy isolated positions. The
Prince is supposed to he on the Sebastian line, but the
fact is not established beyond a doubt.
• ♦ * *
Star of the East, Ballarat, 2045 tons, 4040 z.—
payable, but too low-grade for divs. Will somebody
explain why this mine recently threw out 25 vanner
concentrators and is working Halley’s shaking-tables
instead?
* * • *
South Star, Ballarat, having spent thousands
in development, and worked at a loss for years, now holds
a good credit, and chairman Vale anticipates early divs.
Last crushing, 305 tons, averaged close on 17Jdwt. The
Star of the East, adjoining, has driven north at the
1000 ft. level and picked up the continuation of the stone
now being operated by the South Star. Stone, sft. wide,
shows gold freely.
* * * *
Seymour, of Bainbridge and Seymour, London,
who came out to report on the Londonderry, says that 10
years hence Ooolgardie will have a population of 100,000.
♦ * * «
The Auckland (M.L.) syndicate which des-
patched Hunt, of Shotover fame, to Ooolgardie, sold his
discovery there—the Mararoa—for £B3OO.
* * * *
Of all Australian gold-fields, Charters Towers
extorts most admiration for consistency. Its monthly
yield of something over 20,0000 z., at about an oz. to the
ton, has now long continued with neither undue depres-
sion nor undue exaltation, but, if anything, a slight and
sustained increase. The May yield was 21,4900 z. from
21,854 tons, paying divs. £24,662. The features of the
month, according to Bright’s circular, were the decline
in Mills United output, owing to divs. having apparently
been considered before development; and the counter-
balancing improvement in the Brilliant mines. Mount
Leyshon, by-tbe-by, now manages to make working
expenses out of 3dwt. ore, by treating up to 3000 tons per
month ; and sdwt. would show a fair profit. Share prices
are well maintained.
* * * *
Victory (Charters Towers) has made a call,
£4OOO. Half-year’s output: 1861 tons, 16350 z.; expenses,
£10,206 ; profit, £1551195. sd. The glory has departed.
* * » •
Allowing for the general air of unreliability
which pervades Westralian reports, it seems that the
Londonderry has succeed in gluing together some of the
bottom which fell out of it, and has taken “several
thousand oz.” from the new shoot struck. Also, it seems
uncertainly clear that the Wealth of Expectations is
realising on a reef at 110 ft., averaging “20oz. to the ton."
And there is rather less haze about the alleged fact that
the Hit-or-Miss, north of White Feather, which is being
floated in London for £200,000 (vendors, £100,000), has a
reef “31ft. thick,” producing magnificent specimens, and
considered by complaisant “ good judges ” to be the
finest property in Westralia.
» * * •
The Bulletin congratulates Lord Fingall.
Having been played upon by one or two accomplished
boomsters and fooled into believing that there were cer-
tainly millions in the Londonderry, he (a man with a re-
putation to lose) endured the horrible nightmare of seeing
the gold vanish in an hour. It re-appeared last week and
outside the newspaper-accounts, on which little reliance
is ordinarily to be placed, the writer has satisfactory evi-
dence that one of the faces of the reef (in the South drive
at the 100 ft. level) is marvellously rioh. It is, therefore,
easy to believe that the Earl is “ much pleased." But it
is almostifbo good to believe the new find is a continua-
tion of the big shoot. It is more than probably another
distinctive make of rich ore.
• • • •
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Co. of Sydney has realised a profit of over 5 per cent,
on its capital for the year just closed, not 2J per cent, as
erroneously stated in a recent issue. The Intercolonial
is a well-established institution with a solid reputation,
and in times like these, when few such institutions can do
more than pay working expenses, its 6 per cent, is a re-
markable evidence of solvency and good management.
» ♦ • •
Dr. Simon, representing a French crowd, has
paid heavy deposits on the New Victoria and Bendigo-
Coolgardie mines, and the shares of each have boomed.
Three weeks ago Bendigo-Cooigardies were at 7d. ; now
they are 10s. Sales, not mining prospects, send shares
up. Something rich is reported to have been struck in
the New Victoria, whose area is not enough for Paris—
besides, the other mine has got a 15-head battery I
• » ♦ *
Says a recent Ooolgardie wire—and, by the way,
they all seem to emanate from the same source, and
appear simultaneously in the metropolitan papers of the
provinces : “ Hayes’ Hit-or-Miss lease is now regarded as
the finest show in W.A. Stated last week that the pros-
pectors dollied over lOOOoz.” “ Now regarded ”is good.
Like every dog, every mine has its day at Ooolgardie.
Some only half-a-day. Some have only an hour. They
are rich at ten o’clock and poor at eleven— i.e., if any
dynamite is used meanwhile.

Eldorado (Pilbarra, W.A.): “Mine looking

earthly use is anything but the average ? In the Bendigo
sdwt. mines there is some 20oz. stone, but the managers
would be sacked if they said so in their reports.

The costs of the Dean Commission will be,

The late Randolph Churchill had six de-
cidedly plain sisters, of various degrees of plain-
ness-some of them might almost have been called
haggard and bony. Five of them married into
the peerage, and the sixth annexed the son of old
Samuel Wilson, of Victoria. Among them, they
are reputed to have married £10,000,000 of hard
cash.

Sarah Gale, transported to N.S. Wales in
1839 for complicity in the woman-murder for
which James Greenaore was hanged, is buried in
the Wesleyan Cemetery, Devonshire-street, Syd-
ney. For many years the interesting creature
(who had a wicked swivel eye) was consort of a
pie-shop-keeper.

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,

MHE?
“ That which we call a rose, by any other
name would smell as sweet,” and so it
happens that we want to explain what
is MYFA? It is only a name,
the new name for Mitchell’s
“My Favorite” Tea. Why do
we change the name? Simply to
protect you from fraud. The success of
“My Favorite” Tea has been
gigantic; its immense popularity is so
pronounced that it has numbers of imita-
tors already, who have neither the brains
nor the inclination to get an original
name of their own
A recent decision of the Supreme Court has revealed the fact that the
words “My Favorite” can be used by anybody, and we have therefore, in
order to prevent your being misled into buying “ Favorite ” Tea, and

Two verses from “Federation”—a poem
by one J. J. Murphy, of Melbourne
See the hosts of high officials, some with salaries twice
too much,
While the working men are scanted, these folks are get-
ting rich.
They re a swim of selfish seekers who in secret so con-
nives,
They’re identical to drones, sucking honey from the hives.
The public funds are lavished by officials in the jobs,
And palm-oil so kindly given was shoved into their fobs.
Then with balls and dinner parties and choicest of cham-
pagne, '
Hooray, Hip, Hip ! they’ve got the tip, and will visit
there again.

address as “ G.P.0.” at the end of his production.
He can’t be rushed there.
Hogue, M.L.A. (N.S.W.J : “ Victoria has
been going backward and backward as the result
of Protection. The people of Victoria have been
taxed off the soil.’’ Yet, despite her smaller area,
11,000 more people lived by agriculture in Victoria
than in N.S.W. at the date of last census returns.
And in Maoriland, which is more heavily taxed
still, a larger proportion of the people live by
agriculture than even in Victoria.

Marriage has been a splendid success in
land-boom circles, anyway. Another big specu-
lator registers a transfer of household goods and
chattels to the wife of his bosom, in consideration
of her “ love and affection.” Good old L. and A.
How brightly they bloom in the gloaming !
A Booinster loves his ’Lizer
When the banks “ keep right ”;
Naught in reason he denies her
Whilst the sun shines bright.
Yet, although her rings and dresses
Prove the fondness he professes,
Little else that wife possesses
When the banks “keepright.”
But Boomster’s love expandeth
When the banks squeeze tight ■
His belongings then he handeth
To his Heart’s Delight.
Spite of legal snorts and strictures,
’Lizer cops the plate and pictures.
Also furniture and fixtures —
When the banks squeeze tight.
There’s a recklessness Byronic
In a love like this.
Sure, adversity’s a tonic
For hymeneal bliss.
Heaven bless mad speculations
Which lead up to complications
And result in demonstrations
Of a love like this !

SPENCER’S WEEKLY.
A Journal of Practical Reform.
WILL be devoted to the Discussion and
Exposure of the Caxises which produce
DEPRESSION and hinder the return of pros-
perity. In Economics the groundwork of its
argument will be the following axiom :
UNEARNED GAINS
Acquired by the Few, mean
Undeserved Impoverishment
Inflicted on the Many.
“SPENCER’S WEEKLY” will advocate
the gradual Resumption by the State of
the Unearned Increment in the Value of
Land. It will also advocate the RE-
MONETISATION OF SILVER, in
order to remove the CRUSHING BUR-
DEN which has been imposed upon PRO-
DUCTIVE ENTERPRISE by the
UNEARNED INCREMENT in the
VALUE OF GOLD.
“SPENCER’S WEEKLY” will advocate
the adoption of simple and effective
SANITARY MEASURES, but will
STRENUOUSLY OPPOSE
THE INFLICTION OF DISEASE BY
LAW, whether by Compulsory Vaccina-
tion, or any other form of the Compulsory
Inoculation of Disease.
A SPECIAL and MOST VALUABLE
FEATURE will be an accurate and
reliable Review of the COMMERCIAL
and MONETARY MOVEMENTS of
the WEEK.
