SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Maoriland racing clubs have made a new
departure in working the “ tote.” The instrument now
takes deposits, and issues in exchange for them credit
notes, with which the holder can back his fancy at any
machine on the course. Many people who plunge heavily
adopt this system, as it avoids the necessity of carrying
about large sums of money, and £2OOO has been thus
deposited at one meeting. Another alteration has been
made in the system of keeping accounts. Formerly,
when a man bought his tickets at the window the dial
immediately showed how much had been added to the
total sum invested, and also how much had been added
to the amount on each horse. The crowd which was
watching outside thus knew what horses he had backed,
and if the backer was reputed to be a knowing indi-
vidual there was a rush to follow his lead, and
his div. was much reduced in consequence. Many
devices were tried to obviate this difficulty. The
simplest and commonest was to send an unknown
person to put the money on, while the knowing one
walked up to the window and publicly put a pound or two
on the most improbable animal on the list, and left the
crowd to rush the wrong horse to its heart’s content.
This worked very well, provided the person who was sent
to back the right horse didn’t give the secret away to his
own friends, or embezzle the money, or get drunk, or be
found out. Another plan was to put on £lOO or so at the
last moment, and then before the official had rung his
bell a hundred times the race would start, and the
machine had to close. But these and other dodges were
all more or less unsatisfactory, so now as the money comes
in the dial only shows the total amount invested. When
the starting-bell rings the machine closes, and then the
amounts on each horse go up. Consequently the man
who has exclusive information can run his little game on
all the machines on the course without letting the public
into the secret. This often places him in a better posi-
tion than if he bet with the bookmaker, for Mossy Isaacs
used to grow suspicious and shorten the odds before all
the money was on, while the “ tote ” is a guileless im-
plement which has no suspicions whatever.
* ♦ ♦ •
“Jack” Murray, who for several sessions has
fathered the “Tote” Bill in Vic. Assembly, has been
trying to get Govt, support for it, but Premier Turner
(solicitor, by the way, for the V.R.C. and some leading
bookmakers) won’t look at the measure. Attorney-
Gen. Isaacs, who holds many briefs for Abraham, Isaac and
Jacob, can hardly “turn dog” on the crowd. All the same,
Murray’s little Bill, though not “kosher,” is likely to get
more consideration than heretofore, especially as many
of the pious brigade '“ got the run ” at last election.
Also, the prospect of the State’s making £20,000 a year
will weigh in its favor just now.
* • * *
The decision of Sydney Tattersall’s Club to pay
wagers on Merry Girl makes a record even for leather-
lunged effrontery. To put the facts in a nutshell. Merry
Girl weighed in at least 21b. overweight, and no declara
tion having been made, by the rules under which the race
was run, the Kensington Club had no course open to it
but to disqualify the mare, and award the stakes to the
second horse, Pearl Powder. Merry Girl’s owner, a
prominent horse owner and member of Tatt.’s, pro-
tested against the decision, and threatened to
prosecute his claim with the utmost rigor of
the law, from the Water Police-court to the Privy
Council. Where Tattersall’s Club “came in” at this
point of the game is one of those things “no fellow can
understand,” but come in it did, and with some pur-
pose, for it actually over-rode the rule under which the
race was ridden— viz., “that wagers are inseparable from
the stakes,” and instructed its bagged and bejewelled
members to “pay Merry Girl.” That such decision
was distasteful to some of Tatt.’s committee-men
not interested in “the bag,” was evidenced by the im-
mediate resignation of the hon. treasurer, Mr. Hourigar,
and Mr. Harris.
Kensington has ever proved a thorn in the side
of the Associated Clubs, who, as might only have been
expected, joined hands with the Associated Bookmakers,
with the expectation, no doubt, of putting a nail in the
Kensington coffin. Kensington, however, is very much
alh e. Convenient to the city, it is a favorite course with
race-goers, more especially as it has the unique
reputation of trying to put down the open, bare faced
roping carried out on so many other courses ; and it is
just a question whether Kensington, with its small
entrance-fee to the paddock, would not knock out th"
long-railway-journey and half-sovereign courses, if it
decided to hold an every Saturday meeting.
Anyhow, the public are very sore just now, and
with Kensington and the totalisator in view it is not 8 ife
for either the Associated Clubs or the books to play the
game too low down on the crowd who find the sinews ol
war.
* « • •
In re Sydney Tattersall’s billiard handicap, the
members are much exercised over the “ ringing in ” on a
long mark of an alleged top-notoher who, for months, has
been playing at the rooms, receiving 25 and a licking.
He has been backed to win some thousands, but layers
swear they won’t pay out over him, and when Tatt.’s men
say they won’t pay out, they won’t. Anyone who has
doubts on the subject had better ask the Pearl Powderers.
» * ♦ ♦
Victoria was never so hard-up for jumping
cattle as at present. Old sports look back to the days of
Banker, Babbler, Inglesides Simpleton, Lone Hand, and
a score of other 13 and 14 stoners. Blue Mountain and
Redleap seem to have been the last of the jumpers.

thing.” For the recently-deceased sire Nordenfeldt, it is
said, Mr. Sam Hordern (who gave £5BBO for the horse)
could have got from 12,000 to 20,000 guineas. And now
he has the hide.

All difficulties respecting the late O. B. Lowe’s
(of N.S.W.) book on horse-breeding having been smoothed
away, it will be published almost immediately at the
London Field office, under the editorship of William

“You speak of Selling Races,”
said one old chap, after the bush race-meet-
ing. “ I ran a mare here in the early
days. It was a Selling Race, and she was
entered under ray trainer’s name to be
sold for 50 guineas. She won. At the
sale the auctioneer (he was steward and
publican as well) dwelt a long time be-
fore knocking her down, and a young chap
ran her up to £lOO. I wanted my mare,
but not at his price. As luck would have
it, the young fellow got drunk and didn’t
turn up in time to complete his purchase.
Just before she was put up again I went to
the auctioneer and told him I was the
owner, and hinted that he need not take so
long before calling her off. “ Sure, Mr.
K.,” said he, “if I’d have only known it
at first 1 Just see me do the business this
time.” He mounted the barrel and began:
“Now, gintleraen, here’s a mare by Peter
Wilkins out of a mare—l won’t desave yez—
that was not much account. Look at this
mare’s near fore leg. It’s dicky, and
to carry more than 7st. would break her
heart. None but a damn fool would kape
such a baste, so I’ll knocker down to her
owner. Fifty guineas —to her owner,
gintlemen.”

A big effort is to be made by the
V.R.C. to prevent bogus entries for race-
meetings. The nomination by \vire of a
well-known performer always brings down
the weights of the remaining horses and
establishes false market prices. Lately an
owner was fined for not scratching a horse
which he had never entered.

• • * •
The football clubs, Pirate and Wentworth, met
to settle their disputes on Sydney Cricket Ground last
Saturday, and, for the space of 80 min., the combatants
wrestled and flopped and collared and tussled and
scrummed in order to throw ail possible light upon the
subject. The Pirate backs seemed to regard the thing
partly as a holiday outing, for their forwards kept the
Wentworth team scrumming at its own end, and whenever
the ball came near a Pirate back he promptly kicked
it 60yds. or so, and then proceeded to mark time
and await another opportunity. The Wentworth bar-
rackers continually called upon their men to “go on,”
which they undoubtedly would have done but for the
iron arm or heel or head of one of the opposing team.
One of the Wentworth team played like a philosopher,
always losing the ball whenever a Pirate man rushed at
him with assault and battery printed in his large and
glaring eye. The work in the scrums was quick and
effective ; in the space of a minute or so either the ball
would be’kicked out, or a man, or both. The Wentworths
were justly defeated by 11 points to 3.
* * * »
At Wallsend, N.S.W., the other Saturday, foot-
ball barrackers gave a real taste of their quality. They
pelted the captain of the visiting and opposing team
during.the game ; after the game they pelted and hooted
the visitors off the ground, and, following them to their
hotel, where a ’bus stood full of women, they pelted the
’bus and the women with rotten eggs. The explanation
of this larrikin outburst is easy. Footbalj itself is a brutal
game, far more demoralising than boxing at its worst.
In a boxing contest with gloves one man can hit the other
with all the force he can muster in a standing posture,
but in a football match the same man running 10 yards a
second can shoot out his arm with all his strength, and
all the impetus of his speed, and stiffen a would-be
««collarer.” Moreover, in football, one man can throw
another, and if the other doesn’t let go the ball he can
gouge his eyes out, or drive his knee or elbow his
stomach or throat, or twist his neck, or break his spine.
In season ’92, in Brisbane, three football players were
killed outright upon the ground.

The toughs, roughs, louts and bullies who play
football and insult the umpire are having it their own way
in N.S.W. The Rugby Union is powerless. Players
threaten to “break the referee’s blanky nose unless he
blows the whistle,” and the mobs that barrack threaten
to “ pull the referee’s blanky liver out it he orders a
player off the field. There are, in N.S.W., about 5000
men plaving Rugby football every Saturday afternoon,
and apparently a force of 500 police is required to ensure
decent P langua y ge and behavior. The University team
meets its difficulty in its own way. It refuses to play if
Mr. M'Manamey referees— ostensibly because that referee
is too conscientious. m 0

In the two-miles cycling handicap on Sydney
Cricket Ground last Saturday, some 36 cyclists in silken
glory came out to race themselves blind for the miserable
sum of £7. The pace from the start was so fast that the
scratch men were clearly handicapped out of the race ;
nathless Megson rode grandly, and Lewis and O’Brien

♦ * * ♦
Zimmerman, the Carbine of cyclists, comes to
Australia to show his prowess as a tremendous advertising
medium for a particular brand of cycle. He has been
sought after by every cycle manufacturer, and the one
who offered Zimmerman one fourth the profit*
secured him. Last year Zimmerman made £BOOO. nut
Zimmerman will find, like every other man who visits
Australia, that the Australian youth can discriminate
between a cycle and the man who rides it, and that said
youth is shrewd enough to “drop ” to the fact that the
cycle Zimmerman races on has been constructed regard-
less of expense to suit its famous rider. Zimmerman does
not pin his faith to any particular brand of cycle ; he pins
it to the cyclist manufacturer who 'gives him the biggest
fee. It is not a cycle that makes a Zimmerman.
Zimmerman will start for Australia on Bth
Aug., and will be in Sydney the first or second day of
Sept. He will race in Sydney under the wing of the
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, for which privilege they
pay handsomely. The advent of Zimmerman will quicken
the pulse of the would-be cyclist, and once the fever
catches on, Australia, like England and America, will
meet with a cycling boom. In London, last Easter Mon-
day, 20,000 cyclists dashed through Kingston-on-Thames.
Mrs. Haddock, captain of the Sydney Ladies’
Bicycle Club, is going to cycle with her husband to Bris-
bane. Some months ago she cycled from Sydney to Mel-
bourne.

The Bulletin gets this par. from a Fitz-
simmons man: “All the money in the Corbett-Fitzsimmons
match (£4000) is now in, and the date of meeting is 31st
October. Corbett is said to have been having ‘ rather
too good a time,’ and looks flabby. It is any odds the
Yankee wriggles out of the match before the four months
are up. If he fights, it may be the fight of his life.” And
a Corbett man sends along this extract from an American
paper; "Captain Glori, who managed Fitzsimmons until
recently, says; 1 When I saw Fitz, last in Baltimore about
a week ago he was looking like a wreck. His cheeks were
thinned down. His eyes were sunken. He had a glassy
stare, and he walked in a shambling style. In his present
condition he would not last a round with Sullivan, to say
nothing of Corbett. I tell you Fitz. has aged five years in
the last six months, and if he does not change his manner
of living he will never step into the ring with Corbett-
much less fight him successfully. He is simply living too
fast. That is all there is about it.’ ”

THE BIRDS WHISTLED “ THE DEAD
MARCH.”
“ Even the birds on the trees learned to whistle the
‘ Dead March.’ ”
It was an old soldier who was talking. “We
were in camp,” he said “ in a flat, malarious part
of the country. Our Colonel was a splendid
fighter, hut didn’t appear to have any idea of
sanitary matters. Just then we were in more
danger from disease than from the enemy. Pre-
sently fever broke out and men died by the dozen.
Hardly a day but we buried some of them in the
swamp. In fact, we played the ‘ Dead March ’
so often that I used to fancy the birds in the trees
had learned to whistle it. ‘Awful sorry, you
know, hoys,’ said our Colonel, ‘ but so long as we
have to stay here, we can’t help having the fever,’
Yet the Colonel was wrong, as another regiment
camped near us almost wholly escaped. But their
commander fought the malaria with sanitation
and preventive medicine. That made the differ-
ence.’'
In January, 1892, the influenza was epidemic at
Stebbing, near Chelmsford. Among the persons
attacked were Mrs. Abram Thorogood, of White
House farm, her daughter Annie, and her sons
William and Ernest. They had terrible pains in
the head, sore muscles and joints, and were very
feverish. The whole four —mother and three
children—were confined to their beds.
In a letter on the subject Mr. Thorogood says :
“ My wife became quite delirious; she did not
know where she was, and could neither get in nor
out of bed. I gave Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup to all of them with excellent results ; the
fever soon left them, and shortly they were well
and strong as ever, and have since remained so.
I may mention that many neighbours and friends
had the same complaint as my wife and family,
but although the others had doctors and the best
attention and advice, none recovered so rapidly as
my people did. I thank God that I came to hear
of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, for although
we are a family of seven, living at home, during
the eight years I have kept it in the house we
have not needed a doctor, thus saving many
pounds in doctors’ bills and costly prescriptions.
“ The way I first used Seigel’s Syrup was this :
In the spring of 1883 I began to feel ill and out of
sorts. My tongue was dreadfully coated and a
thick phlegm covered my gums and teeth. After
eating I suffered from pain at the chest and
stomach. I had bad nights, and sweat so much
that in the morning my underclothing was soaked
with moisture. In the following August carbuncles
came on the back of my neck, on my nose, and on
my cIiggIc-Idoiig. Wh£it X suiFGrGd. I c&imot qgs-
cribe. I got so low and weak that I could barely
crawl about. The doctors did me no good. And
as for their physic I might as well have taken tea
or water. In pain and suffering I lingered on
until I heard, through a neighbour, of Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup and got a bottle from Mr.
W Linsell, grocer, of Stebbing. A few doses
relieved me, and soon the carbuncles disappeared,
and I was as well as ever. Yours truly, (Signed)
Abram Thorogood, White House Farm, Steb-
bing, by Chelmsford, August 19th, 1892.
In Mr. Thorogood’s own case the disease was
indigestion and dyspepsia, which poisoned his
blood and caused the carbuncles, which are
growths on the underlying layers of the skin,
After the Syrup had purified his blood they were
absorbed and expelled from the system. Ihe
effect of the remedy in the cases of the other
members of his family shows (what we have often
stated) viz., that influenza attacks those whose
blood is filled with the poisonous acids thrown into
the system by fermented food in the stomach. Mr.
Thorogood says his wife had been troubled with
indigestion and dyspepsia, which was no doubt
the case with the children also. Don t allow your
blood to become a breeding-ground of disease.
On the first signs of Indigestion take Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup. You know the prove-b
about the ounce of prevention.

Sydney Grundy wrote a furious letter to London D.T.
ftnent the hypocrisy of managers who made a virtue of
suppressing O.W.’s name whilst continuing to produce
his plays. “ If a man is not to get the credit of the good
things he has done, how ca i he logically be punished for
his misdeeds ?” Grundy reproving Grundy 1
Pro.: “Technically speaking, without being offensive
to anybody, it is miserable to see a comedian like Wilfred
Shine degraded to F. M. Clarkism. Ye stage-struck
amateurs, what are you working for in damnable Poverty
Pointeration? Melb. is replete with Romeos and Juliets.
Yet Hamlet, Othello, Macbeth, and all that crowd, with
Ophelias and Lady Constances by the dozen, have to
whine for a few coppers from the glorious Gaieties.
“The troupe has a chorus of 50 vices.”— Misprint.
A well-known Melb. lady musical professional, left
destitute, with a large family, by her husband s death,
two years ago, prospers at Mildura, curing raisins.
Louis Bradfleld wishes it distinctly understood that he
has not, for any length of time, been understudy to
Roberts ; but he was to Fred Leslie. This is a very sore
point with Bradfleld.
An elderly gentleman in the stalls of a Melb. theatre
had on a close-fitting skull-cap, which a lady, who sat
behind, asked him to take off. “Oh, certainly,” he
replied, removing the cap and exposing an egg-bald head.
“ When I put this on,” he said, “I thought it could not
possibly obscure anyone’s view.” “Never mind, old
chap,” remarked a friend next him, “she would have
asked you to shave your head if it were necessary.”
“Hi! Hi!” was the song which provided material for
Cissy Loftus’s imitation of Letty Lind, at the time when
Cissy was a London rage. In due course Letty got level
with the saucy minx by singing the genuine “Hi! Hi! ”
and then giving her own imitation of the spurious article,
as an encore. She mimicked Cissy’s mimicry and showed
how much it had differed from the original Lind.
Henry Arthur Jones gave 50 guineas to a fund in aid of
the widow of Henry Herman, his “Silver King” collabo-
rateur. Jones and Herman parted company whilst their
drama was booming—each co-author claiming the larger
amount of credit for its success—but the flourishing sur-
vivor has not outlived all sense of indebtedness to his old
pard.
“ John-k-Dreams” (writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent) made a triumphant re-entry at London Hay-
market and went splendidly, with Mrs. Tree as Kate
Cloud vice “Mrs. Pat.” The house was full, and, of
course, Beerbohm Tree made a speech. Haddon Chambers
was all smiles.
Luscorabe Searelle has secured Hall 6 and wife (Norman-
Neruda) for a tour in South Africa. He paid £lOOO down
as caution-money, and Hall 6 and wife in return insured
their lives for the same amount in favor of Searelle, to
secure him against loss in the event of the death of either
of them. This is a new kind of security in theatrical
matters, though common enough in other business.
William Archer, the English dramatic critic, claims to
have read every play worth reading published in the
English language.
“ The Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith ” is another specimen of
dramatic anti-climax. For three acts Pinero is as brave as
you please—though he does truckle to the gallery when
Mrs. C. yells and grabs the Bible from the fire. In the
fourth act, as usual, the parson influence comes out on
top, and the lady, instead of walking off with her affinity,
goes away to pray for him. As the lady started as a
secularist leoturess, and has all along vented her disgust
at conventional morality, this strikes Mrs. Grundy as most
appropriate.
Re the nauseous tomfoolery of presenting Oscar Wilde’s
plays to the public with his name conspicuously absent
from the programme. A London paper to hand states
that Mr. Alexander resolved to continue the run of “The
Importance of Being Earnest,” rather than close the St.
James’s Theatre and throw his company out of work
(considerate man 1), but was careful to remove O.W. from
all announcements. The paragraph further mentions
that “ the name of the author of ‘ An Ideal Husband
has for some days been withdrawn from the bills at the
Haymarket.” From an adjacent par. in the same
Journal it seems that the run of “An Ideal
finished at the Hay market (because the lessee s
tenancy had expired) amidst general enthusiasm,
“ the curtain being raised no less than seven
times.” The public, in fact, were so fond of the
disgraceful person’s dramatic work that it was promptly
reproduced at another theatre, under the same condi-
tions as before. In all directions entrepreneur Podsnap,
whilst coining money by O.W.’s talent, was waving his
moral paws and declining to know that an O.W. existed
anywhere in the world. At the Lyceum Theatre, New
York, a male management followed the Hinglish example.
A lady, however, preferred to deal fairly with the
difficulty. Rose Coghlan was touring the Western Slates
as “A Woman of No Importance,” and she acknowledged
the author’s right to recognition by dropping his play
from her repertory.
The gods to the fore again 1 On last night of the
“Shop Girl,” at Melb. Princess’s, the gallery howled for
Louis Bradfleld to sing “ Some do it this Way.” Brad-
field came forward and said he was sorry he couldn t sing
it as the music had gone on to Adelaide ; whereupon a
hoy aloft called out, “I’ll whistle it for you 1 ’’
| JMay YohS has left London Avenue, writes The Bulle-
tin’s correspondent, and her part as Handy Dick Whitting-
ton is being taken by Millie Hylton. There seems a doubt
as to whether Lady May quarrelled with Greet, or lost her
voice ; anyhow, there has been a severance, much to the
prejudice of the theatre bookings, though Millie Hylton
is quite as clever as the duchess in posse. But there is
something very fetching about May, in spite of her em-
phatic language and funny ways. How she can objurgate.
No bargee is in it with her, for the ordinary vocabulary of
the River below bridge is, in her case, supplemented by
the latest West End swear-words.
Frances Saville has been singing with great success in
Paris in " Paul et Virginie.” She has fallen off a little
in looks of late, writes Tub Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, and has lost, I am sorry to say, at least a little
of her charming sprightliness. Her voice is brilliant and
her acting good, but she will never be a Melba. Apropos
Ada Orossley’s impending London debut, there is a
splendid opening for a contralto in England now, and if
Miss C. sings half as well as her friends say she can, she is
bound to be at the top of the tree ere long.
At Melb. Theatrical Carnival, Florence Morton, of the
Princess’ chorus, was offered by an elderly johnny who
had spent a lot of money—half a sov. for permission to
kiss her hand. The beauteous one promptly told him he
had fooled away quite enough money and had better go
home. But he persisted so far as to give the half-sov. for
an I O U allowing him to kiss the hand at an unspecified
future date. “Mostly fools.’’
Young Dorrington, of the B. and B. Co., ought to have
talent for the stage—son of the Kendals, and nephew of
Tom Robertson. His appearance and mannerisms are
reminiscent of his handsome father, so his brains prob-
ably come from the maternal side. Human beings, to a
considerable degree, are like watches. The parent chiefly
responsible for the case has least to do with the works.
Oscar Wilde might have said that, but his career was cut
short before it occurred to him. So it has been left to
the present writer.
Marshall Hall’s latest fad, the Melb. Conservatoire
sounded the knell of hope for many struggling loca
musicians. The promoters flatter themselves that, as its
terms are high, they cannot be accused of competing with
outsiders. As a matter of fact, though a single subject
at the Conservatoire is a little more expensive than else-
where, there are several branches included in a “course,
which may be taken up without extra cost. For example,
it is a better speculation to take up a “course ” embrac-
ing, perhaps, singing, violin and harmony, not to speak
of the honor and glory, than to pay Signor Screechi two
guineas less lor singing alone.
Brodribb-Irving’s tinsel title befits the professional
tinsel-wearer. The man who struts the stage a king may
as well strut a knight in private life. There is comfort
in the theory that Irving takes his “honor” merely as
another bogus jewel. It would be pity were it otherwise,
for Irving can honestly claim to have dignified the theatri-
cal profession of his country. In his chief capacity of
manager he ranks as the founder of modern scenic
magnificence and historical accuracy. His productions
have educated the public taste and compelled
rival managers to study those small details which
the old-school showman never gave a thought
to. Also, Irving has raised the standard of payment
for stage hands, supernumeries, and all connected
with theatre business. He is the Lord Bountiful of the
profesh, the leader of every worthy movement for its
individual or collective benefit, and a lavish distributor of
the cash which talent and fortunate circumstances have
brought to his treasury. Sassiety started to run after
Irving twenty years ago, or more. Other actors have
run after sassiety, but the “great Henry’s” worst
enemy wouldn’t accuse him of snobbishness. He was
willing to enter Mayfair mansions as a representative
friend of the drama, not otherwise, and when he entered
he took the drama with him. He didn’t leave it outside
on the mat.
The art of combining fanciful, charming
and pretty designs in brilliant jewellery with
prices which bring these novelties quite within the
reach of people of moderate means, is well under-
stood by Messrs. Hicks. Atkinson & Sons, late
Moubray, Rowan and Hicks, Collins-st., Mel-
bourne. With the .approach of the season they
have imported a number of new and attractive
ornaments for the hair, such as crescents, stars,
tiaras, and coronets; also, the new brilliant
buttons now so much in demand. These decidedly
new goods, though handsome and refined, are very
moderate in cost, and afford an interesting display
for inspection.
The Rev. Langham, a 37-years’ laborer in
the Eijian vineyard, writes exultiugly that in 25
years the Gospel has done away “ with cannibal-
ism, widow-strangling, polygamy, infanticide and
the burying alive of aged and worn-out relatives,”
but adds, with true parsonical fatheadedness;
“yet the natives are fast dying out, from some
cause or other, which no one seems to be able to
explain.” Hideous mockery ! The “ History of
of the Gospel in Fiji ” might be written thus :
Parson baptises nigger, nigger puts on tweed coat
(no pants), prays violently, sweats profusely,
coughs, dies, buried. Exit nigger. Parson writes
a book on Gospel triumphs. To a nigger, Clothes
spell Death.
lor coughs, colds you can’t beat Oolemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
There is the usual racial antipathy between
Hi Yah, from China, and the Australian abori-
ginal chief, Billy the Bull, who met him on a
bridge, the other day. Billy : “ Hoo-ki, yalla-
binjie ! ” Hi: “No savee.” Billy; “ Gibbit a
couple-a-ohillin.” Hi: “No havvee.” Billy:
“Gibbit sixpen.” Hi : “Me no catchee siccapen.”
Billy; “Well, dammit, gibbit pew matches.”
Hi; “Me no smokee, no cally matchee.” Billy
(getting truculent): “Wad are yer ? ” Hi : “Me
all-a-same Ing-glis-man.” Billy (seizing him) :
“Big peller lie thad one. (Shaking him). You’re
nod a blagpeller (second shake), you’re nod a wide
peller (third and extra-violent shake) ; yurra
plurry harf-cask ! ” And then our countryman
threw the yellow heathen into the river.
“ A Jew on Jews ” writes The Bulletin :
“ We, of all people, should be Anarchists,
Nihilists, Socialists, anything but Royalists.
Longinus says that love of money is the disease
which renders us most pitiful and grovelling; and
this love of money, is, perhaps, the main reason
that, wherever is royalty, or its representatives,
there will you find the Hebrew pushing his way
to the front to join in the grovel; whether it is
Baron Hirsch, juggling his glittering millions to
dazzle the eyes of European royalties, or the
Cohens, Raphaels, and Mosses of the colonies
rushing to Governors’ levees. When Glasgow
held his initial levies, in the Maoriland cities, a
perusal of the visitors’ lists would have led one
to imagine that at least 50 per cent, of the popu-
lation were Hebrews, whereas there are only
about 5000 Jews in the whole country. The
bitterest invective against democracy and demo-
cratic governments emanates from the Hebrews.
If Isaac A. Isaacs, democratic Solicitor-General
for Victoria, were to stand for East Melbourne,
where Jews most do congregate, against a pluto-
cratic representative of the white-vested interests,
the strongest opposition he would have to fight
against would come from the Hebrew electors.”
[All is forgiven. Heine was a Jew. —Ed. B.].

The late disclosures of the rottenness of
the N.Y. police system recall the fact that not
long ago the Argus urged that a mission should
be sent to New York for the purpose of getting
hints for the remodelment of the Vic. force.
A Melbourne paper apologises :
We are happy to state, after making the fullest inquiry,
that not only was our information of the most exaggerated
character, but that the actual facts were not true.
A newspaper’s “actual fact ” is often a lie-and-a-
half.
A howl comes from the West re the im-
perfect state of Bibles used for taking the oaths
in Never-Never courts of justice. How on earth
can a man take a valid oath on a Bible containing
perhaps only a half of Malachi, a quarter of
Exodus, and, mayhap, none of Jonah?
Dr. Faust’s Specific for coughs, colds and
kindred complaints wants to come under the
notice of people with barks and whoops, and
assures the public that it will give speedy relief
for the comparatively small sum of two-and-six.
The large, gross citizen who can t get
through his own front door unless his wife pulls
him by the waistcoat and his family push him
with several horsepower at the small of his back,
is requested by advertisement to notice that Dr.
Steinmeyer’s Pills, though guaranteed perfectly
harmless, will give him back his long-lost “ fig-
,ger” in a trice. The stout female who loads
down the omnibus is also implored to consider
the error of her weighs.
Bishop Webber (Q.) asserts that he would
be ready to beg again in England to-morrow,
arguing justification on the ground that most of
the profits of Queensland stations go to English
spenders who pay Australia neither Land-tax nor
Income-tax. The local Labor-party should be
grateful to the Bishop for keeping these ugly facts
under the public eye. But, on the other hand, is
it not presumption on his reverence’s part to say
that the money which the Q. Govt, culpably
neglects to collect should be raked into his own
pocket ?
When Maoriland Govfc. first proposed to
lend money cheaply on mortgage, the local Tory
press pointed out that the State would inevitably
lend too much, in order to gratify supporters and
placate hungry politicians, and there would be a
frightful loss when it came to realise on the
security, ;and the Loan Department would
burst up, and the Treasury would go bankrupt,
and the London money-market would be dis-
turbed, and capital would be withdrawn from the
country. Now it transpires that the State
demands a larger margin than do private bor-
rowers, and the Tory press states that this undue
conservatism will ruin the business, and urges the
department to launch out and “plunge.” It is
difficult to satisfy some people.
Dear Btjlleein, —Me that Q. fairy-story of
the snake crawling up the shunter’s arm. On
Frankston (Vic.) line, some years ago, guard Tom
Ward was holding the lever for the passenger-
train, and as it approached noticed the engine
letting off water. He called out, but couldn’t
make himself heard, and so stood and received the
full force of the boiling water and steam. As it
was night-time he was unnoticed at first, but on
being missed search was made and he was found
at the points, scalded to insensibility. _ It took
him months to recover, his clothes having to be
out off him.—W.S.W.

Dear Bulletin, —It has come. We have
educated the intruding alien. Some men camped
near Bourke, N.S.W., were surprised the other
day by an Afghan, who in tolerable English, en-
quired, “You men look for work?” Chorus of
“Yes.” “ I want a few post-holes sunk in my
yard,” continued the man of camels; “anyone
sink ’em, I give him rations.” Dead silence. But
at night two young fellows sneaked away, and re-
turned the following (Sunday) night with their
bags filled. They had sunk the post-holes.—6 xB.

One of the deadest and gonest of Mel-
bourne institutions is the annual Yorkshire
dinner, which passed away with the halcyon
period. It was a gathering of fine, big men of
Yorkshire birth or descent—railway Speight, ex-
Mayor Dodgshun, Horsfall, Parker, Dick Rowe
and Clough of the wool-trade, Vasey, the Gas Co.
potentate, and many another solid tyke. Good
digestion waited on good appetite at the Yorkshire
dinner. There were no flies on the grub, no heel-
taps in the glasses, and the company, which num-
bered about 150, had a larger aggregate heart than
any other Melbourne company on record. This
latter circumstance hastened the sad end of the
Yorkshire dinner, no doubt. One or two of the
chief supports gave way, a lot of the minor lights
were snuffed out, and over went the show. But,
though dead, it is not forgotten.
ST. ALBAN'S ESTATE (Geelong). View taken across verandah, showing entrance
to consercatory.
Photo. by Talma.
When Melbourne biz. was booming
And Gillies bossed the State,
Nor recked of trouble looming;
When Syme with Dicky Speight
Was still a warm “ chin-chinner ’*
And rents were awful dear,
A famous Yorkshire dinner
Was gobbled once a year.
Alas ! for “Yorkshire’s” dinner —
When fat times dwindle thinner
Such pleasures disappear.
No entrees, jellies, ices,
Or kickshaw trifles vain—
York ham, served up in slices
(Twas fried in dry champagne).
Roast-beef, with Yorkshire puddin’,
And heaps of heavy fare;
The cheese was proved a good ’un
By all the heroes there.
But, whilst they sat at dinner,
The saint upon the sinner
Was feasting everywhere.
Then banks and bankers busted
And high-time prices fell,
And saints, once blindly trusted,
Got tickets through to h —1.
To-day those burly diners
Have mostly tasted woe—
Their fairy golden shiners
Have melted like the snow.
And when some story-spinner
Refers to “ Yorkshire dinner ”
Their tears in silence flow.

Card. Moran subscribes to, and otherwise
supports, the John Robertson memorial move-
ment. “Old Jack” was an advocate of Home
Rule for Ireland all his life. Ho used to say that
the Home Rule he advocated when very young
was the scheme Isaac Butt afterwards adopted
and elaborated.

right. He has foolishly ad vocated the heaving of
petty baubles at literary men. One result of his
mihl agitation is, a p.b. for Besant, whose demo-
cratic sentiments are now made ridiculous by this
titular “distinction.” The alleged excuse for the
“ nobbling ’’ of Sir Walter is, doubtless, the unfor-
tunate gentleman’s philanthropy—a virtue which
woidd have preserved him from such a cruel late
had he not courted it.

Ed. Bulletin, —lie Card, Moran on the
fact that the churches of St. Phillip (in Sydney)
and St. John (in Parramatta) are indebted for
their names, not to the apostles, but to the
-Governors of the day. A still more gruesome
case is related of Toowoomba (Q.), where the church
was christened after an old local identity, usually
called “Pumpkin,” on account of the size and
shape of his head. It is called “St. James’s,”
that being the Christian name of Pumpkin afore-
said. (All flesh is grass.)—Veuax.

(whose initials, by the way, are L.F., and not J. 0., as
printed).
“I know,” says Mr. 0., “every inch of the
freehold property, my duties as secretary necessitating
two visits of inspection extending over 10 months. I
have had the opportunity of obtaining properly authen-
ticated particulars— viva voce—ot the immense amount of
gold taken from N.Z. Gully in the days of the first rush
from a number of ‘Old Q. Miners,’ who, unlike your cor-
respondent, remained in the district for 30 years, having
secured competencies the nucleus of which was found in
N.Z. Gully.
“ The matter stands briefly thus N.Z. Gully
and the reefs in the Boyne Range feeding it are the pro-
perties of two syndicates (who combine for purposes of
flotation in England).' The shareholders include honoured
residents of three provinces. For exactly two years
solid prospecting work has been done at considerable ex-
pense on the freehold containing the historical N.Z.
Gully. Nearly 100 shafts—in the ‘ gutter ’ and wide of it
—were put down ; and it has been demonstrated to the
satisfaction of the shareholders that the entire Gully
carries Sufficient payable gold to warrant sluicing on a
large scale.
“ Under the auspices of the Gladstone Chamber
of Commerce an inspection was recently made by three
old residents of the district miners of admitted
experience. They made the following sworn declara-
tion;^- 1 We are well-known old residents of this dis-
trict, and have no interest whatever in the above pro-
perty, eil her directly or indirectly, examining it solely
wjth the view of securing an independent report of
its value. ,We are of opinion that the entire Gully
systematically sluiced on a face will average at
least six pennyweights of gold to the load.’
This testimony is supported by a gentleman who has lived
for over to years in the Port Curtis district, and is now its
representative in Q. Parliament. Further support to the
of the reports is given by the president of the
Chrtmbyr of Commerce and Mayor of Gladstone. I, per-
sonally. firmly believe the property will, immediately
afWr the neo ssary machinery is erected, produce an
annual output of gold second to no mining property in
Queensland.
“ We are advised by competent hydraulic en-
gineers that two ‘ Monitor ’ nozzles will break down and
sluice over 2000 loads per diem. The gully—like all
auriferous alluvial deposits—is patchy, running from
many ounces to a few grains per load. The declared aver-
age is 6dwts. ; but, to be perfectly safe, the bulk yield is
out down by two-thirds. The cost of treatment as
furnished us is 4£d. per load ; but we have endeavoured
to be on the sure side by estimating the expenses at Is.
per load. We have an unlimited water-supply; and
allowing for wear and tear, and costs of two sets of pump-
ing plant, the weekly expense is covered by £220, treat-
ing, say, 10,000 loads. Three grains to the load would,
therefore, leave a profit. It is utter nonsense in these
days of gigantic mining undertakings, and particularly
with a property like the Central Queensland G.M. Pro-
prietary's—possessing a multitude of untried reefs and
millions of loads of payable gravel—to accept the experi-
ences of ‘ A i Old Q. Miner,’ who, 30 years ago, worked
without profit a piece of ground in N.Z. Gully 20 feet
square! It is Well-known that thousands of diggers were
unsuccessful. What ‘ rush ’ has there been but provided
victims? It is notorious that Mount Morgan, at the date
of ‘An Old Q. Miner’s ’ brief stay in the district, was
‘ napped ’ and tried by numberless fossickers ; but 20 x 20
feet of ironstone worked with pestle and mortar and a
dish had no attraction in those days, neither, for the
matter of that, would it prove very remunerative at the
present day,
“What qualifications has ‘An Old Q. Miner’
for parading his estimate of 2grs. to the load for river-
dredging, or what reason for his denunciation of the reefs
wherf it la taken into consideration that no dredge ever
worked on the River, nor were reefs opened out in his
day—or, indeed, for many years after? The reefs alto-
gether have merely had most primitive trials. Take the
Alexandra. It was literally ‘scooped’ out like a deep
ditch to a depth of 70 feet, and the stone roughly crushed,
Some 300 tons averaged 3oz. to the ton ; then mundic was
struck, and the crude gold-saving appliances were unable
to deal with it. I could give you assays and tests ex-
tending over the last two years; but really there is no
necessity for this. They merely concern the share-
holders. ‘An Old Q. Miner’commences his letter: ‘ You
ad vertise the prospectus,’ &c. Now, you do nothing of
kind. No prospectus has been advertised of the
Ge,ltral Queensland G.M. Proprietary-this being only
distributed privately to the immediate connections of the
ori e inal shareholders. All the shares (6144) are now
allot ted, and our reserve-fund for future developments is
ln hard cash > veBted in trustees. Your advertising
columns contained no invitation for the general public to
subscribe, but merely a special notice of interest
only to those who received the private prospectus.
Under all these circumstances the comments of

A Scrap.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They were three men all under 26, and they talked
over whisky and cigars—especially whisky. Said
one :
“ Bub why the devil did you let her slip, man ?
She was certainly handsome in a way, and nob so
much older than you, after all.”
“ And the beans 1” ejaculated another.
“ Yes, the beans. Ib wasn’t like you, Tom, to
let that 30 thou, go easily. Come, out with it.”
“Well,” said Tom, regretfully, “I admit I was
an ass. I made too sure after the engagement,
nd one day I was feeling blase, and I kissed her
the forehead.”
“ Well?”
“It’s this way,” continued Tom, with the air of
afphilosopher. “You can kiss an undoubtedly
young girl on the forehead —if you want to —or
you can kiss an admittedly elderly woman on the
forehead without offence. The act is paternal
or filial. Kitty was 32, and she never forgave
me.”

Michael Davitt on class representation in
the Commons : —“ Agricultural Laborers—Boo,ooo
—have only one representative. Landlord in-
terests have 130 representatives. 600,000 miners
possess seven members; colliery proprietors, 21
direct representatives of their class. 300,000
railway workers have no members at all; but 22
railway directors have seats. There is one re-
presentative of 220,000 British seamen and 25 for
ship-owners. In fact, 25,000,000 wage-owners have
only 15 of their class to speak for them in Parlia-
ment.
The Tantanoola tiger has now been seen
for the 98th time in S.A. by a man who “could
have shot the animal,” but his gun was not loaded,
and (also) he had forgotten to take it with him.
A comparison of the descriptions given on the
several occasions the tiger has been seen produces
wonderful results. The tiger is apparently a cow-
shaped beast, with blue, green, and yellow spots;
it has three different tails, respectively 10ft., 12ft.,
and 60ft. long; and one reliable citizen, who has
lived at Tantanoola for 30 years, and was once a
fisherman and detective, and can therefore be be-
lieved, says the tiger has three ears and two horns,
the latter “four times the size of an ordinary
cow’s,” and barks like a dog. Another citizen,
who was at the battle of Waterloo, says the
animal he saw had a head like a big dog and a tail
like a crocodile, and, when he saw it, was breath-
ing stertorously out of its eyes, but it is believed
that this was a mirage, arising from “local”
whisky. :
Premier Reid: “God made a plan of
human progress long before any white man came
to this continent. What was this vast ocean
between all countries for? It gave human beings
the facilities for carrying on an enormous com-
merce.” Which is reminiscent of the Scotch rail-
road engineer who said that the Lord made the
hills because otherwise there would be no places
to make tunnels through. Some people have
ventured the opinion that the ocean was intended
as a means of supplying rain, so that man might
have a shower-bath in the morning, but Reid
knows better. At the same time The Bulletin
always feels like throwing a lump of mud at the
little podgy individual who is on such intimate
terms with the Almighty that he knows the exact
reason for everything.
Maoriland papers complain that, away
back at a solitary place called Rangatira, near
Paraekaretu, the cemetery is unfenced, and the
pigs root out deep holes with a view to getting at
the coffins and gnawing the dead. Bacon must
be a solemn kind of food at Rangatira, away down
by Paraekaretu. Bacon, by the way, is a solemn
article of diet anywhere. Consider, for instance,
that awesome pig-story which dune from the
N.S.W. back-blocks a year or two ago. A hope-
lessly drunken bushman, so drunk that the resur-
rection wouldn’t have roused him, was sleeping in
the paddock behind a bush pub; and three pigs
ate —but the rest of that pig story is merely a
quantity of shudders. And when the pigs had
finished rooting the- remains of the slumbering
object over the place, they left it in the vicinity of
an ant-hill, and the ants , Anyhow, it was
found an hour later and removed to the hospital,
and even the nurse fled from that still-living object
in insane terror.
Re “ the violent and slanderous language
of professional demagogues,” it is remarked by
the respectable G. R. Sims that “Even Mr. John
Burns, a member of Parliament and a London
County Councillor, recently denounced our brave
soldiers as ‘ gilded popinjays.’ ” “ Brave soldiers ”
is a Tory gag all the world over, and law’n’orderites
who have it on the tips of their tongues are the
loudest scoffers at its Radical counter-phrase—
“ Honest working men.” Yet, one may more
reasonably assume the honesty of a hard-
working craftsman than the bravery of a
nursemaid-mashing soldier. Army-recruits don’t
take the shilling because they are naturally
valorous, any. more than second sons of lords
obtain officers’ commissions with a view of
getting shot, nor is a barracks’ life calculated to
develope special pluck. Brave miners, railway-
hands, cattle-yarders, sailors, steeple-jacks, &c.,
if you like—but not “brave soldiers.” Soldiers!
who have been ordered into smoky action half a
dozen times are less inured to danger than thou-
sands of working men who face a palpable risk of
death, in cold blood, six days a week all the year
round. Moreover, an enormous majority oLthe
world’s professional fighters get through amilitary
career without fighting at all. At the present
time there are very few privates in the French
army, and positively none in the German, who
have tasted “glory.” Leaving little wars with
colored races out of the question, there is hardly a
Frenchman, German or Britisher in the ranlfs
possessing any claim to the term “ brave soldier*”

Bowled Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I walked the verandah of my bungalow on Moto
Ko Buli plantation, Sarawaga river, island of
Vanua Levu, Fiji, impatiently waiting dinner.
It was longer than usual in coming, and more
than once I had shouted to Lonea, the cook, to
hurry up, receiving always the stereotyped
answer, “ To, saka, malua vakalailaie /” (“Yes,
sir, wait a little.”).
Suddenly, after a longer pause than usual,
Lonea pounced on to the verandah in a state of
wild excitement. His eyes flamed and his breath
came fast as he asked :
“ Did you eat any of your curry, sir ?”
“ Eat any of the curry ! How could I when
you haven’t given me a chance? But, see here,
my friend, if it isn’t on the table smartly, by the
Lord I’ll eat you ! ”
Utterly disregarding the terrible threat, Lonea
only rapped out, with rising anger, “Then it’s
some of those niggers” (using the last
epithet of contempt one “nig.” can hurl at
another).
“ What’s that? What have they been up to?”
“ Eaten your curry ! Come and see.”
Now this was a serious matter, and with an
energetic “The devil they have!” I followed
Lpnea to the vale ni kuro. There, sure enough,
stood the dish of curry that would in its perfect
state have adorned my table and progressed
gently through my digestive apparatus—but what
a wreck !
Half the contents of dismembered fowl had
disappeared, and in the rampart of rice by which
it had been surrounded there were the unmistake-
able marks of the thievish fingers which had made
free with the greater portion. My dinner was
spoiled.
“ Who did this ?” I asked, wrathfully.
“ I don’t know, sir; but one of those Ra
men, I think.”
“Were they about here?”
“Yes, sir.”
“Who?”
“Masid, Taniela, Warri, Tioni, Radovo and
Taviuni.”
“ Any in the kitchen ?”
“Not while I was here, sir.”
“ Well, you go to their bare and tell them all
to muster on the verandah at once, if they don’t
want me after them; and send me your chief,
Rogo.”
Tanna men are true as steel in a fight. They
will stand to you like white men. Rogo came
and quickly took my instructions, and I then
went to make my own preparations for dealing
with the culprit among the Ra men ; for this
offence, combining theft, sacrilege and insult, had
to be promptly avenged.
The Ra men were not long in answering the
summons, and, having strapped on my revolver,
I lined them up against the rail.
All were there ; twenty bronze figures perfectly
nude but for the sulu around the loins.
Then Rogo and his ten Tanna men, fully armed,
quietly appeared and took up a position at a little
distance; so that when I began my harangue on
the enormity of the offence committed, and ex-
pressed my determination to discover and punish
the culprit, the Ra men felt I was in earnest.
I told them white men had means of detection
about which ignorant Ra Viti knew nothing, and
that I was bound to find out the thief. Of course,
all loudly protested innocence and denounced the
offender.
Then the stance commenced.
I first went down the line looking for curry
sign, making each one open his mouth. The draw
was not altogether a blank, but it afforded no
positive proof.
“ Now,” I said, “ I am going to show you how
a white man finds out a thief, and woe to him who
tells me lies.”
Then, commencing at the first man, I clapped
my hand on his bare chest, over his heart, and
sharply asked, “Did you eat my curry?”
“ Segai, saka, au bubului ki na Ka/ou dina an
sega ni kunia.” (No, sir ; I swear by the true God
I didn’t.”)
“Did you? Did you? Did you?” and the
answer was repeated all down the line, while the
heart-beat under my hand was but normal.
Then I came to Taviuni (so-called because he
had long lived on the island of that name). “Did
you ?”
“ No, I swear— ” but the thump, thump, thump
of his heart under my hand told a different story,
changing suspicion into certainty, and he turned
the yellowy-white color of dirty fat as I thun-
dered out —“You lie! Here, Lonea, bring me
that bilo.”
Lonea brought the cup in which I had prepared
a gentle dose of ipecacuanha out of the medicine-
chest,
“ Now, drink this !”
At first, Taviuni refused, believing he was to be
poisoned outright; but, by tasting it myself and
showing him the revolver, I at last induced him
to reconsider his decision.
He drank, and we all waited results. Presently
he turned to go.
“ Stop there 1”
“ Au tauveimate, saka ” (‘ I’m unwell, sir ”).
“ All right—stop there.”
“ Au via lua, sakal"
“ All right. Lua where you are !” And—ye
gods—he did 1”
There were one or two premonitory throes ;
then a mighty convulsion of the whole man ; and
—well, everybody soon knew who stole the curry.
The man’s discomfiture was his sufficient punish-
ment ; but the story flow far and wide, and I
became speedily famous as a mighty medicine-
man on the strength of the way in which I bowled
out Master Taviuni.

Another murder at Mackay. This time,

Recently a father and son named Raeside
were living in a tent near Southern Cross (W. A.)
with an intimate friend, George Davidson. One
day the others coming home found young Raeside
shot dead, evidently by accident. The father,
almost immediately afterwards, having gone to
the coast on business, was drowned while bath-
ing. During the last week in May the only
remaining occupant of the tent, Davidson, com-
mitted suicide. The loneliness and the ghastly
memories of the place were too much for him. He
was a bachelor, and well off.
A a recent Q. fire-inquest it came out that
the local Anglican parson acted in three capaci-
ties — viz., clergyman, local dentist, and agent for
a British fire-office.

According to a doctor, at Moorecliffe
(Sydney) Eye Hospital, it has sometimes been
necessary to keep watch at night in order to pre-
vent child-patients being bitten by rats —so
numerous have they become in the dilapidated
building. This is the other side of Sydney hos-
pital management, which knows no mean between
a freestone palace and a vermin-haunted hovel.
Alleged extract from a recent speech
delivered in London by Cecil Rhodes :
The only chance for a colony is to stop these {viz.. Pro-
tectionist) ideas before they are created, and I thought it
a wise thing to put into the constitution that the tariff
should not exceed the present Cape tariff, which is a
revenue and not a protective tariff. (Hear, hear.) The
proof of that is that we have not a single factory in Cape
Colony.
A sweet conundrum of inconsistency is
human nature ! When the first death-warrant
was presented to Nero, that amiable youth ex-
claimed, “Would to Heaven I could not write ! ”
A young French Judge resigned rather than sen-
tence a man to death. His name was Maximilien
Robespierre ! When Lord Melbourne took the
first death-warrant to Queen Victoria, she shed
tears until reminded of her prerogative of mercy,
and for over 50 years she has looked calmly on her
troops slaughtering savage races, fighting vainly
for their freedom in various parts of the “glorious
British Empire,” on which the sun never sets,
probably because that luminary believes the deeds
that would be done in the darkness would be far
worse even than those that refuse to bear the
light.
The Canadian Government, in order to
start an export trade in butter and get a firm hold
on the English market, offers to buy up all the
surplus creamery butter in the Dominion for this
season at lOd. per lb., and sort, pack and export it
at the risk of the State. Which, no doubt, comes
under the heading of coddling and grandmotherly
supervision, but then half the world’s big in-
dustries were started by just such coddling. The
Victorian butter-trade was created in that fashion;
and so, practically, was the growing S.A. wine-
export trade. Many of England’s manufactures
began through the Govt, importing foreign
artisans and giving them special privileges and
inducements. The immense French and German
beet-sugar industries were similarly coddled. -The
great improvement of late years in Austrian agri-
culture is mainly owing to a fatherly department
whose inspectors distribute new sgeds, new
manures and fatherly advice
peasantry, and hold classes for
and otherwise interfere generally. The choic -
generally lies between coddling a new industry, • i
else kicking it to make it hardy and independent,
and, after all, it is just possible that the kicking
process isn’t what it is cracked up to be.
Charity.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was Saturday night—late. We had spent the
evening in town, wandering from one haunt to
another —you know what that means. So, by this
time, we were in the very best of humors. We
halted unanimously, but quite impromptu, at a
corner, and stood discussing warmly all those
things about which we knew least.
While we were talking a man, whom we had
noticed leaning against the corner lamp-post, sud-
denly left his position, and made towards us.
Abruptly, without even a prefatory cough, he
made this statement:—
“ I’ve had a few to-night. Eighteenpence more
and I could get just like you coves.”
The words imparted a slur upon our sobriety
which was quite unwarranted. But, apart from
this, there was something rather taking about his
manner. He spoke a little anxiously, perhaps,
but without any trace of the whine of the profes-
sional mendicant, and had, on the whole, the air
of one who was simply giving information likely
to be received with interest.
Naturally, we hesitated a little, and, I am
afraid, even had the bad taste to allow our hesita-
tion to be seen. For he went on meditatively to
add this deduction by way of further inducement:
“ Sixpence a head is what it ’ud cost yous, if
yous ’ud do it. ’Tain’t much.”
Then, interested, of course, but never obtru-
sively so, he stood waiting.
Well, hang it all, you know sixpence is not
much ; and if it would really make him so happy
it would be well spent. So we collected and gave
him the eighteenpence.
He said : “ I’ll think of you all when I’m drink-
ing it.” Then, to show his absolute good faith, he,
turned straightway into the bar of the corner
hotel.

Hoolan is very rough on New Australia in
the Mundic Miner. According to John Plumper
the fundamental principle of New Australia is
that “ the last man collars the lob ” —a denouement
which he prophesies is not far off. “ The move-
ment originated with one man, and will end with
one man,” is John’s final prediction.
A Victorian youth, the other day, went off
his head with religious mania, and decided to
offer up the old man as a burnt sacrifice. Accord-
ingly, he built an altar in a paddock, and got the
fuel ready with which to frizzle his progenitor, and
then he went into the house with a wild expression
in his eye, and invited the father out for a walk.
As he wouldn’t come, the young man proceeded to
mash him with a crowbar, and also made a great
effort to shove him into the kitchen grate, bub the
fond parent got him down after a breezy fight, and
secured him with a clothes-line. This is another
tragedy which the Bible-in-schools party can’t
well put down to a “godless education.”
